He wiped the card table with a napkin from the stack by the microwave — not because he expected anyone to dust for evidence, but because he was an accountant and accountants closed their books at the end of the day. He pushed the chairs in. He stood alone in the dark TV room and listened: Devon's podcast droning its murderous details, the ventilation rattling in the walls, Burt's wheelchair receding down the hall, and beneath everything the deep institutional quiet of a building where most of the people inside it were dying at various speeds and the rest were being paid not enough to notice.

He sat down in his wheelchair. The adjustment was physical and deliberate — a loosening of his spine, a drop of his shoulders, a slackening around his eyes and jaw that he'd practiced in front of the bathroom mirror until he could hold it for hours. He let his hands go soft on the armrests. He let his mouth hang slightly open, the lower lip loose, the expression of a man who was looking at something that wasn't in the room. He became Harold Zielinski, Room 114A, dementia patient, the one who asked about his dead wife Eleanor.

He wheeled himself into the hallway. The fluorescents hummed. Room 110 was still moaning. Devon was still unconscious. The building was exactly as he'd left it two hours ago, and no one awake in it knew that five of its residents had just conducted a business meeting that moved thirty-eight hundred dollars through a distribution network that none of them could have imagined existing in a place that smelled like this and looked like this and paid its night staff fourteen-fifty an hour to sleep through it.

Harry stopped at his door — 114A, the single room, a privilege earned by seniority and the administrative assumption that he needed quiet for his condition. He wheeled in. He transferred to the bed, the movement efficient even though no one was watching and the performance was for empty walls. He pulled the thin blanket to his chest. The window unit rattled. The room was warm and close, the air thick with late August and the particular staleness of a space one person lived in and never left.

He thought about Carmen Reyes. Not with alarm and not with strategy, not yet — just with the focused attention he'd always given to a number that didn't fit the pattern. She'd caught the count. She'd walked the halls. She'd looked into a room and seen three people who weren't supposed to be awake, let alone talking. None of it was proof of anything, but all of it pointed in the same direction, and Harry had spent four decades reading directional data. She was accumulating evidence the way he accumulated inventory — steadily, because she was steady, and the steady ones always arrived at the answer if you gave them enough time.

He'd told the room he would handle her. He hadn't said how because he didn't know yet. The options sat in his mind like columns in a ledger: co-opt, confuse, discredit, wait. The first was the most dangerous and the most interesting. The second was temporary. The third was something he'd rather not do. The fourth was what he'd choose until the data told him otherwise.

Outside his window, the parking lot was empty except for Devon's Civic and the overnight nurse's minivan. The streetlight at the lot's edge cast a yellow circle on the asphalt. Beyond it, Route 18 hummed with the sparse traffic of people driving somewhere at two in the morning — shift workers, insomniacs, people whose lives hadn't narrowed to a room and a hallway and a card table in a place that smelled like piss and Folgers and the slow, patient work of staying alive when the world had decided you were finished.

Harry closed his eyes. He'd handle it.

He handled everything.


# Chapter 4

The charting system at Lakeview was a hybrid — half-digital, half paper — that nobody had fully committed to in either direction. Carmen sat at the Wing A sub-station at 2:37 AM, entering vital signs from her 1 AM rounds into the computer while the paper backup waited on a clipboard beside her. The terminal was old enough that it hummed. The screen cast a blue-white wash over her hands and the laminate counter, which was peeling at the corner where someone had picked at it during hundreds of similar overnight hours.

She'd taken the double for the same reason she always did. The car insurance was due in nine days and she was $140 short. The overnight differential was $3.50 more per hour, which meant the sixteen extra hours bought her the gap plus gas money if she didn't eat out. She hadn't eaten since the turkey sandwich from the vending machine at 10 PM, and she could feel it — not hunger exactly, but a flatness in her concentration, a lag between noticing something on the screen and understanding what it said.

The building made its night sounds around her. The HVAC cycled on with a low shudder she felt in the counter surface before she heard it. Room 110 — Mrs. Okafor, ninety-one, end-stage COPD — produced the same thin, rhythmic moan she'd been producing for the seven months Carmen had worked this wing, a sound Carmen had stopped hearing the way you stop hearing a refrigerator. A toilet flushed somewhere in B Wing, probably Devon using the staff bathroom, which meant he was at least awake, which was an improvement over last Tuesday when she'd found him face-down on his folded arms at the nurse's station with a podcast about cryptocurrency playing through one earbud.

Carmen finished the vitals entry for Room 112 — Lola Fernandez, blood pressure 128 over 76, pulse 72, temp 97.8 — and tabbed to the next field. Her eyes were dry. The building's air was always dry, but at night the dryness concentrated, and by 3 AM every shift her contact lenses felt like they'd been sitting on a dashboard in July.

She'd been thinking about the pill count. Not constantly, not the way a person worries at a problem, but the way a splinter works — you forget about it and then you move your hand wrong and there it is. Three oxycodone, five alprazolam. The charge nurse's dismissal. Lola's mouth opening for the pill check with the smoothness of someone who'd practiced the motion. The voices in the TV room — four, five people talking in complete, coherent sentences at one in the morning, and then silence and slack faces the moment she appeared in the doorway.

She couldn't make the pieces add up to anything she was ready to name. The count was wrong, and the patients were wrong, and those two facts pointed in a direction she didn't want to follow because following it meant paperwork and phone calls and an investigation that would land on her shifts, her counts, her license number on the dispensing log.

She clicked save on the vitals screen and stretched her neck. The sub-station sat at the elbow of Wing A's L-shape, which meant she could see down the short leg of the hall — six rooms, all doors closed except 118, whose occupant, Mr. Petrelli, insisted on sleeping with his door open because he'd been a fireman and believed in egress — but the long leg, where the sicker residents and the singles were, angled out of sight past the linen closet.

Movement registered in her peripheral vision before she consciously looked. A shape in the long hallway, just past the angle.

She turned her head.

Harry Zielinski was walking.

Not the shuffle she'd guided through a hundred med passes, the halting, uncertain progress of a man whose legs didn't quite know where to go and whose hands groped for walls and chair rails. Not the arthritic, listing gait she'd documented in his mobility assessment, which she'd done herself three months ago and which rated him as requiring assistance for ambulation.

He was walking. Upright. His spine was straight, or as straight as eighty-four years would allow — a natural curve, not a hunch. His feet moved with the deliberate spacing of a man who trusted his knees, each step placed and lifted, heel to ball. He was carrying something in his right hand, wrapped in a white hand towel from the bathroom, held close to his thigh the way you carry something you don't want noticed.

He was thirty feet away. The hallway was at half-light — every other fluorescent panel was on, the facility's overnight energy-saving measure that turned the corridors into alternating bands of pale light and weak shadow. He moved through them steadily.

Carmen's hands were on the keyboard. She didn't move them. She watched him the way she watched a patient whose vitals were wrong — not with alarm yet, but with the full weight of her attention.

Harry reached the intersection where the long hallway met the short one, where the sub-station was, where she was sitting in the blue-white light of the monitor with her name badge clipped to scrubs she'd been wearing for twelve hours, and he looked up and saw her.

He stopped.

They looked at each other across the linoleum.

It lasted three seconds. In the first second, Harry's face was open — unguarded, the face of a man who'd been alone in a hallway doing what he needed to do, and the expression on it was ordinary irritation at being interrupted, the look you'd give someone who walked in while you were on the phone. In the second second, she saw him recognize what she was seeing and what it meant. His eyes, which she'd spent a year documenting as confused and intermittently oriented, were clear and sharp and reading her the way she'd been reading vitals all night — taking data, making calculations, reaching conclusions. In the third second, his jaw set, and something crossed his face that she would later decide was resignation.

Then he put his left hand on the wall. His weight shifted. His shoulders dropped forward into the familiar hunch, and his knees bent slightly, and his right foot turned inward, and she watched him rebuild the performance from the ground up, piece by piece, like an actor stepping back into costume — the posture first, then the grip loosening on the towel-wrapped object, then the face going slack, the eyes losing focus, the lower lip dropping.

"Eleanor?" he said. His voice was cracked and querulous, the voice she heard every shift. "Is that you? Eleanor, I was looking for — where did you — the bathroom. I was trying to find the bathroom."

Carmen stood up. Her badge swung against her chest and then settled.

"It's Carmen, Mr. Zielinski." Her voice came out the way it always did with residents — steady, clear, warm enough. "Let me help you back to bed."

She crossed the thirty feet between them. The linoleum was cold through her sneakers, the way it always was at night when the HVAC ran low. She could hear his breathing now — not the labored, confused breaths of a disoriented man, but normal breathing, even and controlled, held slightly shallow the way someone breathes when they're trying not to give anything away.

She took his arm. His forearm was thin — she could feel the bones through his skin and the sleeve of his pajama shirt — but underneath the thinness there was tension, muscle engaged and holding. This was not the arm of a man who needed her help standing. This was the arm of a man who was allowing her to hold it because the script required it.

They walked back down the long hallway together. His feet dragged now, scuffing the floor in the shuffle she'd seen a thousand times. He leaned into her, his weight practiced and precise — enough to seem like dependence, not enough to actually require her strength. She could feel the difference the way you could feel a patient faking a range-of-motion test. The muscles were doing the wrong thing. When someone genuinely couldn't support their weight, the lean was dead weight, gravity and collapse. Harry's lean was a performance — she was supporting tension, not mass.

The towel-wrapped object was pressed between his arm and his body. She didn't look at it directly.

"Here we go, Mr. Zielinski. Back to bed."

"Eleanor makes the bed," he said, mumbling. "She always makes the bed. I can't — the corners. She does the corners."

She walked him into Room 114A and guided him to the bed. He sat on the edge, and she lifted his legs — one at a time, the way you do, hand under the knee, supporting the calf — and swung them onto the mattress. His legs were lighter than they should have been for a man who'd just been walking unassisted, and she understood that this, too, was part of it: he was going limp the way a competent person goes limp, deliberately, with control over the limpness itself.

She pulled the blanket to his chest. The room smelled like him — the warm, stale scent of a body that didn't get outside enough, skin that didn't ventilate, clothes that sat in the same dresser for weeks. Under that, the sweet-sour note of medications processed through aging kidneys. Under that, the universal Lakeview baseline: floor cleaner, industrial soap, the faint ammonia ghost that never fully left any room where someone lived in a diaper.

"Good night, Mr. Zielinski."

He'd closed his eyes. His face was slack. The towel-wrapped object was tucked against his side under the blanket, and his hand rested on it, and she could see the knuckles, and the knuckles were white.

She stood in his doorway for a moment. The hallway was empty and half-lit and silent except for Mrs. Okafor's distant moaning and the HVAC cycling down to its lowest register, a sound like the building exhaling.

She looked at the man in the bed, and she thought about the night three shifts ago when she'd heard clear voices in the TV room, and she thought about the pill count, and she thought about the day he'd corrected her on the tramadol dosage — two-fifty, not two hundred, said with the quick certainty of someone who'd been keeping track — and every separate anomaly she'd been collecting clicked into a pattern so obvious she couldn't believe she'd ever considered the alternatives.

She went back to the sub-station and sat down. The vitals screen had gone to sleep. Her reflection stared at her from the dark monitor — tired eyes, hair escaping the ponytail, the particular gray look that twelve hours of fluorescent light put on brown skin.

She sat there for eleven minutes. She knew it was eleven minutes because when she looked at the computer clock it said 2:52, and when she stood up it said 3:03, and in between she didn't think about anything clearly, just sat with the knowledge that was still settling in her the way sediment drops through water — not fast, not clean, just the slow accumulation of something heavy reaching the bottom.

---

The medication room was off the central nurse's station, between the two wings. It was the size of a large closet — one door, no window, a locked cabinet bolted to the wall, a counter with a sink, and a plastic chair nobody sat in because there was barely room for it. The room smelled like rubbing alcohol and the fine, dry dust that came off pill bottles when you handled hundreds of them.

Carmen locked the door behind her, which was protocol for controlled substance counts. She pulled the keys from her hip — the medication key was the small brass one on the ring with seven others, and she could find it by touch at this point, the way you could find your house key in the dark.

She opened the cabinet. The controlled substances were on the top two shelves, each in its own labeled container, each with a count sheet clipped to the front. The count sheets were simple: date, shift, count, initials. Two signatures per shift — outgoing nurse and incoming nurse, verifying the same number. Carmen's initials were on most of the evening entries. The overnight nurse's initials — Maria or Dolores or whoever they'd pulled from the float pool that week — were on the rest.

She started with the oxycodone. Five milligram tablets, prescribed to four residents on Wing A and two on Wing B. She removed the container, opened it, and counted. She counted slowly, touching each tablet with the tip of a tongue depressor the way she'd been taught in pharmacology, pushing each pill from one side of the tray to the other as she counted it. Eighty-six tablets should have been in this container according to the last verified count plus pharmacy deliveries minus dispensed doses.

She counted sixty-seven. She counted again. Sixty-seven.

Nineteen short.

She wrote the number on the back of a count sheet and moved to the alprazolam. One milligram tablets, prescribed to three residents. Expected count: one hundred forty-four. Actual count: one hundred twenty-eight.

Sixteen short.

The tramadol was next. Fifty milligram capsules, four residents. Expected: eighty-eight. Actual: eighty.

Eight short.

She opened the hydrocodone container. Ten milligram tablets, two residents. Expected: thirty-six. Actual: thirty-two.

Four short.

She stood at the counter with the numbers on the back of the count sheet. Nineteen, sixteen, eight, four. Forty-seven pills.

The ventilation hummed above her. She could hear, faintly through the closed door, the distant rhythm of Mrs. Okafor and the building settling into its own weight the way old buildings did at night, a creak in the ceiling every few minutes that sounded like someone shifting in a chair.

Forty-seven pills across four controlled substances. Not concentrated in one medication, which would suggest a single patient hoarding or a single staff member with a preference. Spread across the formulary in a pattern that looked, to Carmen's eye, like distribution rather than personal use. Someone was taking a variety because someone had a market for a variety.

She checked the count sheets again. Her own initials appeared at the start of each evening shift, and at the end, the numbers matched — she'd counted correctly every time. Which meant the pills were leaving between her departure and the start of the next verified count. Overnight. When Devon slept through his shifts and the hallways belonged to whoever was awake in them.

She thought about Harry's walk. The posture, the stride, the clear eyes. She thought about the rolled towel pressed against his leg. The practiced ease of his return to performance — how quickly the lucid man dissolved into the confused one, how complete the transformation was, as if he'd been doing it long enough that the muscles knew the routine without instruction.

The tramadol correction from her first week surfaced. Two-fifty, not two hundred. He hadn't been confused. He'd been counting. He'd been keeping inventory, and the correction had slipped out the way Carmen herself might automatically flag a blood pressure that was ten points off from where she expected it — a reflex, professional, involuntary. He'd caught himself and gone back to asking about Eleanor, but the number had been exact, and exact numbers didn't come from confused minds.

Forty-seven pills. Dispensed under her license. Missing from her shifts.

She put the containers back in the cabinet. She locked it. She folded the count sheet and put it in her scrub pocket, then took it out, unfolded it, looked at the four numbers she'd written, and put it back.

She unlocked the medication room door and stepped into the nurse's station. Devon's phone was propped against the monitor now, the screen showing a man in a garage talking about engine rebuilds. Devon was tipped back in the office chair with his mouth slightly open, not quite asleep but not registering her either.

Carmen walked past him. She went to the supply closet, got a fresh set of vitals equipment she didn't need, and carried it back to the Wing A sub-station, where she sat down and set it on the counter beside her. She didn't open it.

She knew what forty-seven pills meant. She knew what the Controlled Substances Act said about diversion and what the New Jersey Board of Nursing said about a licensed practical nurse whose medication counts didn't reconcile and what a prosecutor would say about a pattern that extended across multiple substances over multiple shifts. She knew, with the specific clarity of someone who'd sat through the pharmacology module on diversion three times — once in school, once in orientation, once in the annual competency review — that the pills were documented under her name, dispensed under her license number, counted under her initials, and that the gap between what should be in that cabinet and what was in that cabinet was her problem regardless of who had actually moved them.

She sat at the sub-station until 4 AM, when she made her next scheduled rounds. She checked on every resident in Wing A. Most were sleeping. Mrs. Okafor was moaning. Mr. Petrelli was snoring through his open door with one foot hanging off the mattress, and she tucked it back under the sheet without waking him. In Room 112, Lola Fernandez was curled on her side with one hand under her cheek and the other wrapped around her gold crucifix, and she looked like someone's grandmother, which she was.

In Room 114A, Harry Zielinski was on his back with the blanket pulled to his chin and his eyes closed, and he looked like a frail old man dreaming about his dead wife, and Carmen stood in his doorway and watched his chest rise and fall and knew that he was awake and that he knew she was there and that neither of them was going to say a word about it.

She finished her rounds and went back to the sub-station. She had seven hours left on her shift. She charted, answered two call bells, helped Devon turn a patient at 5 AM, and drank the coffee she'd brought in a thermos that was now cold and tasted like the inside of the thermos. The folded count sheet stayed in her pocket. She touched it once, through the fabric, the way you touch a cut to check if it's still bleeding.

The hallway stayed empty. Dawn came slowly through the window at the end of Wing A, first gray, then pink, then the flat white light of a September morning that made the linoleum look like what it was.


# Chapter 5

Carmen waited until Devon left the station.

He did this every night around twelve-thirty — walked to the kitchen, ate whatever the dietary aide had left out, came back in twenty minutes smelling like butterscotch pudding. She'd never had a reason to track his schedule before. Tonight she had timed it on her watch, standing at the sub-station pretending to chart, marking the moment he pulled out his earbuds and shuffled toward the kitchen in his untied Nikes. Twelve thirty-one. She gave him two minutes' head start.

The hallway was dim at this hour, half the fluorescents off, the others buzzing at a frequency she could feel at the base of her skull. Wing A stretched ahead of her — a single corridor of closed doors and the faint ammonia smell that never fully left the linoleum, no matter how many times the overnight aide mopped. The AC had been struggling all week. Late August in central New Jersey and Lakeview Gardens was holding heat like a kiln, the building's cinder-block walls radiating warmth back into the hallways hours after dark. She could feel it through the soles of her sneakers.

She carried her clipboard. She didn't need it — she wasn't charting, wasn't rounding, wasn't doing any of the things a clipboard authorized her to do at twelve-thirty in the morning. But the clipboard was habit, the same way the badge clipped to her scrub top was habit. The tools of a nurse who belonged in this hallway at this hour. Cover.

She passed Room 110 and heard Mrs. Delacroix making her sounds — not words, not cries, just a low continuous murmur that rose and fell like breathing with a voice attached. Carmen had checked on her at eleven. She was fine, or she was the same, which was what fine meant in Room 110.

The TV room was at the far end of the wing, past the supply closet and the handicapped bathroom, the farthest common area from the nurse's station. Not visible from Devon's desk. That was the point — the ring's point, she understood now, the reason they met here and not in someone's room.

The door was open. The television was off. Harry sat in his wheelchair at the card table, his back to the hallway, a newspaper folded in his lap. The only light was the green-white glow of the emergency exit sign above the far door, casting the room in a color that made everything look submerged.

Carmen stepped inside and closed the door behind her. The latch clicked.

"Mr. Zielinski." She stayed standing. "I need to talk to you."

Harry didn't move. His head was tilted forward the way it always was when he was sleeping — chin to chest, mouth slack, the posture of a man who'd lost track of the room and the hour and possibly the year. His breathing was slow, wet, heavy. A thin shine of drool on his chin.

"Harry."

She used his first name. She had never done that before — not in thirteen months of med passes and evening check-ins and gentle redirections when he wandered toward the fire exit asking for Eleanor. He was Mr. Zielinski, the way all her residents were formal names, because the formality was part of the care and Carmen did not cut corners.

He held the performance for two more seconds. She counted — one one-thousand, two one-thousand — watching for the seam, the place where the act would start to peel. She'd seen him break character once, in the hallway, and it had been fast, a light switch. But that had been accidental, a caught moment. This was something else.

Harry lifted his head. His spine straightened — not dramatically, he was eighty-four and his back didn't snap to anything, but the slouch was gone, the way a folded thing unfolds when you pick it up and shake it out. His shoulders squared against the back of the wheelchair. His chin came up. And his eyes, which she'd watched drift and search and lose focus a thousand times during med pass, locked onto her face with a precision that was almost physically uncomfortable. Like a flashlight pointed directly at her when she'd been expecting candlelight.

He wiped the drool from his chin with the back of his hand. One clean motion. Not fumbling, not patting, not the tentative dabbing she'd watched him perform over pill cups and dinner trays. A man cleaning himself up because the costume was off and he didn't need the prop.

"Close the other door," he said.

His voice was different. Not just coherent — she'd heard him coherent, the tramadol correction, the hallway at 2:40 AM — but running on a completely different frequency. The confused Harry spoke in fragments, rising intonations, questions that dissolved before they finished. This Harry's voice was flat and level. Each sentence ended like a period looked: hard, complete, closed.

"It's closed," Carmen said.

"The other one." He nodded toward the far wall, where a second door led to the back hallway. She hadn't noticed it was cracked open — two inches, enough for sound to carry.

She crossed the room and pulled it shut. When she turned back, Harry had folded the newspaper and placed it on the card table. She could see the crossword from here. Completed, in ink, in handwriting that was small and square and precise — nothing like the shaky scrawl in his chart, the trembling Harold Zielinski who couldn't remember how to spell his own last name for the intake assessment.

He waited. He didn't ask why she was there. He sat in his wheelchair with his hands folded on the table, his faded blue cardigan pulled tight across his narrow chest, and he waited the way she imagined he'd waited across a desk from clients for forty years — the patient silence of a man who already knew the number and was letting you arrive at it yourself.

Carmen stayed standing. Her hand went to her badge and she caught it, pulled it back.

"I know what you're doing," she said.

"Okay."

"The medications. I ran a full manual count last night — you're forty-seven pills short across four controlled substances. Oxycodone, alprazolam, tramadol, and hydrocodone." She heard herself falling into med-pass language, the clinical vocabulary that made everything procedural and clean. She stopped. "You're stealing pills. You and the others. Agnes, Lola, Earl — I've seen the meetings, Harry. The TV room, after midnight. I've seen you walk. Not shuffle, not lean on the rails, not — walk. You're not impaired. You're not confused. You're running something."

Harry's expression didn't change. He didn't deny, didn't flinch, didn't offer the scrambling panic she'd half-expected. He looked at her the way she imagined an accountant looked at a balance sheet that was coming up short — with attention, not surprise.

"Sit down," he said.

"I don't want to sit down."

"Then stand." He pulled the vinyl chair nearest to him away from the table, angling it toward her. The metal legs scraped on the linoleum, a sound that in the daytime would have been nothing but at this hour cut through the quiet like something tearing. "But you're going to want to hear this sitting."

Carmen sat. She didn't decide to — her legs made the decision, or the heat made the decision, the TV room's heavy warmth pressing on her like a hand. The chair exhaled under her weight. Old vinyl, cracked along the arms, the cushion held together with facility-issue tape that had gone amber and stiff. She could feel the split upholstery through her scrubs.

"How long," she said.

"Eighteen months. Give or take."

"How many of you."

"Five. Plus distribution."

"Names."

"You know the names."

She did. She'd known since the hallway, since the TV room at 1 AM weeks ago when the animated voices had gone slack at the sight of her, since the count sheets that kept coming up short on her shifts and nobody else's. She knew. She was asking him to say it because saying it made it something she could document, respond to, process through the professional channels that were supposed to exist for moments like this.

"Why are you here, Carmen?"

"Because forty-seven pills are missing and they were dispensed under my license number."

"I know that. That's not what I asked."

He watched her. His pale eyes — she'd always thought of them as watery, unfocused, the eyes of a man looking for someone who wasn't in the room. They weren't. They were sharp and light and specific, the color of old ice.

"Why are you here. In this room. Talking to me."

Carmen pressed her palms flat on her thighs. The fabric of her scrubs was damp — the room's heat, or her own, she couldn't tell.

"Because you need to understand what you've done to me. Not to yourself. Not to whoever's buying. To me." She leaned forward. Her voice was getting quieter, which was what happened when she was truly angry — she didn't get louder, she got smaller, more exact, as if she could control the situation by controlling the volume. "Every pill that went missing came through my med pass. My name on the count sheet, my license number in the dispensing log. If the state board pulls my records — if the DEA looks at Lakeview's controlled substance tracking — I'm the one they see. Not you. You're an eighty-four-year-old man in a wheelchair. They'll put you in front of a judge and the judge will see a confused old man who doesn't know what day it is and they'll give you probation, they'll give you supervised care, they'll write it up as a facility failure. But me?" She stopped. Swallowed. Her hand found the badge again and this time she didn't pull it back — she turned the clip between her fingers, felt the sharp edge of the laminate. "I go to prison. I lose my license. I lose my job. I lose my son."

The room was quiet enough that she could hear the ventilation system laboring two hallways away. The AC compressor kicked on and off in cycles — she'd timed those too, during long overnight shifts with nothing to count but the intervals between mechanical sounds. Right now it was off, and the air in the TV room sat heavy and still.

Harry hadn't moved. He listened the way he waited — with patience that felt less like kindness and more like calculation, a man letting the numbers come in before he responded.

"You done?" he said.

"Don't talk to me like this is a board meeting."

Something shifted in his face. Not softness — Harry's real face didn't seem built for soft. Acknowledgment, maybe. The recognition that a number was real.

"Fair enough," he said. "You're scared. I hear that. But you need to hear me now, because the next thing you do depends on whether you understand what you're actually looking at."

"I'm looking at a federal crime."

"You're looking at five people." He counted them on his fingers, not for drama but the way he counted everything — precisely, by the unit. "Me. Agnes. Lola. Earl. Burt. Burt's eighty-eight. He's got actual dementia — not like me, actual. Agnes is seventy-six with stroke residuals. Lola has Parkinson's. Earl's an eighty-one-year-old veteran." He stopped there, the way he'd stopped before when he'd started to say something about Earl. Whatever it was, he kept it. "We live here. We don't leave."

"That doesn't change what you did."

"It changes what happens if you report it." He pressed his hands flat on the table. She could see his fingers clearly in the exit sign's light — long, thin, steady. She'd watched those same hands tremble over a medication cup, shake while reaching for a glass of water, fumble a spoon in the dining room. They weren't shaking now. "You call the police. The state. The DEA. Pick one. What happens."

"They investigate."

"They investigate. And then they charge. Possession with intent to distribute — Schedule II controlled substances, which means federal mandatory minimums. For five nursing home residents. One of whom can't tell you what year it is." He paused. "You've seen courtroom photos, Carmen. Five elderly people in a courtroom. Agnes weighs a hundred and ten pounds, she can't lift handcuffs. Lola's hands shake. Burt won't understand where he is. The optics are a prosecutor's nightmare and a headline writer's dream. Elderly Drug Ring Busted. Great story. Terrible case."

"So your plan is — what, look pathetic?"

"We don't need a plan for that. To the system, we ARE pathetic. That's the whole architecture." He shifted in the wheelchair. His left knee popped — she'd heard it before, always the left, and now she wondered how long he'd been performing the wince that went with it when actually it was just a knee. "But here's the part that matters to you. The facility gets investigated. Cliff Moorehead's running a nursing home where five residents operated a distribution network for a year and a half under his nose. Lakeview gets cited, potentially shut down. A hundred and twelve other residents — people who had nothing to do with this, people who are actually impaired, actually dependent — get relocated."

He let that settle.

"You know where they go," he said.

"Wherever has beds." Carmen heard herself say it flatly, without inflection, because she did know. She'd worked per diem at three other facilities before Lakeview. She knew what wherever-has-beds looked like — the places that smelled worse, where the call bells rang three times before anyone answered, where the food was Sysco institutional and the medication errors weren't careful accounting like this but genuine neglect.

"Wherever has beds," Harry repeated. "That's on you. If you report."

"That's not fair."

"No."

They sat with that. The institutional clock on the wall — round, white-faced, never accurate — read twelve-fifty-one. Carmen could hear, very faintly, Mrs. Delacroix's murmuring from down the hall. A continuous sound, almost restful if you didn't think about what it meant.

"What do you actually want?" Harry said.

She opened her mouth. She didn't know. That was the problem — she'd come in here with a fact, with forty-seven pills and a count sheet and a license number, and she'd expected the fact to tell her what to do. The fact didn't tell her anything. The fact just sat there, as specific and unhelpful as a number on a ledger.

"I want it to stop," she said.

"All of it. You want me to flush the stockpile, shut down the operation, and then — what. The count stabilizes. You go back to charting normally. Except." He held up a finger. "The discrepancies you've already documented. Those don't go away. Those are on file — your name, your shift, your signature. The forty-seven pills you're short right now? Already in the system. The three oxycodone from — what, six weeks ago? Five alprazolam the week after? You flagged those. You documented them. You moved on."

Carmen's stomach shifted. She'd been so focused forward — what else could go missing, what else could go wrong — that she hadn't looked at the trail behind her. The count sheets she'd already signed. The discrepancies she'd reported to the charge nurse and then accepted the charge nurse's dismissal of. Months of documentation that showed, at minimum, sustained failure to catch what was happening on her shifts under her license.

"Even if we stop tonight," Harry said, "you're exposed. Any audit, any investigation, any routine review of dispensing records — they see the pattern. Chronic shortages on your shifts. Documented and signed. That's not a charting error. That's diversion. And it happened under your name."

"So you're telling me I'm already screwed."

"I'm telling you that calling the police doesn't make you less screwed. It makes you more visible." He watched her. The exit sign's glow made his face look carved from something pale and cold, but his eyes were alive in a way that the face didn't match — working, calculating, reading her the way he'd read a spreadsheet, looking for the place where the numbers moved. "Give me a week."

"No."

"One week. Let me show you the whole picture. The buyers, their situations. Where the money goes. Who depends on it. Then you decide."

"I've already decided."

"No you haven't."

His voice was quiet. Not gentle — quiet the way a number on a final balance is quiet, because it doesn't need to be loud, because it's just the answer and the answer doesn't shout.

"You already decided, or you'd have called the police instead of coming to talk to me."

Carmen's hands stopped moving. She'd been turning the badge clip and now her fingers were still, holding the little rectangle of laminate with her photo and her name and her license number, the thing she'd spent two years and thirty-eight thousand dollars earning the right to clip to her chest.

He was right.

She had come here. Not to Cliff's office, not to the phone in the break room where the state board hotline poster hung, the one that said IF YOU SUSPECT MEDICATION DIVERSION, CALL, in bold red letters she'd read a hundred times without registering. She had come to the TV room. At midnight. To talk to an eighty-four-year-old man who was not what he pretended to be, and she had closed the door behind her, and she had sat down in the vinyl chair when he told her to. None of that was what a nurse who'd already decided to report would do. A nurse who'd already decided would have picked up the phone. Would have documented, called, followed the protocol. Would not be sitting in a warm dark room having a conversation that, she realized now, she could not tell anyone about without incriminating herself further.

"One week," she said.

It came out rough, forced through something in her chest that didn't want to let it pass.

"One week. You show me whatever you think I need to see. Then I decide. And nothing moves in that week — no pills sorted, nothing packaged, nothing goes out. The operation stops while I'm looking at it."

Harry considered this. She could see him running the math — not the moral math, the logistical kind, what a week's pause would do to inventory, to timing, to whoever was on the other end waiting for supply. His face didn't change while he calculated. It was like watching the outside of a machine.

"Okay," he said.

Carmen stood. Her scrubs peeled away from the chair's vinyl with a sound like tape pulling off skin. She picked up her clipboard from the floor where she'd dropped it — she hadn't noticed dropping it, didn't remember the moment, which bothered her because Carmen noticed things, that was the whole problem, she noticed too much.

"We had some trouble with the pharmacy a few years back," Harry said.

He said it the way you'd mention last year's weather — a fact, not a confession. Carmen had her hand on the door.

"The facility switched suppliers. Cost savings. Half our prescriptions got delayed or substituted. Agnes's pain medication got swapped for something that didn't work. I spent three weeks on Tylenol for a hip that needed oxycodone." He folded his hands on the newspaper. "That's not the whole story. But that's where it started."

"I didn't ask how it started."

"I know," Harry said. "But you will."

Carmen pulled the door open. The hallway's half-light poured in, fluorescent and thin, and for a second the TV room's green glow looked like something she was stepping out of — not a room but a condition, a place with different rules. She walked out without looking back.

---

She sat in her car in the parking lot with the engine off and the windows down.

August didn't let go at night. The air was the same weight at one-fifteen in the morning as it had been at noon — heavy, wet, sitting on every surface like an animal that had lain down and wouldn't move. She could smell the dumpster from her parking spot: the sweet-rot undertone that got worse in the heat, layered over the chemical tang of asphalt softening in its third month of summer. Crickets somewhere in the landscaping strip between the lot and the road, loud enough to be irritating, persistent enough to become background.

She replayed the conversation. Not all of it — her mind kept catching on specific moments, circling back to them the way a tongue goes to a chipped tooth. Harry's back straightening. His eyes sharpening. The drool wiped away like greasepaint after a performance. The crossword completed in ink.

The crossword. He sat in the TV room every evening during visiting hours and stared at the newspaper, and she'd thought — everyone thought — that he was looking at the pictures, or that the newsprint was comforting in some vague sensory way, or that he didn't know what a newspaper was anymore. He'd been doing the crossword. In ink. For eighteen months.

She thought about his voice. The real one. She'd spent thirteen months listening to a man ask for Eleanor in a voice that wandered and broke and couldn't hold a full sentence, and under it, the whole time, was this — this flat, precise, completely controlled instrument that counted things and ended conversations and said words like *margin* and *architecture* and *visible*. The voice she'd heard tonight was the voice of a man who had never been confused about anything in his life, or at least not about this.

Carmen turned her badge over in her fingers. The laminate was peeling at one corner — she'd been meaning to request a new one, but new badges cost eight dollars at the HR office and eight dollars was lunch for two days or the difference between hitting the electric bill and carrying it over. The photo was from two years ago. She'd been heavier then. Her hair was longer. She looked, in the photo, like a woman who still believed the badge meant something simple — competence, authority, the right to be in the building and do the work. It didn't mean that anymore. Or it did, but it also meant something else now, something she couldn't clip to her chest and point to.

Her phone buzzed. She'd forgotten she had it — she must have grabbed it from the medication room on the way out. Her mother: *You okay mija? Reggie had bad dream, he's asleep now. Don't worry. Call me tomorrow.*

The text was forty minutes old.

Carmen pressed her forehead against the steering wheel. The vinyl was warm and slightly gritty — the accumulated film of summer, pollen and road dust and whatever came off her hands after twelve-hour shifts. She thought about the forty-seven pills. She thought about her license number on every count sheet, her signature, the careful documentation she'd been trained to maintain because documentation was protection, because if you charted it you could point to it and say *I did my job, I followed the protocol*. Except now the documentation was a trail. The trail led to her. The protection had become the map.

She thought about Reggie having a bad dream and her mother getting up at midnight with her bad hip to sit on the edge of his bed and say *estoy aqui, mijo, estoy aqui* until he stopped crying. Because Carmen wasn't there. Because Carmen was in a parking lot outside a nursing home trying to figure out whether she was still a nurse or had become something else.

She had come to negotiate. The word arrived in her mind fully formed, unambiguous, and she couldn't push it back. She had walked into that room with terms she hadn't articulated even to herself — conditions, limits, a framework for some arrangement she couldn't have described if someone had asked her. She hadn't come to report. She hadn't come to threaten. She'd come to negotiate, and Harry had seen it before she had, because Harry counted things, and what he was counting tonight was her.

*You already decided, or you'd have called the police.*

She could still call. She could call right now. Her phone was in her hand, her mother's text still glowing on the screen. The state board hotline was a 1-800 number printed on a poster she'd walked past twice a shift for thirteen months. She could call and say: *my name is Carmen Reyes, I'm an LPN at Lakeview Gardens, and I need to report a medication diversion involving five residents.* She could say those words. They existed. They were available to her.

She put the phone face-down on the passenger seat.

One week. She'd given Harry one week, and she'd framed it as a concession — a measured period of evaluation, the kind of reasonable professional response a responsible nurse might offer before taking action. But she knew, sitting in the heat with the dumpster smell and the crickets and the grit of the steering wheel against her forehead, that the week wasn't a deadline. It was a door, and she was already through it.

She started the car. The AC coughed to life and pushed air that was warm, then tepid, then something slightly cooler than the world outside. She pulled out of the lot with the windows still down, the moving air not cool but at least moving. In her rearview mirror, Lakeview was a low shape against the sky, a few lit windows — the station where Devon would be settling back in with his earbuds, the night-lights in the corridor, the exit sign's glow in the TV room where she'd left a man who was already planning what to show her.

On the passenger seat, her phone sat face-down. Her mother's text unanswered. The steering wheel hummed under her hands. She drove.


# Chapter 6

Marcus Webb signed the visitor log at 2:14 PM on a Thursday, printing his name in the same blocky capitals he'd used every week for fourteen months. The girl at the front desk — new, maybe twenty-two, acrylic nails clicking on her phone — didn't look up. He wrote *Lola Fernandez* in the resident column and *nephew* in the relationship column and clipped the visitor badge to his shirt pocket, the laminated edge warm from the previous visitor's body heat.

He knew this building the way a surgeon knows a body he's opened before. The turn past the activities room where three women sat in wheelchairs watching a cooking show at full volume, the host shouting about risotto while one woman slept and another picked at the vinyl armrest with her thumbnail. The smell shifting as he passed the kitchen — today it was some kind of fish, the institutional version where the breading was thicker than the fillet. The hand sanitizer dispensers every fifteen feet, most of them empty or jammed. He'd reported the empty dispensers twice when he worked here. The second time was part of the complaint.

Lola's room was 112A, shared with Betty Ostrowski, who had severe vascular dementia and spent her days facing the window. Betty's daughter had taped photographs to the wall beside her bed — grandchildren, a dog, a house with a flag out front — but Betty looked through them the way she looked through everything, her eyes tracking something the rest of them couldn't see.

"Mijo!" Lola said when he came in, and her face did the thing it always did, this warm crumpling that made her look ten years younger. She was sitting in the vinyl recliner with a magazine in her lap, *People* from three months ago. Her left hand trembled against the armrest. Some days the tremor was barely there. Today it was visible from the doorway.

He bent down and kissed her cheek. She smelled like the coconut lotion Tammy helped her put on in the mornings, and underneath that, the warm close smell of someone who lived in a room that never fully aired out.

"You look skinny," she said. "You eating?"

"I'm eating fine."

"You look skinny. Sit, sit." She waved at Betty's chair, which Betty wasn't using because Betty was in the dining room, or the hallway, or wherever the aides had wheeled her for the afternoon. Marcus sat. The chair's cushion was split along one seam, foam poking through. He'd put in a maintenance request for that chair nine months before he was fired. It was the same chair.

"How's your week been?" he asked, because this was how it always started — fifteen minutes of visit, of being Lola's nephew who came on Thursdays, of the performance that was also not entirely a performance because she was, in the ways that mattered, the closest thing he had.

"Ay, you know," Lola said. "Same. The chicken on Tuesday was okay. Wednesday they gave us something they called a casserole, but I don't know what was in it. I asked Tammy and she said, 'Mrs. Fernandez, I wouldn't eat that if I was on death row.' Tammy's funny. She's — she's having a hard time, mijo. The husband."

"Ex-husband."

"Ex-husband, right. She doesn't say much but I can tell." Lola touched the gold crucifix at her throat, a gesture so automatic Marcus wasn't sure she knew she did it. "Anyway. I saved you some cookies."

She said this loud enough for the hallway. Marcus smiled.

The cookie tin was on her nightstand, between a framed photograph of Tomás and a plastic cup of water with a bendy straw. It was a blue Danish butter cookie tin, the kind you could buy at any dollar store, and Lola kept it stocked with actual cookies on top — pecan sandies from the commissary, three or four, enough to make the tin look right if anyone glanced inside.

Below the cookies, separated by a folded paper towel, were four ziplock bags. Agnes's handwriting on each bag in black Sharpie: the drug name, milligram, count, expiration date. Oxycodone 5mg x 24. Alprazolam 1mg x 30. Tramadol 50mg x 18. Metformin 500mg x 40. The bags were flat, pressed carefully, no air. Agnes packed them like she was sealing evidence, which, Marcus supposed, she was.

He picked up the tin with one hand, popped a pecan sandie in his mouth with the other. "Lola, these are good."

"The ones from the commissary aren't bad. Not like the ones I used to make. Tomás liked the ones with the — what's it called, the almond flour. I can't get almond flour here."

"I'll bring you some."

"You don't have to do that."

"I want to."

She looked at him with that expression she got sometimes, where the warmth turned into something sharper, something that saw him more clearly than he liked. "You're a good boy, Marcus."

He was forty-four years old and she called him a good boy and it still landed somewhere in his chest that he couldn't defend against. He put the tin in the canvas tote bag he'd brought — the bag also contained a paperback western he'd gotten for Earl, a sleeve of Ritz crackers Harry had requested, and a pair of reading glasses from the dollar store that Lola had asked for last week because her prescription pair had a broken arm.

"I gotta get going," he said. "But I'll call you tonight, okay?"

"Okay, mijo. Drive safe. It's hot out there."

It was. Early September and the parking lot asphalt radiated heat through the soles of his sneakers, the air thick and chemical-smelling from the strip of Route 1 traffic that ran past Lakeview's entrance. His car was a 2009 Honda Accord with 187,000 miles and a passenger window that didn't go all the way up, leaving a quarter-inch gap that let in exhaust fumes and highway noise. The AC worked on the first and third settings but not the second. He put it on third and sat for a moment with his hands on the wheel, the tote bag on the passenger seat.

He talked to himself in the car. He'd always done this — his mother used to say he came out of the womb in mid-conversation — but it had gotten worse since she died, or maybe not worse, just more noticeable because there was nobody else to talk to.

"Okay," he said. "Rosa first, then Derek, then Janine. Rosa's off at three, so she'll be there by three-thirty. Derek said he's coming straight from work." He checked the prepaid phone — a Nokia flip phone from 2019 that Harry had insisted on because it couldn't be hacked, which Marcus didn't think was true but didn't argue about because you didn't argue with Harry about operational details. One text from Derek: *running 15 late*. One from Rosa: a thumbs-up emoji, which was the only emoji Rosa used.

The strip mall was twenty minutes south on Route 1, past the used car lots with their flapping pennants and the storage facility where Marcus had kept his mother's furniture for three months before admitting he couldn't afford the unit and donating everything to Goodwill. A half-dead stretch of retail anchored by a Dollar General and a nail salon. The parking lot was big enough for the Kmart that used to be there and now sat empty, its sign removed, leaving a ghost outline on the facade. Marcus parked in the back corner, near the dumpsters, where two other cars were already waiting.

He knew these people. Not the way he'd known his patients at Lakeview — their blood pressure readings, their fall risk scores, their allergies — but in the way you knew anyone you saw twice a month in a parking lot and talked to for ten minutes about their lives while handing them medication the system had decided they couldn't afford.

Rosa Gutierrez got out of her car first. She was fifty-two and she moved like she was seventy, because that's what lupus did when you couldn't afford to manage it properly. She'd been on hydroxychloroquine for eight years — Plaquenil, the brand name, four hundred milligrams daily — and it had kept the joint inflammation and the fatigue and the kidney complications from getting worse. Then she'd lost her job at a dry cleaning plant in Perth Amboy when the plant closed, and with the job went the insurance, and without the insurance the hydroxychloroquine cost her $412 a month at CVS. Four hundred and twelve dollars. She'd done the math once for Marcus: her unemployment was $387 a week. The medication alone would eat more than a quarter of that.

She'd tried the manufacturer's patient assistance program — they rejected her because she had an ex-husband whose income technically disqualified her, even though she hadn't seen him in six years and he paid nothing. She'd tried the generic from a Canadian pharmacy, but the website she found was fraudulent and she'd lost two hundred dollars. Marcus charged her fifteen dollars a bottle. Agnes made sure the dosage and manufacturer were consistent each time.

"Hey, Rosa." He got out with her bag — a single ziplock, metformin and hydroxychloroquine, the hydroxychloroquine added from a separate stash Agnes maintained because it wasn't a controlled substance but it was expensive and Rosa needed it.

"Marcus." She took the bag and put it in her purse without looking inside. She'd checked the first few times. She didn't check anymore. "How's the — how's everyone?"

"Good. Everyone's good." He didn't tell her who supplied the pills or where they came from. She hadn't asked. There was an etiquette to this, a mutual agreement not to examine the thing too closely.

Rosa handed him an envelope. Sixty dollars — two months' worth, because she'd missed last time when her car broke down. She'd written *thank you* on the outside of the envelope in pencil, and Marcus looked at the words and then put the envelope in his back pocket and said, "You good on refills? You got enough for the month?"

"Yeah. The metformin, I can get some of that at Walmart for cheap, but the Plaquenil—" She stopped. She had a way of stopping mid-sentence when she was about to say something that embarrassed her about her own situation. "Yeah, I'm good."

"All right. You need anything else, you text me."

"I will. Thank you, Marcus."

She drove away in a Nissan Sentra with a dent in the rear quarter panel and a child's car seat in the back. Granddaughter, Marcus thought. Or maybe niece. He didn't ask.

Derek pulled in twelve minutes later, still in his work clothes — navy polo with a logo Marcus didn't recognize, khakis, steel-toed boots that didn't match the polo. He was thirty-four and type 1 diabetic and had been since he was eleven. His insulin was covered by the insurance he got through his warehouse job, but the metformin for his secondary glucose management wasn't, and his deductible was three thousand dollars a year that reset in January, which meant that for the first four months of every year he was paying full price for everything that wasn't insulin. He'd been coming to Marcus for five months.

"Yo, what's up." Derek leaned on Marcus's car. He was one of those guys who stood close when he talked, not aggressive, just close, like he'd grown up in a small house with a lot of people and had never recalibrated his sense of distance. "Hey, question. You got any — you know what, forget it."

"What?"

"Nah, it's — my wife's got migraines. Bad ones. Like three, four times a month. Her doctor gave her some shit that doesn't work and she can't go back for three weeks because the office is booked solid. She's been using my mom's leftover Imitrex but my mom's got like four pills left."

Marcus shook his head. "I don't have sumatriptan. Sorry, man. I can ask, but I can't promise anything."

"Yeah, no, I figured. Just thought I'd—" Derek waved it off. "It's all good."

Marcus handed him the ziplock. Metformin, forty pills, which would get him through most of the month. Derek handed him cash — thirty dollars — and they stood there for a minute in the heat, two men in a parking lot, the dumpsters baking in the sun.

"How's the job?" Marcus asked.

"It's a job. They cut our hours last month, so I'm doing some DoorDash on the side. My wife's pissed because I'm never home, but — you know." Derek looked at the ziplock in his hand. "Shit's fucked, man."

"Yeah."

"Like, I'm not even — I'm not trying to game anything. I just need my meds. That's it. That's the whole thing. I need my meds and I can't afford my meds and here we are in a parking lot."

"Here we are."

Derek got back in his car — a Ford F-150 that was too much truck for a guy whose hours had been cut, but Marcus understood that too, the financial decision you made when things were better that became the chain around your ankle when things got worse. Marcus had a CNA certification and a decade of elder care experience and he made twelve hundred a month selling medication to people who needed it out of a parking lot behind a dead Kmart.

Janine was last. She pulled in at 4:10, late, in a station wagon with a handicap placard hanging from the rearview. Janine Coates, sixty, retired from a packaging plant in Edison where she'd worked for twenty-three years. She'd injured her back on the line — L4-L5 disc herniation, confirmed by MRI — and the company's workers' comp had covered her treatment for two years, including the oxycodone prescription that let her sleep and function. Then the claim was settled, the comp ended, and her doctor — Dr. Kaplan, she always said his name like she was reading it from a wanted poster — got nervous about the DEA prescription monitoring program and tapered her off.

She'd tried everything. Physical therapy, which worked but cost her $40 a session with her Medicare supplement, three times a week. Gabapentin, which made her dizzy and didn't touch the pain. Epidural injections, which worked for three weeks and cost her $800 out-of-pocket per shot. Yoga, at the suggestion of a pain management doctor who spent nine minutes with her and billed $350 for the visit.

She was not an addict. Marcus had worked in a nursing home for seven years and he knew what addiction looked like — the urgency, the negotiation, the dose creep, the inability to talk about anything else. Janine wasn't that. Janine was a woman whose back hurt every day and who needed thirty milligrams of oxycodone to make the pain manageable enough to cook dinner and sleep and take her dog for a walk. That was the whole story, and the healthcare system had decided it was too dangerous a story to accommodate.

"Sorry I'm late," she said through her window. "Traffic on Route 1 was a mess."

"No worries." He handed her the ziplock — oxycodone 5mg, twenty-four tablets, which was exactly what Dr. Kaplan used to prescribe before his courage failed. She handed him an envelope. A hundred and twenty dollars. He didn't count it because Janine always had the exact amount and counting it in front of her would have been rude. She also had two other envelopes in the passenger seat, which were bills, which Marcus could see because the envelopes were windowed and one of them was red.

"You doing okay, Janine?"

"My dog needs his teeth cleaned. They want four hundred dollars. For a dog." She shook her head. "I told the vet, I said, I can't afford to get my own teeth cleaned."

"What kind of dog?"

"Beagle mix. He's thirteen. He's a good dog but his breath could knock out a horse." She almost smiled. "I'll see you in two weeks?"

"Two weeks."

She drove away. Marcus sat in his car and counted the money: sixty from Rosa, thirty from Derek, a hundred twenty from Janine. Two hundred and ten dollars. His cut of the larger operation — the portion Harry tracked with his accountant's precision — came to roughly twelve hundred a month, counting all the buyers, not just these three. The twelve hundred covered rent on his basement apartment in North Brunswick, a space he'd found on Craigslist that turned out to be exactly as bad as a nine-hundred-dollar-a-month Craigslist basement apartment in central Jersey would be: low ceilings that he hit with his knuckles when he stretched, a window at ground level that showed him feet and car tires, a bathroom with a shower that drained slow enough that he stood in two inches of water by the time he was done. The remaining three hundred covered gas, food, the phone bill, and whatever was left over, which was nothing.

He drove home. Past the strip mall, past the Wawa where he sometimes bought a hoagie for dinner, past the apartments on Livingston Avenue that were nicer than his. He'd been a CNA at Lakeview for seven years. Not a nurse, but the person who did the work nurses charted about — the lifting, the turning, the cleaning, the feeding, the human contact that kept people from going entirely to seed. He'd been good at it. Not performatively good, not award-ceremony good, but quietly good in the way that mattered: he knew which residents liked the lights on at night and which didn't, who needed their pillow at a certain angle for their GERD, who got agitated if you approached from the left side, who needed to be talked to and who needed to be left alone.

He'd filed the complaint in June 2024 because Wing A was running with one aide for twenty-two residents on the overnight shift, when the state minimum was one for every ten. Devon — the same Devon who now slept at the nurse's station every night — was covering both wings alone, which meant that if two residents needed help at the same time, one of them waited. And in a nursing home, waiting meant lying in your own piss, or lying on the floor after a fall, or lying in bed with your oxygen cannula twisted and your sat dropping. Marcus had found Mrs. Patterson on the floor of her bathroom at 4 AM, her hip already swelling, her call light tangled in the bedsheets where she couldn't reach it. She'd been there for an hour. That was the night he wrote the complaint.

He'd submitted it through the proper channels — Cliff Moorehead first, who'd thanked him for his concern and done nothing, and then to the state ombudsman's office when nothing changed. The state sent an investigator. The investigator found the staffing levels compliant because Lakeview had filed paperwork showing a second aide assigned to each shift, a woman named Marjorie Sims who was on the schedule but did not, as far as Marcus could tell, exist as a physical presence in the building between the hours of midnight and seven. When Marcus pointed this out, Cliff called him into the office and told him his "attitude" was "creating a hostile work environment" and that his "insubordination" — filing a state complaint without going through internal channels first, which Marcus had done, and Cliff knew he had done — was grounds for termination.

He'd been fired on a Tuesday. He'd cleaned out his locker, which contained a spare pair of scrub pants, a jar of Icy Hot, and a photograph of his mother that he'd taped to the inside of the door. He'd walked out past the residents he'd spent seven years caring for, past Harry's room where Harry sat in his wheelchair performing confusion, past Lola's room where Lola watched him go with her hand over her mouth, and he'd driven home to tell his mother he'd lost his job.

His mother was sick by then. Congestive heart failure, diagnosed eight months earlier, managing on a cocktail of medications that Marcus had to help her organize because the dosing schedule was a puzzle only a pharmacist should have to solve — lisinopril in the morning, furosemide twice daily, metoprolol at bedtime, potassium supplements because the furosemide depleted it, warfarin with biweekly blood draws to check the INR. She'd died in her apartment four months later. Not at a hospital, not at a hospice, not surrounded by the well-funded machinery of institutional death. She'd died in her bed, in the apartment above a dry cleaner on Hamilton Street, with Marcus next to her and the television on because she liked the noise and he couldn't stand the silence. He was holding her hand when the breathing changed and he knew what the breathing meant because he'd heard it before, in rooms at Lakeview, that wet irregular slowing that every CNA recognizes. He sat with her for an hour after, because he didn't know what else to do, and then he called 911.

Lola had called the next day. She'd somehow gotten his cell number — Marcus still didn't know how — and she'd said, "Mijo, I heard. I'm sorry." She was the only person from Lakeview who called. Not Cliff, not any of the nurses, not any of the other aides. Lola, from her room at the facility that had fired him, called to say she was sorry about his mother.

The ring had found him three months later. Harry had sent word through Lola: Marcus should visit. Marcus had been doing odd jobs — warehouse temp work, a landscaping crew for two weeks until his knees gave out, a night stocking job at Target that paid eleven-fifty an hour — and he'd gone to Lakeview on a Thursday during visiting hours and signed in as Lola's nephew and sat in Lola's room while Harry, who Marcus had spent years believing was a confused old man who missed his dead wife, laid out a business proposition with the calm, itemized precision of a man who'd spent forty years making numbers do what he wanted.

Marcus's apartment was dark when he got home. The basement meant the light was always wrong — too dim in the daytime, too harsh when he turned on the overheads at night. He had a couch from Goodwill, a television on a plastic crate, a kitchen that was a counter and a hot plate and a mini-fridge. His CNA certification hung on the wall in a frame his mother had bought him. She'd been proud of that certificate. She'd told everyone — her friends at church, the woman at the dry cleaner, the guy who ran the bodega — that her son was in healthcare.

He ate a bowl of cereal standing at the counter because he didn't have a kitchen table and the couch was too low to eat from without spilling. Then he sat on the couch and took out the prepaid phone and called Lola.

She picked up on the second ring. He could hear the television in her room — the news, or a game show, one of those sounds that was just Lakeview's ambient hum.

"Everything went good," he said. "Rosa, Derek, Janine. All three. No problems."

"Good, good." She was quiet for a moment, and he heard the slight rasp of her hand against the phone case. The tremor made it hard for her to hold the phone steady some days. "Agnes wants to know about the metformin count. She said you should have forty left for next time but she wants to be sure."

"Forty went to Rosa and Derek. I'll double-check but I'm pretty sure we're short. I might need fifty next cycle."

"I'll tell her. She'll do the math." Another pause. "Mijo, how's your mom?"

His throat closed. It did this every time, a physical thing, like someone had their thumb on his windpipe. Lola asked about his mother every week. She'd been asking for fourteen months. He'd been lying for fourteen months.

"She's good," he said. "She's got her — her heart stuff, you know, but she's doing okay. She's watching her shows."

"Tell her I said hi. Tell her she raised a good boy."

"I'll tell her."

He didn't know why he couldn't say it. He'd said the words plenty of times to other people — *my mother passed away*, the phrase people used, the clean clinical phrase that kept the conversation moving. But Lola asked with such specific tenderness, such genuine interest in a woman she'd never met, that the lie felt like the only thing Marcus could offer in return. If Lola knew his mother was dead, she'd grieve for him, and he couldn't watch that. He couldn't watch a seventy-nine-year-old woman in a nursing home grieve for his forty-four-year-old loss when she had her own dead husband and her distant children and her trembling hands and the performance she maintained every day just to have a reason to be alert. He lied to protect her from carrying his grief on top of her own, and he lied because saying it would make the Thursday visits something different — would make him the one being taken care of, when the whole point of coming was that he was useful.

"You eat dinner?" Lola asked.

"Yeah. I had — yeah, I ate."

"Cereal isn't dinner, Marcus."

He laughed, a small, involuntary sound that surprised him. "How'd you know?"

"Because I know you. Go make something real. There's rice, you can make rice. Everybody can make rice."

"I'll make rice."

"With something. Rice with something."

"Rice with something. I promise."

He could hear her smile through the phone. "Okay. Goodnight, mijo. Be safe."

"Goodnight, Lola."

He sat on the couch after she hung up. The apartment was quiet except for the boiler in the utility closet on the other side of the wall, which ticked and rumbled at irregular intervals like a mechanical heartbeat with an arrhythmia. Upstairs, his landlord's television murmured. The ceiling was low enough that he could hear the floorboards creak when the landlord's wife walked to the kitchen.

He did not make rice. He sat there with the prepaid phone in his hand and the envelope of cash on the cushion beside him and the CNA certificate on the wall and the low ceiling pressing down and the sound of someone else's life happening above his head, and he thought about Mrs. Patterson on the bathroom floor, and about his mother's hand going slack, and about Janine's red envelope and Rosa's penciled *thank you* and Derek standing in a parking lot saying *shit's fucked, man*, and about Lola's voice asking him to eat something real, and he didn't move for a long time.


# Chapter 7

Carmen got to the school twelve minutes early because she'd overestimated the lights on Route 27. She parked behind a silver Lexus with a stick-figure family decal on the rear window — two parents, three kids, a dog — and cut the engine to save gas.

Mid-September, but the heat hadn't broken all the way. It was looser now, the air starting to think about fall without committing. She cracked the window. Reggie's elementary school was a single-story brick rectangle behind chain-link, its entrance banner reading WELCOME BACK VIKINGS in bubble letters some PTA parent had hand-painted with more enthusiasm than skill. The crossing guard stood at the curb in an orange vest, talking to another parent about something that required hand gestures.

Her mother usually did pickup. But Gloria had a rheumatology follow-up in Edison — her hands again, the swelling that flared and eased on its own calendar — and wouldn't be back until five. Carmen had the day off. Her first in eleven days, if she counted the half-shift she'd swapped with Denise on Tuesday, which she counted, because she'd driven there and back and used the gas.

She checked her phone. No messages from Lakeview. No messages from anyone. She'd been doing this for five days now — picking up the phone, checking the screen, waiting for something she couldn't name — ever since the night she'd told Harry she wouldn't give him a week and then given him one anyway. Two days left.

She put the phone facedown on the passenger seat and watched a line of kids file out the side door. Whatever was happening at Lakeview wasn't happening right now. Right now she was a mother in a pickup line, and the only math that mattered was the grocery list folded in her bag and the $74 she'd budgeted for it.

Reggie came out at 2:47, carrying his backpack on one shoulder because both straps were apparently uncool even in second grade. He climbed into the back seat and buckled himself in without being asked. He'd started doing that sometime in September, and each time it happened she felt the quiet pride of a milestone she'd missed noticing when it first appeared.

"Mom. Did you know sea turtles can hold their breath for five hours?"

"I didn't know that." She pulled out of the line, checking the mirror. "Is that for your project?"

"Yeah. Five hours. That's like from school to bedtime."

"Almost."

"Can we get poster board? Ms. Aldridge says we need poster board for the presentation part."

"What color?"

He considered this with the seriousness it deserved. "Blue. Because the ocean."

"We'll see what they have."

She drove to the ShopRite on Route 18. Not the closer one on Easton Avenue, which had better produce but charged thirty cents more per pound on chicken, and she went through three pounds a week. The parking lot was half-full — mid-afternoon on a weekday, the good hour before the after-work rush hit. She got a cart with a stuck front wheel because she didn't feel like walking it back for another one, and Reggie walked beside her with his hand on the cart's edge, steering them toward the cereal aisle by habit.

"We're going around," she said. "Produce first."

"Can we get Cinnamon Toast Crunch?"

"We'll see."

They wouldn't. It was $4.79 a box and the store-brand version was $2.49 and tasted close enough that Reggie had stopped noticing after the first bowl. But she didn't say no yet because saying no required a reason, and sometimes he forgot to ask again by the time they reached that aisle.

She worked the list. Bananas, sixty-nine cents a pound — she took four. A bag of Gala apples, $3.99 for the three-pound bag. The Honeycrisps were $6.49 and she didn't look at them. Carrots, a head of broccoli, a bag of yellow onions. She picked up the cherry tomatoes at $3.49 a pint, held them for two seconds, put them back, and took a can of diced tomatoes for eighty-nine cents instead.

Reggie held the apples while she compared unit prices on chicken thighs. Bone-in skin-on was $1.89 a pound. Boneless skinless was $3.29. She did the math on yield — bone-in lost maybe twenty percent to waste, but the bones went into stock, which gave her soup base for two dinners, which dropped the per-meal cost below the boneless even before she factored in the labor of stripping the meat. She put two family packs of bone-in thighs in the cart. $7.12 total.

Rice, $3.49 for the five-pound bag — still half a bag at home, skip it this week. Dry black beans, $1.29. Eggs, $2.99 for the eighteen-count. She could never bring herself to buy the dozen at $2.49; the per-egg math was wrong and the per-egg math was the only math. Bread, $1.89, store brand. Peanut butter, $2.69 — she was loyal to this brand because Reggie liked it and some things she refused to substitute.

In the cereal aisle Reggie stood in front of the Cinnamon Toast Crunch and performed a silence Carmen recognized from Gloria. The wordless occupation of a position, patient and obvious.

"Next time," she said.

He picked up the store brand without argument. That was worse than complaining. It meant he'd learned the difference between what you wanted and what the budget allowed, and he was seven, and she hated that he already knew.

The poster board was in the school supply section, wedged between folders and glue sticks. Blue, $1.29. She put it in the cart and he held it flat across the top of the bags like he was transporting something fragile and important.

At checkout the total came to $68.43. Under budget by $5.57, and she'd come back Thursday for milk and whatever fruit was on manager's special. She paid with her debit card and watched the number leave her checking account in real time on her phone's banking app. $411.18 remaining. Rent was due in sixteen days.

She loaded the bags into the Civic's trunk while Reggie sat in the back with the poster board across his lap, tracing waves in the air with his finger.

---

At home — the apartment was on the second floor of a two-family house, on a street where the sidewalks buckled over tree roots — she unpacked groceries while Reggie spread his project across the kitchen table. He had a library book about sea turtles with a cracked spine, a printed worksheet from Ms. Aldridge with guiding questions, and six markers that were all running low because markers were always running low.

"Mom, loggerheads can weigh three hundred and fifty pounds."

"That's like three of me."

He looked at her, calculating. "Maybe two of you."

"I'm choosing to take that as a compliment."

She seasoned the chicken thighs with adobo and garlic powder and put the pan in the oven at 375. Not the broiler — the broiler ran uneven and she couldn't afford a burned batch. Rice on the stove. She washed the broccoli and cut it into pieces Reggie would eat, small enough to seem manageable, not so small she'd be standing at the counter with a knife for twenty minutes.

While the chicken cooked she sat with him and helped draw a sea turtle on the poster board. She wasn't good at drawing. The turtle came out looking like a worried football with flippers. Reggie studied it with generous gravity, said "That's OK, Mom," and carefully drew his own version next to hers. His was better in every way — rounder shell, actual flippers, an eye with expression in it. He labeled it LOGGERHEAD SEA TURTLE in careful block capitals. The second G was backward.

"They eat jellyfish," he said. "And they don't even get stung."

"How?"

"Their throat has, like —" He made a shape with his hands, fingers pointed inward. "Pointy things. Spines. Inside their whole throat. So the jellyfish can't sting when they swallow."

She watched his face while he explained this — the concentration, the pleasure of knowing something she didn't — and felt the thing she always felt when Reggie showed her who he was becoming.
