Not pride exactly. Something closer to fear. He was growing into someone who would need things she wasn't sure she could give him.

They ate at the kitchen table with the poster board moved carefully to the counter. The chicken was fine. The rice was fine. Reggie ate the broccoli without complaint, which meant he was too distracted to notice it, because he was telling her about leatherback sea turtles. They could dive deeper than submarines. "Well, some submarines." And plastic bags in the ocean looked exactly like jellyfish, and the turtles ate them by mistake and died, and there was a photo in his library book of a dead turtle cut open and its stomach was full of plastic.

"That's really sad," she said.

"Yeah." He ate a piece of chicken, chewing thoughtfully. "Ms. Aldridge says we can write to our senator about it."

"You should."

"Will they write back?"

"Probably someone in their office will. Like an assistant."

He considered this. "That's not the same."

"No," she said. "It's not."

She cleared the table. He took a bath — he was old enough now to do most of it himself, which was a relief she felt guilty about the way she felt guilty about every convenience that arrived from him needing her less. She checked behind his ears, the back of his neck. He smelled like the strawberry shampoo in the bottle shaped like a fish, which cost $1.80 more than the store brand and was the one expense she didn't negotiate because it was the only shampoo he didn't fight her on.

Homework: a reading comprehension worksheet and eight math problems involving subtraction with regrouping. She sat on his bed while he worked at his desk, his pencil pressed too hard against the paper the way it always had been, since pre-K. The lead broke on problem six. She sharpened it and handed it back. The school's occupational therapist had said he'd self-correct. The occupational therapist outside the school, the one who could actually work with him regularly, cost $150 an hour and wasn't covered by her insurance. So the pencil grip would self-correct, or it wouldn't, and either way she couldn't afford to find out.

He finished at eight-fifteen. She read to him — three chapters of a book about a dog who solved mysteries, borrowed from the library because books were free if you returned them on time. She was religious about returning them on time.

He fell asleep at twenty to nine. She stood in his doorway for a moment. His breathing had that particular open-mouthed child-sleep sound — deep and unguarded, with a small whistle on the exhale that the pediatrician said was just his adenoids being seven-year-old adenoids, nothing to worry about. His poster board leaned against the desk. The worried-football turtle and the better turtle, side by side. LOGGERHEAD SEA TURTLE. The backward G.

She pulled his door most of the way shut and went to the kitchen.

---

The dishes were done and the chicken was portioned into containers — two lunches, one dinner reheated over rice on Thursday — when she called Yolanda. Nine-twenty. Late enough that Reggie wouldn't wake, early enough that Yolanda would still be doing what she did every night: watching old ER episodes on her laptop with the sound low while her cat sat on the keyboard and she pretended this bothered her more than it did.

Yolanda picked up on the third ring. "Hold on." The sound of the cat being lifted and set down on something. "OK. What's up?"

"Nothing. Just calling."

"You sound off."

"I'm tired."

"You're always tired. This is a different kind."

Yolanda had been an ICU nurse at University Hospital in Newark for nine years. She read voices the way she read monitors — baseline deviations registered before the person talking knew they were deviating. She was also the kind of person who bought name-brand everything and then complained about money, which Carmen found both maddening and faintly admirable, a willingness to choose pleasure over math that she herself had never managed.

"I'm fine. How's work?"

"Garbage. We're short three nurses because two called out and one quit, and HR sent a temp who didn't know where the crash cart was. Nine years and I'm still showing people where the crash cart is."

"Rough."

"That's just Tuesday." A sigh that rattled through the phone's cheap speaker. "Oh — you hear about Dana? Dana Whitfield?"

Carmen didn't know a Dana Whitfield. "No."

"She was on Three South. Med-surg. Been here, like, four years, maybe five. Solid nurse. Not flashy, but she knew her stuff. She had a thing with controlled substances — a documentation issue."

Carmen's hand was resting on the counter. She moved it to the dish towel, folded the towel into thirds, set it on the edge of the sink.

"What kind of issue?"

"Pharmacy sent up the wrong count on a hydromorphone order. Actual pills didn't match what the pharmacy logged as dispensed. Two tablets off. She documented what she received, the pharmacy says their count was right, and now it's her word against theirs. Board pulled her license pending investigation."

"For two tablets."

"For two tablets." Yolanda's voice had gone flat, the way it did when she was past anger and into the duller terrain on the other side. "She swears it was a pharmacy error. Maybe it was. Doesn't matter. She can't work while the investigation's open. It's been — what did Keisha say — like eight months? No income for eight months."

"What's she doing?"

"Moved in with her sister somewhere. Bloomfield, I think. She had an apartment, couldn't keep it. She's got a kid — twelve, thirteen? She's doing DoorDash."

Carmen looked at the containers of chicken on the counter. Two lunches. Thursday dinner. Three-day cycle.

"That's —" she started, and didn't find the rest of it.

"I know. And the part that really gets me, even if they clear her? She's got eight months of gap on her résumé that she'll have to explain at every interview for the rest of her career. 'What were you doing from January to August?' 'Oh, I was under investigation for drug diversion, but they cleared me.' You know how that sounds."

Carmen knew how that sounded.

"Anyway." The cat returned — Carmen heard the small thump and Yolanda's automatic "Get off, Nelson." She'd named the cat after the hospital's parking garage because that's where she'd found him, two years old with a torn ear and a personality disorder. "How's Reggie?"

"Good. He's doing a project on sea turtles."

"Cute. He's such a weird little scientist."

"He's not weird."

"He's a little weird. In a great way. He told me dolphins sleep with one eye open and then stared at me to see if I'd try it. That's weird, Carmen."

The laugh was right there, the shape of it in her chest and moving upward, but something was sitting between her ribs and her throat and what came out was a flat "Yeah."

Yolanda paused. The pause was her reading the monitor.

"You sure you're all right?"

"I'm fine. Long stretch. I haven't had a day off in — yeah. I'm just tired."

"Come over Saturday. I'll make that rice thing. We'll drink wine and talk shit about men we don't even have."

"Maybe. Let me see how the schedule looks."

They talked for another ten minutes — Yolanda's upstairs neighbor who played reggaeton at 3 AM, a patient on the unit who'd coded and come back swinging at the respiratory therapist, Nelson's vet bill for a tooth extraction that cost $387 and which Yolanda described with the specific outrage of a person who'd spent that money on a cat's mouth without hesitating and then needed someone to be appalled on her behalf. Carmen listened and laughed at the right places and gave the right responses and didn't say any of the things she couldn't say.

After they hung up she sat at the kitchen table.

The apartment was quiet. The refrigerator hummed the way it did every forty minutes or so, a rising drone that sounded like a question the compressor kept asking itself. Through the shared wall she could hear the downstairs tenant's TV — a laugh track, muffled, the ghost of someone else's evening.

She opened the calculator on her phone.

$47,000 a year. Gross. After federal, state, Social Security, and Medicare, her take-home was $2,998 a month. She knew this number the way she knew Reggie's birthday and her blood type. It arrived on the first and the fifteenth, half and half, and it was gone by the time the next one came.

Student loans: $680. She'd consolidated three years ago and the monthly payment had actually gone up because the interest rate adjusted and she hadn't understood the fine print well enough to argue it. $38,000 remaining. She'd be forty-four when they were paid off if nothing went wrong, if she never missed a month, if the rate didn't adjust again.

Rent: $1,200. The landlord had raised it $75 in June. Carmen had said thank you because the alternative was a $1,400 apartment farther from Reggie's school, and the difference in gas money would have eaten the savings.

Car payment: $400. A 2019 Civic with 94,000 miles. The transmission had made a sound last month — a low grinding on the upshift between second and third — that she was choosing to interpret as normal.

Car insurance: $200.

$2,998 minus $2,480 left $518 a month. For everything else. Her phone, $68, because work required the apps and the scheduling portals. Gas — Lakeview and back, Reggie's school, her mother's house, the ShopRite that was farther but cheaper — $160 in a careful month. Food for two: $74 a week if she bought bone-in thighs and store-brand cereal and never once looked at the Honeycrisps.

The $518 covered those things and then it was done. What was left — Reggie's school supplies, the field trips with their $12 permission slips she couldn't send back unsigned, the shoes he outgrew every four months, the copays, the dentist twice a year at $180 because her insurance covered one cleaning and she wasn't going to skip his second — came from doubles. Fourteen in the last two months. Five hundred, sometimes eight hundred extra a check, before taxes. Her mother watched Reggie those nights for free, which was not free, because Gloria's time had value and the fact that her mother never mentioned this was a cost Carmen carried differently but carried all the same.

The doubles were the margin. The doubles were the entire gap between making it and not making it.

If she lost her license, the $47,000 disappeared. The doubles disappeared. She had nine years of nursing experience and an LPN credential, and if the board suspended her — not revoked, not convicted, just suspended pending investigation the way they'd suspended Dana Whitfield for two hydromorphone tablets — the nine years became a gap she'd have to explain at every interview for the rest of her working life. She could do retail. She could drive for DoorDash. She could move in with her mother, put Reggie in Gloria's second bedroom, and try to rebuild from the floor of a career that had taken her five years of night school and $38,000 in debt to build.

She closed the calculator. The screen went dark.

Reporting what she knew wouldn't make her safer. Even if they believed her — even if the board decided she'd been a victim of circumstance and not a participant, which was less true every day — the investigation alone would freeze her license. Months of the $518 going to zero while the $2,480 stayed exactly where it was. Months of $680 in loan payments with no income to make them from. The math didn't have an answer. It had a shape — a box with her and Reggie inside it, dimensioned to the dollar, no room to move in any direction.

She turned off the kitchen light and walked down the hall.

Reggie had rolled onto his side. One arm hung off the mattress, his hand open, fingers loosely curled the way they went when he was fully gone. The nightlight — a blue plastic turtle she'd found at a garage sale for fifty cents, which he'd carried around the house for two days before allowing it to be plugged in — cast the room in the color of shallow water. His breathing, the small whistle.

The poster board leaned against the desk. The worried-football turtle and the good turtle, side by side. LOGGERHEAD SEA TURTLE. The backward G.

She stood there. The feeling wasn't about right and wrong. It wasn't about the ring or Harry or the pills counted and miscounted under her license number. The feeling was arithmetic. Reggie's breathing and $518 a month and a woman named Dana Whitfield who was delivering strangers' food in her car because two tablets didn't match.

Carmen pulled the door most of the way closed.

She went back to the kitchen and stood with her hands on the edge of the counter, looking at the window above the sink. Her own face looked back at her from the dark glass — thirty-one, hair still up, the shadows under her eyes that had been there so long she'd stopped noticing them in bathroom mirrors but always noticed in reflections she didn't expect. The refrigerator asked its question and answered itself.

She didn't make a decision. She didn't need to make one tonight. She had two more days.

She turned off the light and went to bed.


# Chapter 8

Agnes Chen's roommate breathed in nine-second cycles. A wet click on the inhale, something obstructive lodged deep in her airway that no amount of suctioning resolved, then a pause, then a draining sound on the exhale that resembled water leaving a sink. The interval between breaths varied from seven to eleven seconds. Agnes had logged the data points in her head for two months before accepting that the variation was random, not pathological. A pharmacist's reflex: measure, correlate, intervene. But there was nothing to intervene on. Her roommate, Margaret Okonkwo, eighty-three, was five years into Alzheimer's and had not produced a recognizable word since Christmas two years ago. The breathing was the breathing. It meant only that Margaret's body was still running its autonomic functions while the person who had lived inside it was gone.

It was Sunday morning in October, the second Sunday, which meant Helen was coming.

Agnes lay facing the wall with her eyes open. She had been awake since five-fifteen. She was always awake by five-fifteen. Her body kept pharmacist's hours; she had opened the Walgreens in Edison, New Jersey, at six AM five days a week for twenty-seven of her forty-two years behind the counter, and the internal clock had not adjusted for retirement, stroke, or institutionalization. The suprachiasmatic nucleus, she knew, was largely immune to changes in circumstance. You could strip away everything else and the body would still wake at five-fifteen and expect you to count pills.

The Sunday morning aide was Shanice, early twenties, efficient, talked while she worked. She came in at seven-forty and changed Margaret's incontinence brief with competent hands, narrating as she moved: how's your morning, Mrs. O, let's get you cleaned up, there we go. Agnes gave Shanice credit for this. Most aides talked to the wall or to their phones. Shanice talked to Margaret as if Margaret were listening, which was a small kindness that cost nothing and meant more than Shanice probably understood.

To Agnes, without slowing down: "Morning, Mrs. Chen. Helen's coming today, right? You want me to do your hair?" She spoke at normal volume and speed, which meant she had either not read Agnes's chart recently or had read it and didn't adjust. Either way, Agnes preferred it to the alternative.

Agnes made no response. She had refined her performance over three years into something the staff could categorize and dismiss: silence, stillness, eyes that tracked nothing. The clinical notes read "nonverbal, oriented x0, no meaningful response to verbal or visual stimuli." She had seen the notes because a nurse's aide sometimes left the chart open on the bedside table during linen changes, and Agnes could read upside down. Forty-two years of checking prescription labels across a counter from the patient's side. The diagnosis was wrong. The classification was wrong. But it was consistent, and consistency was what kept the system from looking again.

Shanice left. Agnes sat up. The dizziness that sometimes accompanied position changes, a residual from the stroke, did not come today. She put on her glasses from the nightstand and walked to the bathroom. Four steps. Her left foot scuffed the linoleum faintly, a drag she could mostly control when she concentrated. She used the toilet, washed her hands, looked at herself in the mirror above the sink. Seventy-six. Short gray hair she cut herself with nail scissors when it grew past her ears, because the salon visits the facility offered required a level of performative helplessness she could not sustain for forty-five minutes. Wire-rimmed glasses she had worn since 1993, the frames replaced twice but the prescription stable for the last six years. She weighed a hundred and eight pounds. She had weighed a hundred and eight pounds for fifteen years. Her body, like her internal clock, did not accommodate circumstance.

She ate the breakfast tray in her room: powdered scrambled eggs, toast, a carton of chocolate Ensure she did not need but that the dietary order mandated. She ate slowly, mechanically, the way a nonverbal patient would eat. Margaret's tray went untouched until Shanice came back to spoon-feed her, holding a paper towel under Margaret's chin to catch what slid out.

Helen arrived at ten-fifteen.

Agnes heard her in the hallway first. Helen's voice carried, louder than necessary, narrating her arrival as she narrated everything: "Come on, come on, Grandma's room is right here. Lily, careful of the — Tyler, do not touch that." The grandchildren. Lily was thirteen now and had learned to walk through Lakeview with her eyes fixed on her phone, which was how she avoided the residents in their wheelchairs, the walkers parked along the walls, the warm sourness underneath the disinfectant that no one who worked here could smell anymore but that visitors flinched from. Tyler was ten and still young enough to stare.

Helen came through the door carrying a canvas tote bag and wearing the expression she wore at Lakeview: bright, effortful, the smile of a woman who was performing the act of coping. She was fifty-two, small like Agnes, with Agnes's jawline and her late father's height. She had on a quilted vest over a turtleneck and white sneakers that had never been worn for anything more strenuous than a grocery run. She kissed Agnes on the forehead. Agnes held still and permitted this. Resisting would break character.

"Hi, Mom. I brought those almond cookies from H Mart, the ones you like." Helen spoke slowly, clearly, with each word separated by a fraction more space than it needed. The volume was fifteen percent above conversational baseline. Agnes had measured this against the volume Helen used with the children, and the differential was consistent across visits: Helen spoke to her mother the way she spoke to Tyler when he was wearing headphones. "Lily, come say hi to Grandma. Tyler, I said come say hi."

Lily approached and said, "Hi, Grandma," with the compressed discomfort of a thirteen-year-old who did not know what to do with a grandmother who did not respond. She held her phone against her thigh with both hands. Tyler came to the foot of the bed and said, "Hi, Grandma," borrowing his mother's intonation, because he was ten and borrowed his emotional cues from the nearest adult.

Agnes let her eyes rest on each grandchild for a slow count of three, long enough to seem like involuntary recognition, and then returned her gaze to the wall. This was the performance that cost her the most. Not because the children's discomfort bothered her; children were uncomfortable around illness and age, and that was appropriate and even healthy. It cost her because Lily had Agnes's hands. The same long fingers, the same narrow nail beds, the same slight turn of the thumb that Agnes had noticed when Lily was seven and had confirmed at every visit since. Those were pharmacist's hands. Good for precise work.

Helen filled the silence. She was practiced at this. She set the cookies on the nightstand, unpacked a new cardigan she'd found on sale (lavender, soft, the kind of purchase that made Helen feel she was doing something without requiring her to examine what that something was), and narrated: Lily had made the school volleyball team, her serve was strong but her passing needed work; Tyler's teacher said he talked too much in class but his reading was advanced; the landscaping people had finally finished the backyard; Bill was traveling again, always traveling, she hardly saw him. Helen delivered her own life in the same bright, careful voice she used for all of it, the children's news and her own loneliness flattened to the same register, and Agnes listened to all of it and said nothing.

Helen visited every other Sunday. She brought cookies or a cardigan or a photograph of the children. She talked for forty minutes. She touched Agnes's hand before she left. And she went home believing she was a good daughter doing a hard thing, because the alternative was a thought that would require her to dismantle three years of decisions, and Helen did not dismantle. Helen built forward. She had always built forward. It was the quality Agnes had admired in her as a child and had not recognized as dangerous until it was applied to her.

The stroke had happened on a Tuesday in March, three years ago. Agnes was in her kitchen in Edison, the house she had owned for thirty-one years, the house she had paid off in 2014, when the left side of her body stopped cooperating. Her left hand released the coffee cup it was holding. The cup hit the counter, not the floor; she remembered this with the specificity of a woman whose brain had apparently decided to catalog acoustics while her motor cortex was hemorrhaging, because the sound of ceramic on laminate was different from ceramic on tile, and she had registered the difference before she registered that she was falling. She made it to the phone. She called 911. She tried to say her address and what came out was noise.

The stroke was ischemic, left middle cerebral artery. The aphasia, the word loss, was the most dramatic symptom. She could think in complete sentences. She could not produce them. The words were on the correct shelves in her mind, properly labeled, but the dispensing window had jammed shut. She could write, slowly, with her right hand. On the hospital whiteboard she wrote: "I am a pharmacist. I am cognitively intact. My speech will recover." She wrote this three times in three days. The neurologist, Dr. Fein, confirmed the prognosis: Broca's aphasia with high probability of significant recovery within six to twelve months.

Helen had driven down from Bridgewater, forty minutes away, and spent four days making decisions. Agnes was still in the hospital, still pushing words past the blockage in her speech, when Helen called a realtor. Agnes found out the house was listed when a speech therapist mentioned it during a session, casually, as though it were common knowledge: "Your daughter said you'd be moving to an assisted living situation, so we should probably focus on functional communication for that environment."

Agnes wrote on her whiteboard: "I did not agree to this."

The speech therapist looked uncomfortable and said Agnes should discuss it with her daughter.

Helen sold the house for three hundred and twelve thousand dollars. She moved Agnes's possessions into a storage unit on Route 1, the one next to the tire shop, $189 a month. She toured three facilities and chose Lakeview Gardens because it was near her own house and because the administrator, Cliff Moorehead, had used the phrase "person-centered care" during the tour. Helen signed the paperwork. Agnes arrived at Lakeview in a wheelchair she did not need, with a medical summary that described her as "recent CVA with significant cognitive-linguistic deficits, requires 24-hour supervised care."

Her speech began to recover within two months. By month four, she could produce short sentences with effort and concentration. By month six, she was fluent again, with occasional word-finding hesitations under pressure that most people would not notice.

She spent those six months fighting. She wrote three letters to the Long-Term Care Ombudsman, each more detailed than the last, describing her RPh credentials, her cognitive status, her NJ license number, her demand for independent neuropsychological assessment. The first letter received a form acknowledgment. The second prompted a phone call from a caseworker who said she would look into the situation, which produced nothing. The third was never answered.

She called Helen. Eleven times in one month. Helen cried on three of those calls and said, "Mom, the doctors said you need this level of care." Agnes said, "Which doctors?" Helen said, "The doctors at the hospital." Agnes said, "Call Dr. Fein. Ask him if I need to be institutionalized." Helen said she would call. She did not call. Agnes knew she did not call because at the next visit Helen said, "Dr. Fein agrees you're in the best place for your recovery," with the particular brightness that meant Helen was repeating something she had told herself until she believed it. Not lying. Something worse than lying: constructing a truth that required no action, and then living inside it.

Agnes demanded a cognitive assessment. She demanded it in writing, verbally, and through the ombudsman's office. The facility administered a Mini-Mental State Examination. Agnes scored 30 out of 30. A perfect score. The results were filed in her chart. Nothing changed. Her care plan was not updated. Her placement was not reviewed. The number sat in her chart like a footnote that contradicted the text above it, and no one at Lakeview Gardens read footnotes.

She stopped fighting in September of that first year. Not because she had surrendered but because she had finished the calculation: the system was not built to reverse itself. Placement, once achieved, was self-reinforcing. The documentation supported the placement, the placement justified the documentation, and the people within the system lacked either the authority or the interest to question a circuit that was already closed. Agnes was institutionalized because she had been institutionalized. The original cause, a temporary speech deficit from a recoverable stroke, had dissolved. The institutional fact remained, as durable and indifferent as the beige paint on the walls.

She began to perform.

The decision was clinical. If the system classified her as impaired, she would be impaired. Not because she accepted the classification but because fighting it consumed energy that produced no result, and Agnes had spent forty-two years directing her energy toward useful work. She would direct it elsewhere. The silence, the wall-staring, the nonresponse: these were not surrender. They were resource allocation.

Harry Zielinski approached her fourteen months ago. She had been at Lakeview for nearly two years by then. She had noticed him early. His confusion was too responsive to audience, too calibrated. Real confusion did not adjust its severity based on who was in the room. Harry's did. She watched him for weeks before he noticed her watching, and when he did, he came to her room one night when Devon, the overnight aide, was asleep at the station and stood in the doorway. He said, in a clear, controlled voice: "The tramadol they give Earl is fifty milligrams. Should it be?"

Agnes had not spoken aloud in four months. Her voice came out rough and dry. But the words were exact: "Depends on his renal function. At eighty-one, if his GFR is below sixty, fifty is the correct ceiling. If they prescribed a hundred, they didn't check his labs."

Harry looked at her for a long time. Then he said, "We should talk."

She joined because she could not stand watching medications mishandled. That was the reason she gave herself, and it was true, but it was not complete. The complete version was that she had spent forty-two years behind the same counter at the Walgreens on Oak Tree Road in Edison, opening the gate at six AM, closing it at six PM, standing on a black anti-fatigue mat she replaced every two years because the foam compressed and her lower back knew the difference. She had filled over a million prescriptions. She had caught fourteen dangerous interactions that would have caused serious harm or death. She had refused to fill a contraindicated benzodiazepine-opioid combination for a physician's wife, and the physician had filed a complaint with the Board of Pharmacy, and Agnes had spent eight months under review before the board found in her favor because she was right and the prescription was dangerous and she did not fill dangerous prescriptions regardless of whose name was on them. She had trained eleven pharmacy interns. She knew the NDC codes for every drug she had stocked in the last decade — knew them as reflexively as a home address.

Then the stroke took her speech, and Helen took everything else, and Agnes became a pharmacist with no pharmacy. A precision instrument locked in a drawer. The ring gave her a counter to stand behind again. The work was illegal. The work was also pharmaceutical, and Agnes was, before anything else, a pharmacist.

She kept three composition notebooks. The black-and-white marbled kind, $3.99 each from the Lakeview gift shop, shelved between the stuffed bears and the get-well cards with lilies on them. She had Harry buy them for her, and he did, without asking what they were for, because Harry understood the value of documentation even if he could not have said precisely what Agnes was documenting.

The first notebook was inventory. Current stock by drug name, NDC code, dosage strength, quantity on hand, expiration date. She updated it after every ring meeting. She tracked losses: pills miscounted, pills damaged, pills that Burt had swallowed by accident when his confusion sent him reaching for the wrong cup. She projected supply based on prescription refill schedules and flagged medications that would run low before the next cycle.

The second notebook was the buyer registry. Not names. Names were Marcus's responsibility. But medical profiles: a column for the buyer's condition, their other medications if known, contraindications and interaction risks, and what they purchased from the ring. Agnes cross-referenced every new sale against the buyer's existing profile. She had flagged a potential interaction between one buyer's hydroxychloroquine and an aluminum-based antacid and relayed the warning through Marcus. She did not know whether the buyer had listened. She had done what a pharmacist does.

The third notebook was transactions. Dates, quantities dispensed, amounts received. This was Harry's territory, the accounting, but Agnes kept her own parallel record because she did not trust anyone's numbers except her own. Harry's figures matched hers within acceptable rounding variance. This was the only professional compliment she had given him.

She kept the notebooks in the back of her closet, behind a stack of adult incontinence briefs that she did not need but that appeared on her care plan as a standard supply item. The aides restocked the briefs monthly and never reached behind them. The three notebooks, taken together, were the ring's entire operational intelligence: inventory control, quality assurance, financial record. They were also, Agnes understood with perfect clarity, the most damning evidence that could be produced in a courtroom. Every transaction dated. Every quantity logged in her handwriting, her precise pharmacist's script, with NDC codes that only a trained professional would think to include. If someone found these notebooks, they would constitute a written confession organized for easy prosecution.

She kept them because she was a pharmacist and pharmacists kept records. The alternative, operating without documentation, relying on memory and approximation, was more frightening to her than the legal risk. An undocumented pharmaceutical operation was an unsafe operation. She would sooner be caught than be careless.

The fentanyl question had been the only argument in which she raised her voice with Harry. Burt Kovacs, Room 116A, wore a 50-microgram fentanyl transdermal patch changed every seventy-two hours for chronic pain. Marcus had a buyer who wanted fentanyl. Harry had weighed the margin.

Agnes said no. She said it once. When Harry began to present his calculation, she interrupted him, which she did to no one except people who were about to make a pharmacological error, and explained: a fentanyl patch designed for a patient with established opioid tolerance delivered a dose that would kill an opioid-naive adult. The therapeutic index was narrow and the onset slow enough that a user would not recognize the respiratory depression until it had progressed past reversal. She had counseled patients on transdermal fentanyl safety for twenty years. She had read the overdose literature. She would not attach her judgment, her forty-two years of training, to a transaction that carried a calculable probability of killing someone.

Harry had looked at her. Then he said, "All right." That was eleven months ago. The patches stayed on Burt's skin where they belonged.

Helen left at eleven. She kissed Agnes's forehead, told the children to say goodbye, and walked out with the empty tote bag and the specific set of her shoulders that meant she was relieved. Lily said, "Bye, Grandma," already turning toward the door. Tyler waved.

The cookies sat on Agnes's nightstand. Almond cookies from the H Mart on Route 27. Lily had eaten two and Tyler had eaten four, peeling the wax paper wrappers off with sticky fingers. Agnes would eat the rest over the coming week, slowly, because they were good cookies and because Helen, despite everything, still remembered which ones she liked. This was the fact Agnes could not reconcile. Rage had a classification, a shelf location, a clear label. The cookies did not.

---

Agnes waited three days.

She had been watching Carmen Reyes since the confrontation with Harry. Not closely, because close attention from a woman classified as nonverbal would be conspicuous, but in the peripheral way she had always monitored her environment. Forty-two years behind a pharmacy counter had trained her to check prescriptions with her hands while tracking the rest of the store with her eyes. The skill transferred.

Carmen was performing her own uncertainty. She came to work, did her job, administered medications with the same competent warmth she always showed, and then hesitated, a beat too long at certain doorways, an extra second checking vitals she had already checked, a glance at Agnes's face that lingered before Carmen remembered to look away. Carmen was deciding something. Agnes could see the decision turning behind her eyes, the same slow orbit she had watched at the pharmacy counter in customers working up to admitting they could not afford their prescription.

On Wednesday evening, Carmen came to Room 108A for the seven o'clock medication pass. Agnes's standing orders were a statin she did not need (her LDL had been well-controlled through diet for over a decade, but the facility physician had prescribed based on age-bracket protocols without ordering a lipid panel) and a low-dose aspirin that was appropriate for secondary stroke prevention and was therefore the only medication on her list Agnes approved of.

Carmen knocked on the open door. "Evening, Mrs. Chen. Got your meds." She came in with the medication cup, the water, the clipboard. Professional, warm, slightly too careful. She glanced at Margaret, who was asleep. She set the cup on Agnes's tray table.

Agnes was in the chair by the window. The window faced the parking lot and, beyond it, three sugar maples whose leaves had been red and orange that morning and were now, in the early dark of October, just shapes. She turned her head and looked at Carmen.

Not the performance look. Not the unfocused gaze that slid across surfaces and registered nothing. She looked at Carmen the way she had looked at prescription labels for forty-two years: directly, with the full weight of professional attention. She looked at Carmen as she had once looked at the Board of Pharmacy investigator who questioned her refusal to fill a dangerous prescription.

Carmen stopped moving.

Agnes took off her glasses, cleaned them on the hem of her shirt — an old habit from behind the counter, a way to buy three seconds before speaking — and put them back on. She said: "Close the door."

Her voice was dry from twelve hours of daytime silence. At night, during ring meetings, she spoke freely, but her daytime vocal cords had been resting and the sound came out slightly hoarse. She cleared her throat once. She did not repeat the instruction.

Carmen closed the door. She did it slowly, her hand on the handle, her body held in the cautious suspension of a nurse encountering a sudden change in a patient's presentation. When she turned back, Agnes could see the recalibration happening in real time: the clinical training, the assessment instincts, running this new information against three years of chart notes that said this woman could not speak.

"Sit," Agnes said.

Carmen sat on the edge of Margaret's bed. Margaret did not stir. Carmen put the clipboard in her lap and looked at Agnes with controlled alarm, the face of a clinician who was not yet sure whether a dramatic change in status was a crisis or a discovery.

"You can talk," Carmen said.

"I have always been able to talk. I was placed here after a stroke that temporarily affected my speech. The speech recovered. The placement did not."

Carmen's mouth opened, then closed. Agnes let the pause hold. She had patience for necessary processing time.

"How long have you been —"

"Three years. I scored 30 out of 30 on a Mini-Mental exam in my first year here. The result is in my chart. No one has acted on it."

Carmen looked at the medication cup on the tray table. She was doing what nurses did when the clinical picture shifted: reassessing, recategorizing, trying to fit new data into an existing framework. Agnes watched her fail to fit it.

"Your daughter," Carmen said.

"My daughter."

A silence settled between them. Carmen's hand went to her badge, adjusted it, an automatic motion Agnes had seen her make during medication counts that did not add up. Then Carmen said, "The statin — on your med list. Is it —"

"Unnecessary. My LDL has been below a hundred for twelve years through dietary management. The prescribing physician did not order a lipid panel. He prescribed by age and the facility formulary defaults." A pause. "The aspirin is appropriate. I take the aspirin."

Carmen studied her. Agnes could see the professional recognition happening, one clinician registering another. Carmen said, "You've been — you're the one who does the inventory. The sorting. Harry said someone tracked the expirations and the —"

"I am a pharmacist. I was a pharmacist for forty-two years at the same store. I track expirations, drug interactions, and buyer contraindications because no one else in the operation has the training to do it safely. An unmonitored pharmaceutical distribution is dangerous regardless of its legal status."

Carmen said nothing for several seconds. Then: "Why are you telling me this now?"

"Because you have been deliberating for two weeks and the deliberation is visible. You spend six extra seconds at this door every shift. You check my vitals and then stand in the hallway deciding how you feel about what you know. You are trying to determine whether the people in this operation are victims who need your protection or criminals who need your judgment, and neither category fits, and the discomfort of that is what you are actually managing. Not the situation. Your own response to it."

Carmen's expression shifted. Not to anger, which Agnes had anticipated, but to something more uncomfortable. Recognition.

"I did not ask for your protection," Agnes continued. "Neither did Harry or Lola or Earl. You found an operation that was functioning adequately without your participation, and you have spent two weeks treating your uncertainty as the primary problem. It is not. The primary problem is that people outside this building cannot afford the medications they need, and medications inside this building are being prescribed to patients who do not require them or are being dosed incorrectly. I have the training to manage that gap safely. I have been doing so for fourteen months."

Carmen's jaw worked. Agnes watched her resist several responses.

"I could lose my license," Carmen said.

"That is a real cost. I will not pretend it is not." Agnes paused for exactly the interval required to separate an acknowledgment from a dismissal. "But you have already looked the other way during medication counts. You have already given Harry a week instead of calling the police. You have already begun making the accommodation. What you have not done is looked at the actual work."

She pointed toward the closet, a gesture so specific it might have indicated a particular shelf in a particular pharmacy. "I keep records. Inventory logs. Every transaction, every buyer's medical profile, every interaction I have flagged. I have been running quality control on this operation since I joined it, because that is what a pharmacist does." She lowered her hand. "You want to know what you are protecting or what you are thinking about destroying. Come to the meeting. Look at what is in front of you. Then decide. But stop standing in my doorway rehearsing your conscience. It is not useful."

Carmen sat on the edge of the bed. She was quiet. Agnes let the silence hold. She had made her case. She did not make cases twice.

After a while Carmen picked up the medication cup. She looked at the two pills, one larger, one smaller, both white. She looked at Agnes.

"Can you tell which is which?" Carmen asked. "Without the packaging."

Agnes glanced at the cup. "The round one, seven millimeters, scored on one side, is the atorvastatin, ten milligrams, generic. The smaller tablet, unscored, slight bevel on the edge, is the aspirin, eighty-one milligrams. The atorvastatin has a manufacturer's imprint you cannot see from this angle."

Carmen separated the aspirin from the statin and held it out. Agnes took it and washed it down with the water. The statin remained on the tray.

"I'll chart that you took both," Carmen said.

"I know."

Carmen stood. She picked up the clipboard and the cup with the remaining pill and moved toward the door. She stopped with her hand on the handle.

"When's the next meeting?"

"Thursday. One in the morning. Harry will expect you."

Carmen opened the door and stepped into the hallway, and her posture reassembled itself: shoulders settling into the practiced ease of a nurse moving between rooms, face smoothing into professional composure. Agnes watched the shift and recognized it. She performed her own version every day. The difference was that Carmen's performance was a choice she could walk away from. Agnes's was not.

She turned back to the window. The parking lot lights made orange pools on the asphalt. The sugar maples were dark shapes against a darker sky, their October color invisible now, just mass and outline. Behind her, Margaret's breathing cycled: the click, the pause, the drain. Nine seconds. Agnes sat in the chair and waited for Thursday.


# Chapter 9

The flowers arrived at seven-fifteen in the morning, which was how Carmen knew the state was coming.

In thirteen months at Lakeview Gardens, she had never seen a living plant in the lobby. The silk ficus by the reception desk didn't count — it had a coating of dust thick enough to write in, and someone had once hung a pine-tree air freshener from one of its branches like an ornament on the world's saddest Christmas tree. But these were real flowers. Grocery-store carnations in a plastic vase, pink and white, their stems already starting to bend from being jammed together too tightly. They sat on the front desk next to the sign-in clipboard like an apology nobody had asked for.

Cliff Moorehead materialized from his office at seven-twenty, which was its own kind of unprecedented. Carmen had worked here for over a year and could count on one hand the number of times she'd seen the administrator before nine. He was wearing a polo shirt she hadn't seen before — navy, still creased from the package — and khakis with an ironed pleat down each leg. His face had the specific look of a man whose salary depended on a number generated by people he couldn't charm, and he was trying to figure out charm anyway.

"All right, everyone." He clapped his hands once in the hallway outside the nurse's station, the clap of a man who'd read a book about leadership sometime in the nineties and hadn't updated his material. "Surveyors are here at ten. I need all documentation current by nine. All documentation. MDS assessments, care plans, medication records, incident reports. If it's not up to date, it doesn't exist."

Tammy, passing with a stack of linens balanced against her hip, didn't break stride. "So half our patients don't exist."

"Tammy."

"I'm just saying. You want me to chart or you want me to change Mrs. Patterson, because she's been sitting in it since six and I only got two hands."

Cliff's mouth worked for a moment, opening and closing around words that didn't arrive. "Chart first. Then — yes. Prioritize."

"I'm prioritizing Mrs. Patterson's sacrum, Cliff. She's got a Stage Two on her tailbone and she needs turning every two hours and she gets it every three because you won't hire another aide for this wing."

"We're not discussing staffing right now."

"We're never discussing staffing. That's the point." Tammy turned the corner with her linens. Cliff stood in the hallway for another five seconds, then retreated to his office. A moment later, Carmen heard the printer start up — the heavy, grinding sound it made when someone was printing more than ten pages at once.

By eight-thirty, the transformation was visible. Someone — Cliff, or someone Cliff had deputized — had taped a motivational poster to the wall outside the dining room. It featured a sunrise over mountains with the words QUALITY CARE STARTS WITH YOU in a font that suggested clip art and a 3 AM deadline. The edges were still curling from the printer heat. It had been positioned directly over a scuff mark that had been on the wall since before Carmen started, and it covered the scuff but not the nail hole above it, so the effect was less "inspirational workspace" and more "someone is trying very hard."

The carnations had already begun to wilt.

Carmen moved through her morning routines — eight o'clock medications, vitals, the blood sugar check on Mr. Andersen in Wing B, the dressing change on Mrs. Patterson's pressure ulcer — with the focused calm of someone who knew what was coming and had decided how she would handle it. She'd worked a state survey at her previous facility in Edison, a smaller place that got dinged for medication storage temperatures and a bathroom handrail loose enough to spin in its bracket. Surveyors came annually, theoretically unannounced, though facilities always seemed to know within hours of the survey team's departure from their regional office. Cliff had sent a staff email at four-twelve in the morning. Carmen's phone had buzzed on her nightstand while Reggie slept.

She was not worried about the surveyors finding the ring's operation. The ring left no physical evidence in accessible places — Agnes had insisted on that from the beginning, with the unbending precision of a pharmacist who'd spent forty-two years understanding exactly what regulatory scrutiny looked like. No pills in drawers, no cash in envelopes, no notebooks in rooms during the day. Everything moved through Marcus and out the building within hours of a meeting. The prepaid phone was powered down and hidden inside a heating vent cover in Harry's room, behind a screw Agnes had replaced with one that turned by hand.

What worried her was the medication count log.

She had documented every discrepancy herself — had written her initials and her license number next to each one, because that was the procedure, and Carmen followed procedure. Six documented variances in the past three months. All on her shifts, all under her name, all explained in the notes column with the kind of plausible fictions the charge nurse had taught her to write before Carmen understood she was covering for something larger than charting errors: documentation error, shift-change miscommunication, pharmacy delivery discrepancy. Each explanation was the kind of thing that actually happened at understaffed facilities with hybrid paper-electronic systems and overnight nurses who'd been hired two weeks ago. None of them were true.

A competent surveyor would see the pattern. Not necessarily what the pattern meant, but that a pattern existed, and that it centered on one nurse.

She'd known this was coming since the moment she stopped counting honestly. She'd just managed to not think about it as a specific event on a specific date. And now the date was here, and the carnations were dying in the lobby, and Cliff's motivational poster was peeling off the wall.

Dr. Prasad arrived at nine for her biweekly rounds, moving through Wing A with the efficient speed of a physician who saw forty patients a day across three facilities and couldn't afford to linger. She didn't seem to know about the survey — or if she knew, she didn't change her rhythm. She reviewed charts, wrote orders, asked Carmen about Mrs. Patterson's wound progression and Mr. Andersen's morning glucose, and was gone by nine-forty-five. Carmen envied her. Prasad was a doctor. She came, she prescribed, she left. She didn't sit in the building for eight hours knowing that her license number was on a stack of documents that told a story she'd invented.

---

The surveyor's name was Linda Horvath. She was about fifty, with gray-brown hair cut short enough to be practical and long enough to still need tucking behind her ears, which she did every few minutes — a gesture so habitual it seemed less like grooming and more like a kind of punctuation. She wore shoes that told Carmen everything: black, rubber-soled, scuffed at the toes, ugly and clearly comfortable. Those were the shoes of a woman who walked facility hallways for a living and had stopped caring what her feet looked like years ago. She carried a binder thick enough to stop a door, a clipboard with a pen clipped to the top, and a stainless steel coffee thermos she refilled twice from the pot Cliff had set up in the conference room. Real brewed coffee, not the instant packets they usually had — another tell that the state was in the building.

She brought a second surveyor, a younger woman named Keisha who handled the dietary and environmental review while Horvath took clinical. Carmen saw Keisha photographing the dining room steam table with her phone, then crouching to check the temperature gauge on the reach-in cooler, writing something in her own binder. Cliff hovered near both of them with the energy of a man trying to appear relaxed while standing on a nail.

"Can I get you anything?" he asked Horvath for the third time in twenty minutes. "Water? We've got — I set up coffee in the—"

"I'm fine, Mr. Moorehead."

"Cliff. Please."

Horvath didn't use his first name. She opened her binder and began with resident interviews at eleven.

Carmen was doing her late-morning med pass when Horvath entered Room 114A. Harry was in his wheelchair by the window, wearing the blue cardigan he wore every day, staring at the parking lot with the emptied expression of a man who had misplaced the present tense entirely. He'd been in this posture when Carmen had come through with his morning medications at eight — pills set in the cup, water on the tray table, Harry taking them without looking at her, swallowing with the mechanical indifference of someone for whom pills were stimuli, not decisions. She'd watched him do it and had the strange doubled experience of seeing an act she knew was an act performed so well it looked like the thing it was imitating.

Now Horvath sat in the vinyl visitor's chair, the one that exhaled a small puff of disinfectant-scented air when weight hit it, and opened her binder.

"Mr. Zielinski? I'm Linda, from the state health department. I just have a few questions for you today."

A pause. Then Harry's voice, querulous and thin, the voice of a man calling across a distance only he could see: "Eleanor?"

"No, sir. I'm Linda. From the health department. Can you tell me what today's date is?"

Another pause, longer. Harry seemed to be considering the question with genuine effort, his mouth working slightly as though the answer was a physical thing he was trying to locate in a cluttered room. "It's... March? No. No, it's — is it Tuesday?"

"Can you tell me what year it is?"

"Seventy-eight." He said it with sudden confidence, sitting up slightly in the wheelchair, and his voice shifted from lost to certain in a way that was startling. "Seventy-eight, and I'll tell you something, the Yanks are done. Reggie Jackson can't carry them again. Munson's gone, did you hear that? Thurman. Plane crash. That was — was that this year?"

Horvath's pen moved across her clipboard. "Do you know the name of the president?"

"Carter." Harry leaned forward, and his voice took on the urgency of a man who'd been reading the newspaper and needed someone to listen. "Carter, and he's going to lose Iran. I'm telling you. Eleanor won't listen to me about it but I've been watching the news and that Shah is finished. We've got people over there, embassy people. If Carter doesn't get them out — you know what the price of gas was last week? A dollar and two cents at the Exxon on Route 18. A dollar and two cents. That's — Eleanor would know what it was before—" He stopped. The urgency drained from his face. He looked at his hands. "Where's Eleanor?"

"I'm not sure who Eleanor is, Mr. Zielinski."

"She was just here. She comes at — what time is it? She brings the — there's a thing she brings." He gestured vaguely at the nightstand. "With the..."

"That's all right. Can you tell me the name of this place? Where you are right now?"

Harry looked around Room 114A as though seeing it for the first time. His gaze traveled across the adjustable bed, the nightstand with its box of tissues and plastic water pitcher, the window with its view of the employee parking lot. He seemed baffled by all of it — not frightened, not distressed, just genuinely unable to reconcile his surroundings with whatever internal geography he was navigating.

"Is this the — no." He rubbed his face. His hand trembled slightly — a tremor Carmen had never seen from him before, which meant it was part of the performance, added today for the surveyor's benefit. "I don't. Where's Eleanor? She'd know."

Carmen stood at the sub-station near the Wing A entrance, charting vitals she'd already charted, and listened. Nine minutes. Horvath spent nine minutes with Harry Zielinski, and Carmen timed it because she couldn't help herself, the way you watch a tightrope walker and count the seconds not because you want them to fall but because you need to know how long a person can sustain the impossible.

Nine minutes of a man whose mind could run the financial architecture of a pharmaceutical distribution network answering every question with a 1978 that never existed — but a 1978 so detailed, so internally consistent in its specifics, that Horvath wrote steadily the entire time. Harry mentioned the score of a Yankees game that hadn't been played, the price of gas at a specific station on a specific highway, a presidential crisis he'd conflated with events from four years earlier in a way that mimicked the temporal drift of genuine dementia — the past collapsing into itself, years stacking on top of each other like transparencies on an overhead projector.

He asked about Eleanor six times. Each time, the question arrived with a slightly different inflection — confused, then concerned, then plaintive, then matter-of-fact, then lost again — as though her absence was a wound he kept rediscovering from different angles.

It was the best acting Carmen had ever seen. Not because it was flawless but because it wasn't. Harry let his face go slack at slightly wrong moments, let his eyes wander mid-sentence to track something only he could see, produced the kind of micro-errors that looked like a broken mind rather than a performing one. A bad actor would have been too consistently confused. Harry was inconsistent in exactly the right ways, the ways that real dementia was inconsistent — patches of startling clarity inside the fog, sudden conviction about a false fact, the confused person's particular stubbornness about details that didn't matter.

When Horvath left Room 114A, Carmen was at the medication cart, drawing up an insulin dose for Mr. Andersen. She glanced toward Harry's doorway. In the small window above the door handle, she could see his reflection in the glass — he was watching Horvath walk away, and his face was completely still. The man in 1978 was gone. The man who ran things was back. He held the stillness for two seconds, three, then let his face slacken again, and the transition was so smooth it was like watching water close over a stone.

---

Agnes was next. Room 108A.

Her roommate, a ninety-one-year-old woman named Doris who had advanced Alzheimer's and hadn't spoken a recognizable word in eight months, was asleep in the bed by the window, her mouth open, her breathing a thick, slow rasp that was audible from the hallway. Agnes sat in the chair by her own bed, hands folded in her lap, eyes fixed on a point approximately fourteen inches above the floor. She did not look up when Horvath entered.

"Mrs. Chen? I'm Linda, from the state health department."

Nothing.

"Mrs. Chen, can you hear me?"

Agnes's eyes moved — not to Horvath but to a different point on the floor, six inches to the right of the first point. Her expression didn't change. She might have been watching a crack in the linoleum. She might have been watching nothing at all.

"Can you tell me your name?"

Agnes opened her mouth. She made a sound — not quite a word, not quite silence. Something between a sigh and a syllable, like a radio tuned to the space between stations. Then she closed her mouth and resumed staring.

Horvath tried three more questions, spacing them out with the patience of a professional who'd been trained to give silence room. Agnes responded to none of them. At one point she raised her right hand — the one with the slight weakness from her stroke, which she normally overrode with the sheer force of her pharmaceutical precision — and touched her own face with the slow, exploratory gesture of someone encountering their own features for the first time. She ran her fingers along her jaw, her cheek, the bridge of her nose. Then her hand returned to her lap.

Four minutes. Horvath wrote a note, closed her binder, and left.

Carmen was passing in the hallway with a blood pressure cuff, heading toward 110A, and she caught a two-second glimpse through the closing door. Agnes was watching Horvath's back with the focused, evaluative expression of a pharmacist watching a technician count pills — not hostile, not amused, just assessing. Her eyes were completely lucid, her posture alert. Then the door swung shut and the glimpse was gone.

A seventy-six-year-old woman with forty-two years of pharmaceutical expertise, a woman who could calculate drug interactions in her head faster than the pharmacy software, who had once nearly lost her license for refusing to fill a contraindicated prescription because she'd rather lose the license than lose a patient — sitting in a vinyl chair in a room that smelled like Doris's incontinence pads, pretending she didn't know what language was.

---

Lola was in the dayroom when Horvath found her.

She'd positioned herself in the chair nearest the window, wearing a pink sweater with a small stain on the collar — coffee, Carmen thought, and probably deliberate. Her gold crucifix caught the late-morning light through the window. Her hands were wrapped around a rosary, the beads threaded between her fingers, and the tremor in her right hand made the beads click against each other in a soft, arrhythmic pattern, like a clock that had lost its mechanism but kept trying.

"Mrs. Fernandez? I'm Linda, from the—"

"Oh!" Lola looked up with such genuine-seeming delight that even Carmen, who knew the act, felt a moment of warmth. "Mija, sit down, sit. You want coffee? The coffee here isn't — well, it's not coffee, it's more like — you know my husband, Tomás, he used to make café con leche, real café, from scratch, his mother's recipe, she was from — where was she from?" Lola looked at the ceiling. "Matanzas? No, that's not right. Somewhere near there. A town outside Matanzas. There was a bakery. She always talked about this bakery—"

"Mrs. Fernandez, I just need to ask you a few—"

"—and the bread, Dios mío, she said the bread was so good you could cry, and Tomás always said she exaggerated about Cuba, you know how sons are about their mothers, but I think she didn't, I think the bread was exactly that good, because when we went to Miami for his cousin's wedding — when was that? Was that before the children or after? No, it was after, because Elena was walking already, she was — she had those little shoes—"

Horvath tried again. "Can you tell me—"

"—pink shoes, with a buckle, and Tomás said they were too expensive but I said what is too expensive for your daughter's feet? Forty dollars. For shoes she'd outgrow in six months and he acted like I'd bought a Cadillac. Men." Lola shook her head with fond exasperation, then turned to Horvath with sudden attention. "Are you married, mija?"

"I — yes."

"He treats you good?"

"Mrs. Fernandez—"

"You look tired. They work you too hard, I can see it. You eat today? You're thin. Not too thin, you look nice, but thin. Tomás's mother, she always said—"

Something shifted in Horvath's face. Not suspicion — the recalibration of a woman who'd done this job long enough to recognize when a conversation had left her control and wasn't coming back. She tried once more. "Do you know what medications you take?"

"Medications?" Lola's face went soft with confusion — not Harry's elaborate temporal displacement but something warmer, more like a woman who'd simply lost the thread of a conversation she'd been enjoying. "The pills? Oh, the pills. They give me — the girl gives me the — what's her name, la morena, the one with the — the pretty one—"

"Do you know what the pills are for?"

"For?" Lola held up her tremoring right hand, the rosary beads clicking. "This, I think. For the shaking. And for my — for sleeping. And there's a white one, a big white one that I don't like because it's — Tomás, you know, he never took pills. Not one. Not even aspirin. He said his mother taught him that the body knows how to heal itself, which, you know—" She leaned closer to Horvath, conspiratorial. "He was wrong about that. Obviously. But you couldn't tell him."

Twenty-two minutes.

Carmen knew because she was in the dayroom charting vitals for the Wing B residents and couldn't leave without making it obvious she was watching. Twenty-two minutes of Lola Fernandez spinning a fabric of her dead husband, her daughter's baby shoes, a bakery in Cuba that may or may not have existed, a trip to Miami that kept restarting from different beginnings each time she circled back to it, and a detailed account of Tomás's opinions about pills that somehow became a story about their first apartment in Union City. She never answered a single one of Horvath's questions. She never refused to answer, either. She was warm and delighted by the company and utterly, comprehensively useless as a source of clinical information, and the warmth was real — that was the thing. Lola genuinely liked people. She genuinely wanted Horvath to sit and talk. The performance worked because it was built on something true.

Horvath's handwriting got smaller and faster. She checked her watch twice. The second time, she closed her binder with a finality that was courteous but absolute.

"Thank you, Mrs. Fernandez. You've been very helpful."

"You're leaving? But I didn't finish telling you about the bakery. In Matanzas. Or near Matanzas, I don't—"

"Maybe next time."

"You come back, mija. And eat something. Promise me."

Horvath walked out of the dayroom with the stride of a woman who'd just lost twenty-two minutes she couldn't recover. She tucked her hair behind her ears. Her pen clicked against her binder three times in rapid succession.

Carmen looked at Lola. Lola was looking down at the rosary in her hands, turning the beads with her tremoring fingers, and her face — just for a moment, before the performance rebuilt itself — was tired in a way that had nothing to do with the talking. Tired from the distance between the woman telling the story and the woman choosing which story to tell.

---

Earl and Burt were interviewed together in Room 116A because Horvath was behind schedule.

Earl was in his bed, propped up on two pillows, a three-month-old issue of Time open on his lap to an article about western water rights he'd already read. When Horvath entered, he looked at her with the flat, uninvested expression of a man who had seen too many people come through this room to put energy into another one. He coughed — the deep, wet rattle that Carmen had been hearing more of lately, the kind of cough that came from somewhere below the chest and took its time leaving — and turned a page he wasn't reading.

"Mr. Washington, can you tell me today's date?"

Earl looked at her. He looked at the magazine. He looked at her again. Then he closed his eyes.

Horvath waited. She was good at waiting — Carmen could see that. Patient in the practiced way of someone who'd sat through a hundred of these silences.

Earl's breathing deepened. His head tilted slightly left. Within thirty seconds, he appeared to be asleep. He wasn't — Carmen could see from the hallway the way his fingers still held the magazine's edges, gripping rather than releasing — but Horvath couldn't see that from the visitor's angle, and the performance was more than good enough for a surveyor with sixteen facilities left this quarter.

Burt, in the bed by the window, was a different kind of quiet.

Burt Kovacs was eighty-eight and his dementia was real. He'd been playing since before the ring was a concept and long before the ring was a word anyone used — playing clubs in New York and Philadelphia and Budapest, his wife Erzsébet in the audience for the early years, his hands moving over piano keys with the speed and intention of someone who thought in music. Now those hands lay on the thermal blanket, long-fingered and mostly still, occasionally twitching in patterns that might have been remembered chord progressions or might have been nothing at all.

"Mr. Kovacs?" Horvath leaned forward, speaking clearly. "Can you tell me your name?"

Burt looked at her. His eyes moved across her face the way they moved across most faces now — searching, not quite finding, the effort of recognition visible but the result uncertain. He was alert in the way a person could be alert and lost at the same time.

"Erzsi?" he said softly. Then, in Hungarian, a string of syllables that sounded like a question asked with great tenderness to someone who wasn't in the room.

"I'm Linda. Can you tell me where you are?"

"The music is..." Burt trailed off. He seemed to listen to something that existed only for him — his head tilted, his fingers twitched on the blanket, and for a moment his face held the concentration of a man hearing a passage he wanted to remember. Then the concentration dissolved into something like distress. "Where's — the thing. The book. My book."

Horvath wrote a notation. She asked two more questions. Burt answered one with a fragment about Erzsébet that trailed into Hungarian and ignored the other entirely, staring at his own hands as if they'd been attached to his wrists by someone else and he was still adjusting to the arrangement.

Six minutes. The fastest interview of the day.

It was funny and it wasn't. The other performances — Harry's Oscar-caliber 1978, Agnes's pharmaceutical amnesia, Lola's twenty-two-minute filibuster, Earl's tactical nap — were comedy. Burt was the thing the comedy was built on top of. The confused old man the others were pretending to be was the confused old man Burt actually was, and the gap between their pretending and his being was the gap the whole operation lived inside of.

Carmen went into 116A after Horvath left, ostensibly to check vitals. Earl opened one eye.

"She gone?"

"She's gone."

Earl grunted and reopened his magazine. Burt was still looking at his hands.

---

Horvath found Carmen at the sub-station at two-fifteen, after lunch.

Carmen had been expecting this. Not with dread, exactly — with the specific, concentrated focus she used when she knew a task would require precision under pressure and she couldn't afford to hesitate. She'd rehearsed nothing. Rehearsing would have made her sound rehearsed, and Horvath had been doing this too long to miss the difference between a nurse who was remembering and a nurse who was reciting.

"Ms. Reyes, I'd like to go over some of your medication management documentation."

"Sure." Carmen pulled the second chair out from behind the sub-station desk. "What do you need?"

Horvath sat and opened her binder to a tabbed section. Carmen could see the photocopies — her count sheets, her handwriting, her initials, her license number stamped at the top of each page.

"I'm seeing some variances in your controlled substance counts." Horvath ran her finger down a column of numbers with the steady tracking of someone who read count sheets the way other people read newspapers. "Six documented discrepancies over the past three months. Oxycodone, alprazolam, tramadol. All occurring on your shifts."

Carmen's hands were on the desk, fingers laced. She'd placed them there before Horvath had started talking — a deliberate choice, because if she held a pen she'd click it, and if she touched her badge she'd adjust it, and her hands would do the things they did when she was lying and she didn't trust them not to.

"Right. I documented those." Her voice was level, professional. The voice of a nurse who'd been through audits before, which she had — just never from this side. "We've had some issues with the overnight handoff. The hybrid charting system — we're half electronic, half paper for the controlled substance logs — it creates gaps. There was a period over the summer where the night nurse was documenting counts on the paper log but not entering them in the system, so when I'd do my end-of-shift count, the electronic total didn't match the physical count."

It came out the way she needed it to. Not too smooth — she paused where a nurse recalling a genuine frustration would pause, frowned at the memory of the documentation mess, let her voice carry the specific irritation of a competent person whose work had been made to look sloppy by someone else's failures. The story was the kind of thing that actually happened at facilities like Lakeview, where systems were held together with habit and inertia and the night nurse might have been hired off a staffing app last Tuesday.

Horvath wrote something on her clipboard. "You attributed three of the six discrepancies to overnight documentation errors."

"Three to overnight, one to a pharmacy delivery that came in short — I called the pharmacy on that one, they confirmed the miscount on their end and sent a corrected invoice — and two I honestly couldn't track down. I documented them as unresolved variances. That's what the protocol says to do."

"The protocol says to notify your administrator."

"I notified Mr. Moorehead on both unresolved discrepancies. In writing." This part was true. Carmen had sent Cliff two emails, dated and timestamped. Cliff had not responded to either one, because Cliff did not respond to things that might require him to do something.

Horvath looked at her. It was not an accusatory look. It was the look of a woman who had reviewed controlled substance logs at dozens of facilities this quarter and had this exact conversation at half of them. The same discrepancies, the same explanations, the same hybrid systems and overnight handoffs and pharmacy miscounts. The infrastructure of American nursing home care produced these gaps as reliably as it produced bedsores and staff turnover, and Horvath had been swimming in it long enough to know that most of the time the paperwork problems were real and the explanations were true and the system was simply broken in the ordinary way.

Carmen could see the competence in how Horvath read the log — not scanning but tracking, following the numbers across columns the way Agnes tracked dosages, catching the pattern of Carmen's initials recurring next to every variance. In a different world, with fewer facilities to cover and more hours in the day and a mandate that extended beyond documentation review into actual investigation, Horvath might have looked harder. Might have pulled the pharmacy invoices. Might have cross-referenced Carmen's shift schedule against the specific medications that came up short and noticed that oxycodone and alprazolam — the drugs with the highest street value — were disproportionately represented.

But Horvath had seventeen more facilities to visit before the end of the quarter. The skin under her eyes was dark and loose, and her pen had been clicking against her binder for the past ten minutes in a rhythm that suggested not enough sleep and too much coffee.

"I'm going to note the documentation gaps as a finding," Horvath said. "You'll need to implement a reconciliation process for the paper-electronic crossover. Both formats should be updated simultaneously at the point of count."

"Absolutely. I've actually been talking to our charge nurse about that."

She hadn't. She wouldn't.

Horvath made a final note, closed her binder, and stood. "Your medication administration records are thorough, Ms. Reyes. I can see you take the documentation seriously."

"Thank you."

"The discrepancies are a concern, but the documentation trail supports your explanations. I'll include recommendations in the report."

Carmen nodded. Smiled — not too much, just the professional acknowledgment of a review that had gone well. Horvath gathered her thermos and binder, tucked her hair behind both ears simultaneously, and headed toward the conference room where Cliff was waiting in a chair he'd positioned to face the door, his posture suggesting a man who expected to be told something he didn't want to hear.

Carmen sat at the sub-station for another minute after Horvath left. She unlaced her fingers and found that they'd been pressing against each other hard enough to leave white marks on her knuckles. She flexed them until the color came back.

---

The survey concluded at four with a stack of findings that would keep Cliff busy for two weeks: the medication documentation gaps that Carmen had invented a reconciliation process for, a dietary storage temperature variance Keisha had photographed, two overdue care plan updates, a hand sanitizer dispenser that was empty, and the motivational poster, which Keisha had noted was covering a fire safety placard in violation of corridor clearance standards. Minor deficiencies. The kind of findings that let a facility continue operating without penalty while generating enough paperwork to prove the state health department was performing its function.

Cliff ordered pizza. Three boxes from a place on Route 1 — pepperoni, plain, and something with roasted vegetables that no one touched. He set them on the break room table with a two-liter of warm Pepsi and a sleeve of paper cups and told the staff they'd done a great job. He used those exact words. "Great job, everybody." He said it twice. He didn't mention Mrs. Patterson's pressure ulcer, or the staffing ratio that made it inevitable, or the fact that the facility's survey score was a number that measured documentation completeness rather than whether anyone in the building was actually being cared for.

Tammy took two slices of pepperoni and ate them standing by the break room window, watching the surveyors' state sedan back out of its parking space.

"Flowers are dead," she said, chewing.

"They're not dead," Cliff said. "They're wilting."

"They're dead, Cliff. Rest in peace."

Carmen didn't eat. She poured a cup of the warm Pepsi, drank half, and tipped the rest into the break room sink. She finished her shift. She charted the afternoon medications, updated Mrs. Patterson's wound assessment — the ulcer was the same, neither better nor worse, which was what happened when you turned someone every three hours instead of two — and restocked the medication cart for the evening nurse. Normal work. The kind of work she could do with her hands and the front of her mind while the rest of her sat somewhere else entirely, in a place she couldn't yet look at directly.

At six-forty, she clocked out. She walked to her car. She sat in the driver's seat and put the key in the ignition.

She did not turn it.

The parking lot was half-empty — day shift gone, evening shift trickling in, the gap visible in the spaces between cars. Late September light came in low and amber through the windshield, the kind of light that made everything look like a photograph of itself. A plastic bag skidded across the asphalt and caught on a shopping cart someone had left near the dumpster. The courtyard trees were starting to turn — just barely, the first suggestions of yellow at the edges of leaves that had been green all summer.

Carmen sat with her hands on the steering wheel, her key in the ignition, her foot on nothing, and looked at the building she'd spent the day protecting.

Beige stucco. Small windows with institutional blinds, half of them crooked. A sign that read LAKEVIEW GARDENS REHABILITATION AND NURSING CENTER in letters that had faded from green to a color that was no longer any color in particular. No lake. No garden. Three scrubby trees in a paved courtyard and a view of Route 1 if you stood on your toes.

Inside that building, Harry Zielinski was sitting in his wheelchair, possibly staring at the parking lot, possibly staring at 1978, and there was no way to know which from out here. Agnes Chen was in her room with Doris's breathing for company, her hands folded and her mind running pharmaceutical calculations that no one would ever need again. Lola Fernandez was holding her rosary and the story about the bakery near Matanzas was over, and the distance between the woman who told it and the woman who chose to tell it was the distance Lola crossed every day and couldn't talk about with anyone. Earl Washington was coughing into a magazine. Burt Kovacs was looking at his hands.

And Carmen Reyes, LPN, license number 26NR03891, had sat across from a state health department surveyor and manufactured a coherent, plausible, procedurally fluent explanation for the documented evidence of a controlled substance diversion operation running out of a nursing home in central New Jersey. She had done it without raising her voice or touching her badge or clicking a pen.
