She had done it with the specific competence of a nurse who understood exactly which excuses a surveyor with too many facilities and not enough time would accept without further investigation.

She had done it without hesitating.

Her phone buzzed on the passenger seat. Her mother — probably about picking up Reggie. She let it ring.

She sat in her car for fifteen minutes. She watched the light change from amber to something grayer. She felt the steering wheel under her hands, the vinyl warm from the closed car and the day's last sun, and she noticed that her grip was tight enough to feel her own pulse in her fingertips.

She wasn't crying. She was somewhere else — not before tears or after them but in a different country from tears entirely, a place where the thing she'd done was settling into the fact of her life the way sediment settles into water, becoming part of the composition. She had lied to a government official. She had lied fluently, competently, and well. She had protected five elderly people running a prescription drug operation out of a facility that couldn't be bothered to turn its patients on schedule, and she had done it because she understood why they did what they did, and because the alternative would have destroyed them and probably destroyed her, and because when the moment came she discovered she was good at it.

That was the part that sat in her like a stone she'd swallowed. Not that she'd lied. That she'd been good at it. That the same professional competence she'd spent seven years building — the documentation, the precision, the procedural fluency that was supposed to be the thing that made her trustworthy — was exactly what made her an effective liar. The skills were the same skills. The voice was the same voice.

She turned the key. The engine caught on the first try. She put the car in reverse, backed out of her space, and drove to her mother's house to pick up her son.


# Chapter 10

Harry had said one o'clock. Carmen had asked for the meeting three days after the survey — three days after she sat in her car unable to drive, feeling the specific weight of what she'd told the state surveyor settle into a place she couldn't reach to remove it. She'd found Harry alone in the TV room during evening med pass, leaned against the doorframe while Lola pretended to watch a game show behind him, and said, "Show me all of it." Harry had said one o'clock. Nothing else.

So at one o'clock she walked the length of Wing A past Devon sleeping at the nurse's station with his phone face-down on his chest, past Room 110 where Mrs. Rowan was making the low steady sound that Carmen's brain had reclassified as building noise months ago, and pushed open the TV room door.

Harry sat in his wheelchair at the card table — the one the activities aide used for bingo on Tuesdays — wearing the faded blue cardigan he wore to every after-midnight meeting Carmen had attended. Agnes sat across from him. Lola was at her post by the hallway door, one hand resting on the gold crucifix at her throat, her other hand still in the tremor that worsened at night. Earl sat in the vinyl recliner by the far window with his eyes closed, though Carmen could tell from the set of his shoulders that he was listening. Between Harry and Agnes, spread flat under the fluorescent tube that buzzed from a loose ballast, were Agnes's notebooks.

There were four of them. Composition books, the black-and-white marbled kind you could buy at CVS for a dollar nineteen, and Agnes had filled them in handwriting so small and uniform it looked typeset. Agnes didn't look up when Carmen pulled a vinyl chair to the table and sat. She was sorting pill bottles by a system Carmen couldn't immediately read — not alphabetical, not by DEA schedule. Later she'd realize it was by buyer need, grouped into dispensing sets the way a pharmacist would prep an order.

"Sit," Harry said, which Carmen was already doing.

He started with the money.

Eighteen months. That was the first number. The operation had been running since early last year, since before Carmen started at Lakeview, since before Carmen had even applied to Lakeview. Roughly four thousand dollars a month in gross revenue. Forty-eight thousand, approximately, total.

He said the numbers the way he said all numbers — flat, exact, stripped of everything but value. His posture was a man giving a quarterly review: shoulders squared against the wheelchair back, voice level, each figure placed between them like a coin on a table. Carmen had expected the total to shock her. It didn't. Forty-eight thousand dollars over eighteen months sounded like a lot until you divided it by the number of people involved and the number of months they'd been doing this. It was roughly what Carmen made in a year before taxes at a job that was supposed to be legal.

"Marcus takes thirty percent," Harry said. "About twelve hundred a month. His cut for distribution."

"And risk," Carmen said.

Harry looked at her. A beat. "And risk."

Marcus was the one in a car with pills in the center console, the one meeting people he barely knew in a strip mall parking lot, the one who'd go to prison in the unambiguous way — not the way where sympathetic news segments showed elderly faces and the judge considered the defendants' ages and the public defender mentioned diminished capacity. Marcus would get sentenced. He'd do time. Carmen didn't need Harry to explain why thirty percent was a number Marcus might reasonably consider low.

"After his cut," Harry said. "Twenty-eight hundred a month. Distributed."

He turned one of Agnes's notebooks to face Carmen and pointed at a page of handwritten double-entry ledger — old-fashioned, meticulous, the kind of bookkeeping Harry's generation had learned before spreadsheets made it quaint. Revenue by month on the left. Outflows on the right.

Jack Zielinski. Harry's grandson, twenty-six. He'd started a landscaping business in South Brunswick with equipment bought on credit and a fifteen-thousand-dollar hole he couldn't climb out of on what landscaping paid in the off-season. Harry sent five hundred a month to an account Jack believed was funded by a certificate of deposit his grandfather had cashed before the nursing home. The money went through a prepaid debit card Marcus loaded with cash, which showed up in Jack's account as a recurring transfer from a name Jack didn't recognize and hadn't thought to question. Harry's voice didn't change when he talked about Jack. It stayed flat and numerical, the accountant's register, but his hand on the notebook page pressed down a little harder when he said the name, and Carmen noticed.

Denise Washington. Earl's daughter, home health aide in Trenton. Three kids in school who needed coats and fees and field-trip money and the kind of expenses that sounded small when you listed them individually and consumed every dollar Denise didn't have when you added them up. Earl's share — four hundred a month — arrived wrapped in a lie: a VA supplemental benefit program for Korean War veterans that did not exist in any government database. In the vinyl recliner by the window, Earl didn't open his eyes. He coughed once — a deep, wet, rattling sound that came from somewhere lower than his throat — and settled back into his stillness. Nobody commented on the cough. Carmen had noticed it before, during other meetings, and each time it seemed a little heavier.

Lola's share went to Iglesia de la Caridad in Little Havana, a church she'd attended for thirty years before her son moved her north. Three hundred and fifty a month by money order, no return address. The church ran a free clinic three days a week — blood pressure, diabetes screenings, the baseline care that kept people out of emergency rooms and cost almost nothing to provide and was funded by almost nothing because the people who needed it had almost nothing. From the doorway, Lola made a small sound when Harry named the church — not a word, just an exhalation, something between recognition and prayer. Then she went back to watching the hallway.

Burt's share covered the gap between what Medicare said he was entitled to and what his body actually required. A hearing aid — not the four-thousand-dollar kind, not even the decent kind, but something better than the pharmacy-counter amplifier the facility had provided, which turned every sound into the same compressed roar and gave him headaches by noon. Compression stockings that fit his legs instead of the one-size-fits-nothing pairs the supply company shipped in bulk. The brand-name version of his Parkinson's medication, carbidopa-levodopa, because the generic the facility pharmacy had substituted eight months ago made his tremor worse and left him nauseated for two hours after every dose, and nobody had reviewed the substitution because nobody reviewed anything. Five hundred a month, roughly, depending on what needed replacing.

Agnes.

Harry said her name and stopped. He didn't say the amount. Agnes was sitting three feet away, and the amount was hers to claim or not.

Agnes's share sat in a white envelope under her mattress. She didn't explain. Carmen looked at her — at the wire-rimmed glasses, at the precise hands still turning pill bottles label-forward, at the short gray hair and the slight leftward tilt from the stroke that had given her daughter the excuse to put her here. Agnes had no one to send the money to. No grandchildren. No church. No cause she believed in that needed four hundred dollars a month. Her daughter Helen visited every other Sunday with the children and talked to Agnes in the slow, loud voice of someone addressing a condition rather than a person, and Agnes sat through it with the compressed fury of a woman who could conjugate drug interactions in her sleep but who had been classified, filed, and stored. Her money accumulated under the mattress the way her expertise accumulated inside her silence — purposeless, precise, unclaimed.

"That's where the money goes," Harry said.

He said it without editorializing, without framing it as defense or justification. He said it the way he'd said every number: as fact. This is what exists. Carmen could do what she wanted with the information.

She sat with the notebooks for a while. The fluorescent tube buzzed. Outside the window, the parking lot's sodium lights turned the courtyard trees the same flat orange — the leaves were starting to go, it was almost October, and a few had drifted onto the benches nobody sat on at night. The building settled around them with its nocturnal sounds: Mrs. Rowan, the ventilation cycling, the toilet in Room 109 that maintenance had been asked to fix three times and hadn't.

Carmen didn't think about legality. She noticed this the way you notice you've been holding your breath — something that started before she was aware of it and was now simply true. She thought about flow. About the institutional machinery she worked inside: medications prescribed by Dr. Prasad, who saw forty patients a day across three facilities and spent eight minutes per visit and couldn't be expected to hold eighty drug profiles in her head; dispensed by a pharmacy that substituted generics without chart review and delivered in bulk shipments nobody checked against individual care plans; administered by nurses who couldn't flag overprescription without risking their standing with the physician they depended on for orders. The system produced surplus. It overprescribed because underprescribing required conversations, follow-up, clinical judgment — and those things cost staff hours that cost money that Cliff Moorehead's budget didn't have. A resident who was agitated got lorazepam. A resident who couldn't sleep got trazodone. The medications accumulated in bodies and in cabinets and on charts, and nobody asked whether the accumulation served the patient because the question itself was more expensive than the pills.

And on the other side: Rosa with lupus. Derek rationing metformin. Janine cut loose from her pain management because her doctor was nervous about the DEA's opioid-prescribing audits and it was easier to drop patients than fight the board. People whose conditions hadn't changed but whose access had — who needed the same medications they'd always needed and couldn't get them because the system that produced the surplus had no connection to the system that produced the deficit, and the only bridge between them was this room, these people, this card table.

"The buyer list," Harry said.

Agnes pushed a composition book toward Carmen — the most recent one, its spine still stiff. Carmen opened it and understood the organizing principle immediately. The pages were divided not by name but by diagnosis. Agnes's handwriting in ruled columns: LUPUS-SLE. TYPE 2 DM. CHRONIC PAIN — MUSCULOSKELETAL. HYPERTENSION. HYPERLIPIDEMIA. Under each heading: dosages, quantities, frequencies, a coding system Carmen didn't recognize, and in the margins, notes in red ink.

"No names," Carmen said.

"Names are not relevant." Agnes said this the way she said everything — once, without decoration. Her hands kept working the pill bottles, sorting by some internal logic that was as natural to her as a pulse check was to Carmen. "A person with lupus needs hydroxychloroquine. That fact does not change with their name. I organize by need."

Carmen turned the pages. The entries were meticulous. Next to each diagnosis, Agnes had noted contraindications, maximum daily dosages, and potential interactions with medications the buyer might be taking from other sources. Three entries were marked with red asterisks and a notation Carmen could read as clinical shorthand: FLAG — INTERACTION RISK.

"The flagged ones," Carmen said. "You contacted them."

"Marcus contacted them. I provided the guidance." Agnes aligned a row of amber bottles with her fingertips — small adjustments, millimeters. "I do not dispense medications that could harm someone. I have never done that. I will not begin now."

There was no bitterness in her voice. Agnes didn't traffic in bitterness. She trafficked in facts, and this was one: the institutional pharmacy that had substituted Burt's Parkinson's medication without reviewing his chart, that had delayed Agnes's own prescriptions for two weeks during a supplier switch three years ago while she sat in her room in pain that no one checked on because her chart said she was non-verbal — that pharmacy operated under the full authority of the state of New Jersey and the federal regulatory apparatus that licensed it. Agnes's composition notebooks, organized by diagnosis and screened for interactions by a pharmacist with four decades of retail experience, operated from a card table in a room that smelled like vinyl upholstery and the cold coffee Lola had smuggled from the kitchen. Carmen could see which system was more careful. She could see it in the handwriting.

Agnes opened the second notebook — older, its cover softened from use — to a page in the middle. Two columns. The left was headed FACILITY MONTHLY MEDICATION COST PER RESIDENT (INSTITUTIONAL RATE). The right: EQUIVALENT MONTHLY COST (UNINSURED, RETAIL).

The numbers on the left Carmen recognized. A resident at Lakeview might be prescribed eight to twelve medications. The facility's pharmacy billed Medicare at institutional rates, and the per-resident monthly drug cost regularly exceeded two thousand dollars. Some of it necessary. Some of it the institutional reflex to medicate every symptom — agitation, insomnia, appetite loss, anxiety — that might otherwise require staff time the facility didn't have to give.

The numbers on the right were the ones Carmen hadn't seen laid out like this. What it cost to buy the same medications at a retail pharmacy without insurance coverage. Hydroxychloroquine, two hundred milligrams, ninety count: three hundred eighty-nine dollars. Rosuvastatin, twenty milligrams, thirty count: two hundred twelve. Metformin, five hundred milligrams, sixty count: eighty-seven. Oxycodone, five milligrams, thirty count: she didn't need Agnes to tell her what oxycodone cost on the street, but the pharmacy price without insurance was two hundred forty-one dollars, and the markup between what Lakeview paid per unit and what an uninsured person paid at CVS was a factor of six.

"Surplus on the left," Agnes said. She closed the notebook. "Deficit on the right. That is not a moral argument. It is an arithmetic fact."

Harry watched Carmen. He hadn't moved since he finished the distribution breakdown — hands folded on the card table, still, assessing, already calculating what she'd do with what she'd been shown. He had the patience of a man who'd spent a career waiting for ledgers to balance.

"I want you to meet someone," he said. "A buyer. So you see the other side of the numbers."

Carmen looked at the notebooks, at the columns, at the red-ink flags.

"When?"

"Thursday. Your shift starts at three. Be in the lot at two-thirty. Silver Civic, far end."

Carmen closed the composition book and handed it back to Agnes, who took it with the quick, certain grip of a woman who did not drop things.

---

Rosa drove an early 2000s Civic — silver paint going milky at the fenders, a St. Christopher medal on the rearview mirror, a striped beach towel folded over a tear in the passenger seat. She was parked at the far end of Lakeview's lot, away from the entrance and the employee spaces, in the spot where visitors parked when they didn't want to be seen arriving or didn't want anyone to remember them leaving. Carmen knew the spot. Families used it when they came to admit a parent they wouldn't visit again.

Thursday. Two twenty-five. Carmen had arrived early, crossing the lot in her scrubs with her badge clipped and her bag on her shoulder. The afternoon was warm for late September, holding onto a summer that had already ended — the trees in the courtyard were turning but the air hadn't caught up.

Rosa got out of the car when she saw Carmen coming. She was fifty-two and moved like someone whose body charged her for every step — carefully, with a small preliminary adjustment before each motion, the way you navigate a room where the furniture has been rearranged and you haven't learned the new layout yet. She had dark hair cut close, practical, and she was wearing a fleece vest over a long-sleeved shirt despite the warmth. Lupus made her cold. Carmen knew this before Rosa spoke — cold intolerance, Raynaud's, the thermoregulatory disruption that came with autoimmune disease — the same way she'd know a limp meant hip or knee, the automatic clinical recognition that happened before she decided to look.

"You're the nurse," Rosa said.

"Yeah."

"The old man — Harry, whatever his name is — he said you wanted to talk."

"I wanted to understand the costs. What you're paying."

Rosa leaned against the Civic and crossed her arms. Not defensively — the posture of a woman who'd been sitting in a parking lot for twenty minutes and was deciding whether the trip had been worth it. She studied Carmen with the expression of someone accustomed to explaining her situation to people who held some kind of authority over her life and rarely did anything with it.

"OK," Rosa said. She turned and opened the passenger door and pulled out a CVS bag. "I went this week. I do that sometimes — walk in, hand them the prescription, ask what it costs. Just to see." She pulled a folded piece of receipt paper from the bag. She'd creased it neatly, precisely, the fold of someone who kept track of numbers because the numbers kept track of her. "Hydroxychloroquine, two hundred milligrams, ninety count. Three eighty-nine at CVS. Three sixty-two at the Walgreens on Route 1." She put the receipt back in the bag. "Four twelve at the Rite Aid, but the Rite Aid on Cleveland closed, so."

"And from Marcus?"

"Forty dollars."

Carmen didn't say anything.

"A month," Rosa said. "Forty dollars a month for a ninety-day supply. And whoever's doing the packaging — a woman, Marcus won't tell me her name — she checks the expiration dates and puts a slip in the bag. Don't take with antacids. Space four hours from other meds." She paused. "My rheumatologist used to tell me the same thing. Back when I could see my rheumatologist."

They stood in the parking lot. A medical supply van was parked by the loading dock with its rear doors open. Two cars passed on Route 27. The wind moved a paper cup across the asphalt in a slow arc and left it against a curb.

"I was at a dental office in Woodbridge," Rosa said. "Receptionist. Twelve years. The dentist retired, and the new owner brought his own staff." She made a small motion with her shoulders — not quite a shrug, something more resigned and less performative. "I looked at COBRA. The plan was small-group, under twenty employees, so COBRA wasn't an option. I looked at the marketplace. Premiums were five-forty a month, and the deductible was three thousand before anything kicked in. I make — I was making sixteen an hour at a temp agency, and you can do the math on that faster than I can."

Carmen could do the math. Not Rosa's specific math, but the architecture of it — the corridor between making too much for Medicaid and too little for a plan that covered the medications you needed, the gap that swallowed people silently, without the drama of a crisis, just the steady arithmetic of not-enough compounding month after month. Carmen was thirty-one years old and an LPN and she was one lost job from the same corridor. She knew this because she'd sat at her own kitchen table after Reggie was asleep and run the same kind of numbers that all ended at the same wall.

"What happens when you don't take it?" Carmen asked.

Rosa looked at her. "You're a nurse. You know what happens."

"I'm asking you."

"Flares." Rosa held up her right hand. The knuckles were swollen, the skin over them tight and shiny with the specific texture of chronic synovial inflammation — not bruised, not injured, just perpetually wrong, the joints thickened by a disease that treated her own tissue as hostile. "Mostly joints. My knees, my hands. Last time I went without — this was before Marcus — I ran out for three weeks. Couldn't open a jar. Couldn't button my pants." She looked at her hand the way you'd look at a tool you'd been issued that no longer functioned properly. "I called in sick for a week at a job I'd just started. A warehouse, data entry for their inventory system. They let me go after day four. The temp agency stopped calling after that."

She said this without emphasis. Not performing her own suffering, not framing it as injustice, not asking for anything. It was a sequence of events that had happened to her body and then to her employment and then to her ability to pay for the medication that would have prevented the sequence in the first place. She described it the way you'd describe a car accident from a few years ago — something that rearranged things, that you stopped expecting sympathy for because sympathy didn't fill prescriptions.

"Can I see that one?" Carmen said, and nodded at Rosa's left hand, which she'd been holding against her vest pocket.

Rosa extended it. Carmen took it. The metacarpophalangeal joints were warm and boggy — the synovitis she'd studied in her pharmacology textbook and seen in clinical rotations and recognized now by touch the way she recognized a fever or an irregular pulse. She turned Rosa's hand gently, feeling the swelling over the ring finger knuckle, feeling the heat that meant the inflammation was active.

For a moment she was just a nurse holding a patient's hand in a parking lot, checking a joint. It was the most familiar thing she'd done in weeks. This was what she knew. Assess, observe, feel what was wrong and name it. The complications were elsewhere — in the TV room at one in the morning, in Agnes's notebooks, in the numbers she'd signed off on for the state surveyor. The hand was just a hand, and Carmen knew what to do with a hand.

"This one's getting worse," she said, pressing lightly on the ring finger joint.

"I know."

"You should get it imaged."

"With what?" Rosa said. Not angry. A real question with a real answer, and they both knew the answer was the same wall Carmen had seen from her own kitchen table: with what insurance, with what copay, with what deductible, with what money.

Carmen let go. Rosa put her hand back in her vest pocket.

"I'm not asking you for anything," Rosa said. "I get it — you've got your own situation. Your license, or whatever. The old man said you were worried. I understand being worried." She shifted her weight against the Civic. "But I'm telling you what I'm doing. I'm buying my medication from the only place I can afford it, and I'm taking it the way my doctor told me to take it, back when I could afford to see my doctor. That's what's happening. That's the whole thing."

She got back in the Civic. The engine turned over on the second try — a hesitation, then the catch, the sound of a car that started every morning but might not start forever. Carmen watched her pull out of the lot. Brake lights at the exit, a left signal, and the silver car merged into the afternoon traffic on Route 27 and was gone.

Carmen stood in the lot. Her shift started in seven minutes. Through the glass front doors she could see the lobby — the silk flowers on the reception desk that Cliff had put out for the survey and hadn't bothered to remove, the LAKEVIEW GARDENS sign in its welcoming font, the hand sanitizer dispenser mounted by the entrance. Tammy's Corolla was in the employee row. The medical supply van had pulled away from the loading dock, leaving tire marks on the pavement where it had idled too long.

She thought about Agnes's notebooks on the card table. The composition books with their ruled columns: diagnosis, dosage, quantity, interaction flags in red ink. She thought about the two-column comparison — facility cost on the left, uninsured retail cost on the right, the gap between them wide enough to swallow a woman who used to work a reception desk in Woodbridge for twelve years and now couldn't button her pants without a forty-dollar bag of pills from a cookie tin in a nursing home.

Three hundred and eighty-nine dollars versus forty. A ninety-day supply. The medication that let Rosa's hands work — that let her open jars and button clothes and type inventory data and do the ordinary mechanical things a body did when a body wasn't attacking itself. The difference between those numbers was being bridged by four residents and a fired CNA, using composition notebooks and a card table and a prepaid phone, operating between midnight and two in the morning in a room with a broken television and vinyl chairs. And the system that produced the surplus — the one that overprescribed because underprescribing cost staff time, that substituted generics without review, that billed Medicare for all of it and called it care — was the system Carmen worked inside, and was licensed by, and had lied to a state official to keep functioning, and could not, standing in this parking lot with the smell of asphalt and exhaust and the late-September air that couldn't decide if it was still summer, argue against.

She went inside. She clipped her badge to her scrub pocket — the small automatic gesture, badge to pocket, stethoscope check, hair smoothed back. The motions of becoming the nurse. She signed in at the desk, noted the time in the log, and walked toward the medication room to begin her shift.


# Chapter 11

The call bell from Room 116A went off at 3:07 AM. Carmen heard it from the medication room where she was recounting the shift's controlled substance log, trying to make the numbers come out the way Agnes had taught her to make them come out — carefully, with specific purpose, each discrepancy accounted for in the parallel ledger she now kept in her head.

She was in the hallway before the second chime. Lakeview at night was its own creature — half-lit corridors, the ventilation cycling down to save on electricity, every sound traveling farther than it should. Her sneakers on the linoleum. The distant hum of the ice machine. Room 110's soft continuous moan, which had become as ambient to her as highway noise on her drive home.

Room 116A. She pushed the door open.

Earl was standing between the two beds in his undershirt and pajama pants, barefoot on the cold linoleum, and he had one of the facility's thin white towels pressed against the side of Burt's head. His other hand was braced on the nightstand for balance. His breathing was heavy, wet, and the effort of getting out of bed and crossing three feet of floor had cost him something she could hear in the rattle of each inhale.

Burt was on the ground. He'd gone down between the bed and the nightstand — the gap was barely eighteen inches — and he was wedged there on his side, one arm pinned under him, the other reaching toward nothing. His legs were tangled in the sheet that had pulled off the bed with him. The bedside rail was down. It shouldn't have been down. She'd put it up at the start of her shift — she was almost sure she'd put it up — but the rails at Lakeview were old and the latches were loose and Burt moved in his sleep sometimes, reaching for things that weren't there anymore.

The blood was what she saw first and what she'd see when she closed her eyes for the next three nights. Scalp lacerations bled like nothing else, especially skin this thin, this old, the capillaries close to the surface and the tissue paper-fragile. Burt's white hair was dark and matted on the right side. Blood had pooled under his head on the linoleum, a small lake of it catching the light from the hallway, and there were smears on the nightstand's sharp laminate edge where he'd hit. The blood was thin and watery-looking, the way old blood was — less viscous, the body's production line running slower than its losses.

"He fell," Earl said.

Carmen was already on the floor next to Burt. "Mr. Kovacs. Burt. Can you hear me?" She kept her voice level, the nurse voice, the one that cost her effort to hold steady. Her hands did the work — penlight from her scrub pocket, left pupil sluggish, right pupil responsive but slow. She took the towel from Earl's hand and pressed it to the laceration, feeling the split in the skin through the terry cloth, two inches long above his right ear, deep enough that she could feel the hard ledge of skull underneath.

"How long ago?" she asked Earl.

"A few minutes. I heard him get up. Then—" He stopped. The cough came — deep, from his chest, the kind that folded him at the waist. He sat down on his own bed heavily and hacked into the crook of his elbow, and for a moment the only sounds in the room were Earl's coughing and Burt's breathing, which was shallow but regular, which was something.

"It's okay. You did right pressing the bell."

Burt's eyes were open but unfocused. His lips were moving. Carmen leaned closer. Hungarian — he was speaking Hungarian, the language that surfaced when the dementia peeled back the English like old wallpaper. *Erzsébet*, she heard, and then something else she couldn't parse, a long run of syllables that sounded like a question or a prayer. His wife's name, his wife's language, the one she'd used the dictionary to teach him before she died. Before her dictionary became something else entirely.

"Stay still, Mr. Kovacs. Don't try to move." She held pressure on the wound with one hand and pulled her phone from her scrub pocket with the other. 911. She gave the information in the clipped shorthand of someone who'd called ambulances from this building before: 88-year-old male, ground-level fall, scalp laceration approximately two inches right temporal region, altered mental status at baseline with acute change, history of vascular dementia, current medications including fentanyl patch 25 mcg transdermal and oxycodone 5 mg PRN. Possible concussion. She wanted c-spine precaution. She wanted it fast.

She hung up. The room was quiet except for Burt's murmuring and Earl's breathing and the tick of the wall clock that ran two minutes slow because nobody had fixed it in the fourteen months Carmen had worked here. Seven minutes until the ambulance, maybe ten. Central New Jersey at three in the morning, the roads would be empty.

She pulled a second towel from the stack on Earl's shelf and layered it over the first, which was soaked through. The blood was on her hands now, in the creases of her knuckles, under the nail she'd broken last week opening a medication blister pack. The overhead light was off — Earl and Burt slept with it off — and she was working by the light from the hallway and the green glow of the pulse oximeter on Burt's bedside table. She clipped the oximeter to his finger. Ninety-four percent. Low but not dangerous. His hand in hers was cool and slack, and his fingers — those long musician's fingers, the ones that had held a trumpet in a photograph she'd seen a hundred times — didn't grip back.

And then she saw the shelf.

It was behind her, mounted to the wall above the radiator on Burt's side of the room. Three items: a framed photograph of Burt with a woman who must have been Erzsébet — both younger, him holding a trumpet, her laughing at something beyond the camera's edge — a ceramic mug with the Berklee College of Music logo that held two pens and a broken watch, and the dictionary. Hungarian-English, hardcover, faded red cloth binding, spine cracked and reglued with tape that had yellowed to the color of old teeth. The book Erzsébet had given him so he could teach her his language while she taught him to think in hers. The book that, since Agnes had hollowed out the center with a box cutter and pharmaceutical patience, held sixty-three oxycodone tablets and forty alprazolam, wrapped in small ziplock bags that Agnes had labeled in her pharmacist's print.

Carmen looked at the dictionary on the shelf and her hands kept pressing the towel to Burt's head and her mind split into two tracks that ran simultaneously, that she would realize later had always been running simultaneously, that had started the night she walked Harry back to his room and didn't call the police. Track one: patient care, spinal precaution, pressure on the wound, monitor pupils and oxygen, wait for EMS. Track two: when the EMTs came they would assess the room. They might not search the shelf — they were here for the patient, not his belongings. But the hospital would inventory his personal items if they came with him. Or they'd leave the belongings and send a facilities report, and Cliff would dispatch someone to clean the blood off the linoleum, and that person might straighten the room, and if anyone picked up that dictionary and felt how wrong the weight was, how a book that should weigh a pound weighed three — if anyone opened it and saw the hollowed-out cavity packed with labeled bags of Schedule II opioids —

She moved without deciding to move. That was the thing she would think about later, in her car, in her apartment, in the shower with the water too hot — there was no moment of decision, no internal argument, no weighing of consequences. She stood up, keeping one hand on the towel against Burt's scalp, and reached back with the other hand and took the dictionary off the shelf. It was heavier than a book that size should be. She could feel the pills shift inside, the soft rattle of plastic against hollowed-out pages, the weight of a hundred and three tablets that represented roughly eighteen hundred dollars in street value and a federal possession-with-intent charge that would end her career, her freedom, and the life she'd built for Reggie.

"Earl."

He looked at her. He looked at the dictionary in her hand. His face didn't change — not because he was hiding his reaction but because his face had already settled, minutes ago, into the expression of a man who understood what was at stake and had decided to let someone else handle it. He'd been a union organizer. He knew when to act and when to let the person with standing take the floor.

"Keep pressure here," she said. She guided his hand to the towel, repositioning it over the laceration. "Press hard. Don't let go until I come back."

Earl's large hand replaced hers. He pressed. His nails were trimmed short and clean — Tammy did that for him, every Tuesday, with the same clippers she used for three other residents — and there was blood between his fingers now, his roommate's blood, and his hand didn't shake.

Carmen stood with the dictionary against her hip and walked out of Room 116A. Left down the hall, past the darkened rooms where people slept or didn't, past the sub-station where her charting waited half-finished on the desk, past the bathroom where she'd once seen Harry walking at 2:40 AM carrying something in a towel, and into the supply closet at the far end of Wing A.

The closet smelled like industrial bleach and the synthetic lavender of a plug-in air freshener that someone had wedged behind the toilet paper rolls. Mops in a bucket. Cases of latex gloves stacked to her shoulder. The shelf above her head, behind a case of adult incontinence pads — large, overnight, the brand Lakeview bought in bulk because it was cheapest and leaked by 4 AM. Carmen put the dictionary on the shelf, spine facing the wall, and closed the closet door.

She stood in the hallway. Her right hand was tacky with Burt's blood, already cooling and stiffening in the creases of her palm. The fluorescent above her head flickered with its perpetual tick-tick-tick, the dying ballast that maintenance had been promising to replace since August. Down the hall, she could hear Earl coughing.

She walked back to 116A.

The EMTs arrived eight minutes later — two men, young, efficient, moving through the room with the quiet competence of people who did this three times a shift and would do it again before dawn. They stabilized Burt's cervical spine, assessed his Glasgow score at 11, dressed the laceration with hemostatic gauze and a compression bandage, started an IV line in a vein so thin and fragile the younger EMT needed two attempts. Burt kept speaking Hungarian. One of them asked Carmen what he was saying. She said she didn't know. She knew one word — the name he kept repeating, soft and broken, the same syllables over and over — but that wasn't theirs.

They loaded him onto the stretcher and took him out through the side entrance. Carmen followed to the ambulance bay and watched the doors close. The October air was cold and damp, the smell of wet asphalt and dead leaves and the exhaust from the ambulance as it pulled away, lights on, no siren. No siren meant they didn't think he was dying. Just an 88-year-old man with a bleeding head and a disease that was taking him apart one synapse at a time, which didn't qualify as an emergency because it happened every day in every facility like this one, across every state, a slow-motion catastrophe that the system had learned to process as routine.

She went back inside. She washed her hands in the medication room sink — the water running pink, then diluted pink, then clear, the institutional soap leaving her skin tight and dry. She pulled up Burt's chart on the computer and began the incident report. Date: October 3, 2026. Time: 0307. Location: Room 116A, between bed and nightstand. Description of event: *Resident found on floor with scalp laceration, approximately 2-inch, right temporal region. Bedside rail found in down position. Roommate (E. Washington) reports hearing fall, pressed call bell. Nurse responded, applied pressure to wound, assessed LOC and vitals, called 911. EMS arrived 0315, patient transported to Saint Peter's University Hospital for evaluation. Family notification per protocol.*

Her handwriting was steady. She documented everything she was supposed to document.

She didn't mention the dictionary.

---

Burt came back two days later in a medical transport van, belted into a wheelchair he didn't try to get out of. Carmen was starting her shift when the van pulled up to the side entrance. She signed the transfer paperwork from Saint Peter's — concussion with post-concussive syndrome, six sutures to the scalp laceration, CT scan showing no acute subdural hematoma but noting diffuse cortical atrophy consistent with moderate-to-severe vascular dementia, recommend follow-up with neurology in two weeks — and walked alongside the wheelchair as the transport aide rolled him to 116A.

He was lighter. Not his weight, though that had dropped too — the hospital discharge summary listed 142 pounds, down from the 151 in his Lakeview chart from three weeks ago, nine pounds lost in two days of hospital food he hadn't eaten and IV fluids that weren't the same. She meant something else. The volume of him. The presence. Before the fall, Burt had been half-present — his dementia was real, had always been real, the only one in the ring who wasn't performing — but there had been something still alive in the confusion. He told stories about the Blue Note, about playing trumpet in clubs on Fifty-Second Street in a decade he might or might not have actually worked. The stories looped and stalled and fused with other stories, but they had heat. They came from a place in him that still had opinions about the world. He'd hum — standards, mostly. She'd recognized "Round Midnight" once, and fragments of "In a Sentimental Mood," melodies that surfaced and sank like things seen through water.

He wasn't humming. He sat in the wheelchair with his hands in his lap, those long fingers knotted and liver-spotted, trembling with a fine tremor that was new or that she hadn't noticed before. The sutures on his scalp were covered by a gauze pad taped to his thin white hair. His eyes were open and aimed at the hallway but they weren't tracking anything. They were receiving light the way a window receives light — without processing, without response.

She got him into bed. He didn't resist. Before the fall he had resisted — small, wordless protests, pulling his arm back when she reached for the blood pressure cuff, turning his face from the thermometer, the stubborn physical vocabulary of a person who still had preferences about what was done to his body. Now she lifted his arm and it stayed where she put it. She wrapped the cuff. 128 over 76. Pulse 68. She checked the suture site — dry, no infection, the wound healing the slow grudging way old skin healed, as if the body resented the effort. She changed his fentanyl patch, peeling the old one from his shoulder and pressing the new one to his upper arm, and he didn't flinch, didn't try to bat her hand away the way he used to.

Earl watched from his bed. He had a Sports Illustrated open on his lap — the same issue from three weeks ago, the one that hid the paperback he was actually reading — but he wasn't reading either one. He was watching Carmen check Burt, and his face had the expression she'd seen in photographs of men at union halls: someone counting costs and finding the total higher than the estimate.

"He's settled," she told Earl. "I'll check on him through the shift."

Earl nodded. He looked at Burt, then looked away. Then looked back. It was the looking-back that got her — the way he couldn't leave it alone, the way his eyes kept returning to his roommate the way your tongue keeps finding a broken tooth.

Carmen finished the intake assessment. Under cognitive status she wrote: *Decline noted from baseline. Reduced verbal output. Decreased resistance to care activities. Diminished environmental awareness and engagement. Pattern consistent with post-concussive acceleration of existing dementia. Will monitor and update physician at next rounds.*

The words were clinical and precise and they described a man who was disappearing. She could document the disappearance in the language of assessment and monitoring. She could chart it. She could not stop it.

She left the room. The shelf above the radiator on Burt's side was missing one item. The photograph was there — Burt and Erzsébet, the trumpet, the laugh. The Berklee mug was there with its two pens and broken watch. The space where the dictionary had sat was a rectangle of slightly lighter paint where the wall hadn't faded.

No one had asked about it.

---

The ring met at one in the morning on a Thursday, five days after Burt came back. Carmen had started attending meetings after the survey — not every one, but enough that her arrival no longer produced the careful silence it had the first two times. She came because she needed to know what they were planning, and she stayed because this room at this hour was the only place where everything she knew was allowed to be real.

The TV room was the same as always: vinyl chairs in a loose half-circle, the wall-mounted TV dark, the card table with one short leg propped on a folded napkin. Harry preferred the brass reading lamp that Lola had taken from the nurses' break room months ago — it threw warm, limited light that made the room smaller, almost domestic. Like a kitchen. Like somewhere people actually lived. Outside the windows, the parking lot was empty under its orange lights and the October wind pushed leaves across the asphalt in fitful scrapes.

Harry was in his chair, the one nearest the lamp that no one else used. His faded blue cardigan was buttoned one off from the top, and either he hadn't noticed or it didn't matter enough to fix. He looked old tonight. Not the performed frailty he assembled for the day staff and the surveyors — the slack posture, the unfocused eyes, the careful theater of diminishment. Just old. The skin under his eyes was bruised with sleeplessness, and his hands rested on the chair's armrests without moving, which meant he was working something out that he hadn't solved.

Agnes sat across from him, back straight, wire-rimmed glasses reflecting the lamp. She had nothing in front of her — no notebook, no pillbox organizer. The operation had pulled inward since the fall, contracting the way a hand closes around an injury, and the meetings had shrunk with it. Less inventory to review. Less logistics to manage. More silence to fill.

Lola was by the door, her usual post, though her lookout role had relaxed since Carmen had started sending Devon to take his breaks at the other wing's station. She held a coffee cup in both hands, the tremor in her left making the surface of the coffee shiver against the ceramic. She was watching Harry's face the way she watched everything — through the performance of not watching, her eyes settling elsewhere while her attention stayed fixed.

Earl had come from 116A, where Burt was asleep. He took the chair he always took, the one farthest from the center, closest to the door. He'd brought nothing. He never brought anything to meetings anymore — before, he'd carried the cash envelopes, or the count from his side of the logistics, but that had stopped after the fall. The operation had contracted and Earl had contracted with it, pulling his participation inward the way he pulled everything inward.

"We need to talk about Burt," Harry said.

Nobody answered right away. Lola drank her coffee. Agnes adjusted her glasses with one finger at the bridge. Earl's silence was the same silence he always produced, but Carmen noticed it was aimed differently tonight — toward the empty space to Harry's left where Burt's wheelchair had been parked at every meeting since the ring began, where Earl used to push him in and set the brake and check that the blanket was tucked around his legs.

"He's still prescribed," Harry said. "Oxycodone five, ninety count, refilled last week. Agnes already flagged it for the rotation."

"I flagged it because that is my job," Agnes said. "That does not mean I flagged it for use."

Harry looked at her. "His prescriptions are the highest-value inventory we move. Without the oxy, we're short about eighteen hundred a month."

"He does not know what month it is."

"He didn't know what month it was six months ago either."

"Six months ago he could tell you he wanted to be part of this. He said yes. He understood what yes meant."

Carmen sat in the chair between Lola and Earl, the seat that had become hers by proximity and repetition. She was still in her scrubs from the shift she'd clocked out of two hours ago. Staying late had become its own small deception — the dedicated nurse, the one who cared enough to linger — and she was tired of the irony without being willing to give it up. She listened.

Harry leaned forward. The chair creaked. "We all agreed when we started this. Every one of us. Burt included. He said—"

"He said yes to a man he trusted, in a room he recognized, on a night when he knew his own name." Agnes's voice hadn't risen, but it had compressed — more facts per sentence, less space between them, the pharmacist under pressure accelerating instead of getting louder. Her hands were moving on the armrests of her chair, her fingers sorting nothing, organizing invisible inventory the way they'd organized pills for forty-two years. "He cannot say yes now. He cannot say no. He cannot produce anything that constitutes informed consent, and you know that, Harry."

"He's not being harmed by it," Harry said. "His pain management is the fentanyl patch. The oral oxy sits in his drawer untouched. We take pills he doesn't use and wouldn't know were gone."

"That is not the standard and you know it."

"What standard, Agnes? We're running a pill operation out of a nursing home. There's no ethics board."

"The standard," Agnes said, and her hands stopped moving, "is that you do not distribute a patient's medications without that patient's knowledge or agreement. I do not care that there is no board. I was the board for forty-two years. A patient with diminished capacity does not get spoken for. Not by his family. Not by his doctor. Not by us."

Lola set her coffee on the card table. The ceramic made a small sound against the wood grain. "He's the same Burt," she said. "He's just — he's tired, that's all, mijo. He was always a little tired."

"He is not the same," Agnes said. "You want him to be. He is not."

Lola's hand went to the crucifix at her throat, her thumb finding the small gold cross and rubbing it the way she did when something landed that she couldn't redirect with warmth or a story. She didn't answer. Her left hand trembled around the coffee cup.

Harry turned to Earl. "You live with him. You see him every day. What do you think?"

Earl looked at Harry. The room waited. Earl's silences were never empty — they had the specific weight of a man who'd spent thirty years in negotiation rooms and on picket lines, learning that the moment you spoke mattered as much as what you said. Carmen could hear the clock in the hallway. The lamp buzzing. Her own breathing.

Earl cleared his throat. The wet sound underneath, the rattle she'd been hearing more of and that he kept turning away to hide.

"He fell," Earl said. "He was on the floor bleeding, and he was calling her name, and he didn't know where he was."

"That's not—" Harry started.

"He was calling her name and he didn't know where he was." Earl said it again. Not louder. The same words, the same flat delivery, but the repetition itself was the emphasis — Earl, who said things once and expected them to land, saying it twice because the room needed to hear it twice.

They sat with that. The reading lamp hummed. Outside, the wind picked up and a branch or a piece of loose gutter scraped across the window glass with a sound like fingernails on a chalkboard.

Carmen looked at her hands. The blood was gone — she'd washed it off the night of the fall and twice more in the days since, the third time in the shower at home with water hot enough to redden her forearms, scrubbing at the cuticles with a nail brush until her skin stung. But she could still feel the texture of it, the tacky cooling weight in the creases of her knuckles, the way it had dried to a film before she made it to the medication room sink. She thought about the dictionary on the top shelf of the supply closet, wedged behind a case of incontinence pads. She hadn't moved it. She hadn't returned it. She hadn't told anyone she had it, though Earl had watched her take it and she assumed he'd told Harry, and she assumed Harry had told Agnes, and the fact that no one had asked her about it — not where it was, not what she'd done with it — meant they already understood what she'd done and what it meant. She'd hidden evidence of a federal crime. She'd tampered with what was technically a fall scene. She'd made a choice that couldn't be undone, and she'd made it without choosing, which was worse, or maybe more honest, than anything she could have deliberated her way into.

"He'd want us to keep going."

She said it before she felt herself forming the words. They arrived in the room finished, with a certainty she hadn't felt building. She was looking at Earl when she said it, or maybe at the empty space where Burt's wheelchair should have been, the gap in the circle that made the room feel lopsided and wrong, and her voice was the steady one, the one she used on the phone with 911 or explaining a care plan to a family member — the professional register that didn't admit uncertainty.

Harry looked at her.

Agnes looked at her.

*Us.* She heard it a half-second after she'd said it, the way you hear your own voice on a recording and don't recognize it as yours. The pronoun she'd never used before. Not *them*. Not *the operation* or *your thing* or *what you do.* Us. She'd placed herself inside the circle, inside the ring, inside the crime, and she hadn't even hesitated. The word had just been there, waiting for a sentence that needed it, waiting for a moment when the argument was happening and her body — her mouth, her lungs, the voice she used without thinking — understood what her thinking self hadn't caught up to yet. She was one of them. She'd been one of them since the supply closet, or since the surveyor's interview, or since the night she saw Harry walking and decided to talk to him instead of calling the police. The word didn't make her one of them. It just named what was already true.

Nobody pointed it out. Nobody said *Did you hear what you just said?* or *So you're admitting it now?* and the absence of comment was what made it irreversible — if Harry had said *You said 'us,'* she could have corrected herself, retreated into the observer role, claimed she misspoke. But the room just absorbed it the way the room absorbed everything, the way these people had been absorbing risk and silence and the slow accumulation of complicity for eighteen months, and Carmen's pronoun settled into the air like smoke and became part of what they breathed.

Agnes was still looking at her. "That is not an argument," she said. "That is a guess about what a man who cannot speak for himself would want. I spent three years being spoken for. My daughter decided what I needed. The facility agreed. Nobody asked me." She paused. Her hands were still now, completely still, which was how Agnes looked when she'd arrived at something she wouldn't move from. "I will not do that to someone else."

It landed. Carmen felt it land — not as a rebuke but as something harder, more structural. Agnes wasn't being sentimental about Burt. Agnes was being precise about a principle that had shaped her life. Someone who loves you deciding what you need without asking — that was what Helen had done, what the system had endorsed, what had put Agnes in this chair in this room in this building. The same logic Harry was using now. *We know what Burt would want. He'd agree if he could.* Agnes had heard that logic from the other side. She'd been the Burt in someone else's argument, and she would not participate in doing it to him.

"So what do we do," Carmen said. "His prescriptions. The rotation."

"We pull his inventory from the active list," Agnes said. "His medications are dispensed to him and administered as prescribed. Every tablet. We do not divert them."

"That cuts into—" Harry started.

"I know what it cuts." Agnes looked at him. Not with anger. With the flat, final certainty of a pharmacist who had refused to fill a contraindicated prescription once and would do it again. "His prescriptions stay with him."

Harry sat back. He looked at the ceiling, the water-stained acoustic tiles that were Lakeview's idea of decor, and Carmen could see him running the calculation behind his eyes — the margins, the monthly take, the spreadsheet that lived in his accountant's brain and updated itself in real time. Without Burt's oxycodone, the ring's most valuable line item dropped to zero. The operation would still function, but it was smaller. It was pulling inward, losing capacity the way its members were losing capacity, and there was nothing Harry could do about it because the thing that was taking Burt wasn't an adversary he could outmaneuver or a problem he could solve with better numbers. It was time. It was biology. It was the one variable that didn't respond to planning.

"Fine," Harry said. The word wasn't agreement. It was concession — the sound of a man who had run the numbers and found them unanswerable. He sat in his chair and looked at the space where Burt's wheelchair used to be and said nothing else.

Lola stood up. Her coffee was cold and she'd barely touched it. "I'll look in on him," she said, and Carmen heard in her voice the thing Lola did when something hurt — she reached for an action, for a kindness she could perform with her body instead of sitting with a feeling she couldn't fix.

"You don't have to," Carmen said.

"I know, mija."

Lola left. Her footsteps were soft in the hallway, the shuffle that was part performance and part the Parkinson's and part seventy-nine years of weight on the same pair of feet. Carmen listened until the sound blended into the building's nighttime hum and disappeared.

Earl stood. He didn't say goodnight or good morning or anything at all. He walked out of the TV room and back toward 116A, toward his bed three feet from Burt's bed, toward the roommate whose breathing he'd learned well enough that he'd woken up when it changed, when the body hit the floor, when the sound went wrong. Earl had gotten out of bed and crossed the room and grabbed a towel and pressed it to the wound and pressed the call bell with his other hand, and that wasn't the ring's logistics man protecting an asset. That was a man taking care of the person next to him because there was no one else awake to do it.

Carmen and Harry and Agnes sat in the TV room. The lamp buzzed. The wind had died and the parking lot was still and the leaves had settled wherever the last gust had pushed them.

"You said 'us,'" Harry said. Quietly. Not as leverage, not as a weapon he'd hold and use later. Just the observation of a man who counted everything — pills, dollars, margins, risks — and who had counted, too, the moment the last holdout stopped holding out.

Carmen looked at him. "Yeah," she said. "I did."

She didn't take it back.


# Chapter 12

The word she was looking for was aluminum.

Not an important word. She'd been trying to describe the foil over the breakfast tray to her roommate Margaret, who couldn't hear her and wouldn't have cared, and the word had gone missing somewhere between her brain and her mouth. She'd stood there with her hand out, miming the act of peeling back the foil, and what came out was "the — the shiny. The paper that's not paper."

Margaret hadn't looked up from her oatmeal.

Lola sat on her bed after breakfast and tried the word again. Aluminum. There it was. Back in place, as if it had never left, and she couldn't tell — this was the thing, this was the thing that woke her up some nights with her heart fast under her nightgown — she couldn't tell if she'd lost it because she'd been performing confusion for fourteen months and the act had gotten into her, worn a groove, or because she was seventy-nine years old with Parkinson's and sometimes seventy-nine-year-old women with Parkinson's forgot words. Both were true. That was the problem. Both were true and she couldn't tell which one was happening at any given moment, and there was no test for it, no blood draw or brain scan that would come back and say this forgetting is real and this one is theater and this one, Dolores, this one we're not sure about.

She held her left hand up in front of her face. The tremor was there, as it always was — a fine, persistent shaking, worse in the mornings before her carbidopa-levodopa kicked in, worse when she was tired, worse when she was afraid. Right now it was moderate. A ripple, not a quake. She watched it and felt nothing useful. You couldn't argue with a tremor. You couldn't ask it to explain itself. It was just there, the way weather was there, and the medication helped and didn't help, and some mornings she could button her blouse on the first try and some mornings the buttons won and she asked Tammy for help with the same flat acceptance she'd learned to apply to everything her body did without her permission.

Her roommate Margaret — Margaret Oates, eighty-six, severe vascular dementia, no family visitors in the eleven months Lola had shared this room with her — was being wheeled to physical therapy by an aide Lola didn't recognize. A new one. They came and went so fast that Lola had stopped learning their names, which felt like its own small cruelty but was also just practical. You couldn't mourn everyone who left.

She got up and walked to the window. October had arrived while she wasn't looking, the way it did, and the three trees in the courtyard had started to turn — though "turn" was generous. They were mostly just giving up, dropping leaves in patchy clumps onto the pavement below. Beyond them, the parking lot, half-full at this hour. Beyond that, Route 27. If she pressed her face to the glass she could see the Dunkin' Donuts sign, which she did sometimes, not because she wanted a donut but because the sign was bright and specific and it reminded her that there was a world out there that did things she no longer did, like drive and buy coffee and stand in a line and choose.

She was supposed to be confused right now. That was her job, her contribution: the lookout, the smokescreen, the warm old woman who distracted nurses and visitors with stories while the real work happened behind her. In the hallways, during meals, during activities, when the aides came in, when Dr. Prasad made her biweekly rounds — Lola Fernandez was chatty, affectionate, mildly disoriented, calling everyone mijo or mija, telling stories that went in circles, asking what day it was three times in a conversation. The warmth was real. The stories were real, most of them. The confusion was manufactured, and it had been easy at first. Fun, even. She'd been a teacher for thirty-one years, fourth grade, and every day in a fourth-grade classroom was a performance of patience and enthusiasm that sometimes became real by third period. Playing confused was simpler than that. You just let the words tangle, let the sentences loop, let your eyes go soft when someone asked you a direct question. You became a version of yourself that people already expected a seventy-nine-year-old woman in a nursing home to be.

But fourteen months of playing confused had done something she hadn't anticipated. The confusion had weight. It pressed down on her actual mind the way water pressed on a sponge — slowly, invisibly, until the sponge was heavy and couldn't remember being dry. She forgot words now. Not performing it, not choosing to stumble for the benefit of whoever was watching, but actually reaching for a word and finding the shelf bare. Aluminum. Thermometer, last week — she'd been trying to tell Carmen her room was cold and she'd said "the thing, the thing that measures" and Carmen had looked at her with that careful nurse's attention and Lola had smiled through it, had made it part of the act, but alone afterward she'd sat on this bed and said thermometer six times fast with her hands balled in her lap. Carnation, the week before that, and that one had frightened her, because Tomás used to bring her carnations, cheap ones from the bodega on Bergenline Avenue, and if she was losing the word for the flower her husband brought her then she was losing a piece of him, and she had so few pieces left.

She thought about Burt. She didn't want to but she did. Burt had fallen two weeks ago and come back from the hospital worse, quieter, the confusion in his eyes no longer intermittent but settled, and watching him — watching a man whose dementia had been real all along, real in the way hers wasn't, or hadn't been, or might be becoming — had put a sound in her ears she couldn't shake. A kind of hum. Not a sound anyone else could hear. Just the low, steady vibration of a future she was walking toward whether she wanted to or not, a future where the word aluminum didn't come back, where thermometer stayed gone, where the act and the woman performing it merged into one thing and nobody, including her, could tell the difference.

Tomás would have said something about this. Something practical, something slightly impatient, because Tomás had not been a gentle man about worry. He'd been gentle about other things — he could cup a baby bird in his hands with a softness that made you forget those same hands poured concrete for twenty years — but worry made him restless, made him want to fix. "Lola, stop," he would have said. "Either it's happening or it's not, and sitting there scaring yourself doesn't change which one it is."

He was right. He was always right about that kind of thing, and wrong about everything else.

She sat on the bed and let herself think about him, the way she did most mornings. Not a prayer exactly. A visit. She talked to Tomás in her head — not as delusion, nothing like that, but as habit, the way you talked to someone you'd shared a bed with for forty-three years. The conversation was one-sided and it always had been, because even alive Tomás hadn't been a big talker, and dead he was even less so.

He'd died of pancreatic cancer ten years ago, and he had not gone peacefully. That was the thing about the euphemisms people reached for — passed away, went gently, is in a better place — they were engineering projects, little bridges built over something people didn't want to look down at. What was underneath: Tomás in a hospice bed in their apartment in Union City, a bed the insurance company had delivered with more efficiency than they'd ever shown for his actual treatment, and three months of dying that he'd spent furious. Not sad. Not resigned. Not at-peace-with-God. Furious. He'd cursed the nurses who came to change his IV. He'd cursed the hospice chaplain, a kind young woman named Sister Beth who'd tried to talk to him about acceptance, and Tomás had told her to take her acceptance and get out of his house. He meant it. He was dying at sixty-eight and he had poured concrete and raised two children and paid his taxes and fixed the sink when it leaked and gone to Mass when Lola asked him to and now his pancreas was eating itself alive and no one could tell him why.

The last two weeks, the morphine drip ran almost constantly and he was in and out, and the in was worse than the out because the in meant he knew where he was and what was happening to him. He'd lost forty pounds. His skin had the color and texture of wet newspaper. He vomited bile into a plastic basin because his stomach couldn't hold anything, not even water, not even the ice chips Lola put on his lips with a spoon. The morphine constipated him so badly that she gave him suppositories, which she did without complaint, without performance, because she'd also wiped his back when he sweated through his shirts at night and held his penis when he couldn't aim at the urinal anymore, and she'd done all of it because that was what forty-three years of marriage actually meant. Not the vows. The vows didn't know to include any of this.

He'd died at 4:17 on a Tuesday morning in January. She'd been sitting next to the bed, not sleeping, her hand on his arm. His breathing had changed — the rattling, the gaps that got longer — and then it stopped, and the room went quiet in a way rooms with living people in them never were, and she'd sat there for a long time before she picked up the phone. Those minutes were the last minutes she'd ever be in a room with Tomás. Once she called the hospice number, the machinery of death would start — the funeral home, the insurance paperwork, the obituary, the calls to Roberto and Miriam, the casseroles from neighbors who meant well and didn't know what else to do — and she wasn't ready for that. She wasn't ready for the shift from wife to widow, the last role change she'd ever have to make, or so she'd thought at the time.

She'd been wrong about that. She'd made one more: from widow to patient. And one more after that: from patient to lookout, to performer, to a woman who watched through a mask of confusion while pills moved through cookie tins and envelopes of cash went into the pockets of a man everyone believed was her nephew.

Tomás, she thought. Amor. What would you say about all this.

She knew what he'd say. He'd say: "Are you eating? Is anyone hurting you? Then do what you need to do and stop worrying." That was Tomás. He operated from a simple moral architecture: are you fed, are you safe, is someone being cruel. Everything else was negotiable.

Her son Roberto called every Sunday from Chicago. The calls lasted about twelve minutes. She'd timed one once, not to catch him, just out of the bleak curiosity that came from having too much time and not enough to spend it on. Twelve minutes covered his wife Elena, the kids, work, the weather in Chicago, and "how are you doing, Mamá?" She always said fine. She performed mild confusion on these calls now, because Roberto talked to the staff, and if Lola was lucid and articulate on the phone but drifting in the building, someone would notice. So she let her sentences circle. She asked about the grandchildren and then asked again five minutes later. Roberto's voice when she did this — patient, a little too loud, careful — sounded like the voice Helen Chen used when she visited Agnes, and that comparison made Lola's stomach turn.

Her daughter Miriam sent cards from Orlando. Birthday, Christmas, Mother's Day, and random Hallmark cards with flowers on the front and messages like "Thinking of you today!" The exclamation point always felt wrong to Lola, something pushed when it should be quiet. If you were thinking of someone, you could come. But Miriam had two kids and a husband who traveled for work and a mortgage she'd mentioned twice with the specific worry that meant it was too much, and Orlando was far, and Lola didn't blame her. She didn't blame either of them. They were living the lives America demanded of children — spread out, working, building families in whatever city had jobs — and the alternative was guilt, which helped no one and which Lola had seen do terrible things to other families in this building. The daughter in Hackensack who visited every day and cried every time, until the resident — a man named Phil who had Lewy body dementia and didn't know anyone's name — started getting agitated whenever she showed up. The son who came once a month from Pennsylvania and spent the entire visit staring at his phone. Guilt made people stupid. Lola preferred the cards.

She touched the crucifix at her throat. The gold was warm from her skin. She didn't pray — she hadn't prayed since Tomás died, because she had some things to say to God and none of them were requests — but the crucifix was her mother's, and touching it was a way of touching her mother, dead longer than Tomás and missed in a way that lived in Lola's hands, in the specific shape her fingers made when they closed around the small gold cross.

Ay, Dolores. What are you doing.

She said it to herself the way her mother would have. Not with judgment. With tired wonder.

---

Tammy found her in the courtyard at two o'clock, which was how most of their afternoons together started — not by plan but by routine, by the way two people who lived in the same small world eventually fell into rhythm without deciding to. Tammy's break was 1:45 to 2:15, and Lola was usually out here by one-thirty because the room was too quiet after Margaret was wheeled back from therapy and fell asleep, and the courtyard, even with its scraggly trees and its view of the parking lot, was outside. Outside still counted for something.

"Move over," Tammy said, and sat down on the bench without waiting for Lola to shift. She was wearing her teal scrubs, the ones with the bleach stain on the left thigh that she'd given up trying to hide, and her hair was in a knot that looked like it had been assembled in the dark, which it probably had been — Tammy's studio apartment, the one she'd moved into after the divorce, had one overhead light and a window that faced a wall. Lola knew this because Tammy had described it three times, each time in the same flat way, as if bad lighting were just another item on a list of things that had gone wrong and she didn't have the energy to rank them anymore.

"Coffee?" Tammy held up two Styrofoam cups from the staff kitchen. The coffee at Lakeview was terrible — thin, vaguely brown, produced by a machine nobody had cleaned since the previous administrator — but Tammy always brought two cups, and she always got it right. Two sugars, milk not cream. It was the kind of thing that shouldn't matter and that mattered more than almost anything.

"Gracias, mija." Lola took the cup with both hands, partly because of the tremor and partly because the warmth felt good. The October air had an edge to it now, a sharpness that meant summer had stopped pretending. Two of the courtyard trees had gone mostly bare, the third holding on to its leaves in scattered orange patches, stubborn or just slow. Leaves littered the pavement around the benches, dry and curling.

They sat. The courtyard was empty except for them and a squirrel working the base of the middle tree, nosing through the fallen leaves for something edible. Lola watched it with a recognition she didn't examine.

"So," Tammy said.

"So."

Tammy drank her coffee and made the face she always made, the one that said this is undrinkable and she was going to finish every drop. "Greg's got a girlfriend."

Lola didn't look at her. She knew from fourteen months of performed inattention, from a lifetime of reading rooms, that Tammy needed her to not make this a moment. "Mm."

"Yeah. Brandy. Her name's Brandy, like the — yeah." Tammy's voice was flat in the way that meant she was working hard to keep it flat. "She's twenty-eight. Works at a Verizon store in Woodbridge."

"Ay."

"It's fine. It's been eight months. It's whatever."

It was not fine and not whatever and they both knew it. Lola could hear the specific frequency of Tammy's not-fine, the way her consonants sharpened and the space between words stretched, as if she were testing each one's ability to hold her weight before she set it down. Tammy's divorce had surfaced over these courtyard afternoons in pieces, not chronologically, not as a story she was telling, but the way someone emptied a bag onto a table: a detail here, a fact there, scattered and unsorted. Greg had left in March. He'd been seeing someone before that — not Brandy, someone else, a woman from his gym whose name Tammy had never said out loud, which told Lola more than the name would have. Papers were final in July. Tammy had moved into the studio in Perth Amboy with her cat, who'd immediately peed on the mattress. She'd replaced the mattress, not the cat. That detail had been delivered with the particular deadpan that meant Tammy was choosing between laughing and something worse.

"Twenty-eight," Tammy said again, looking at the squirrel instead of Lola. "I'm forty-three and he picks a girl who wasn't born when I started working here."

Lola wanted to say something real. The want was physical, pressing at her sternum like a cough she couldn't release. She wanted to drop the mask and say: Mija, I watched my husband die in our apartment. He cursed the chaplain and I wiped bile off his chin and I gave him suppositories because nobody else was going to and we didn't talk about dignity because dignity was a word for people who hadn't had to do those things yet. And twenty-eight-year-old Brandy from Verizon is not going to do that for Greg when the time comes, and the time comes for everybody. She wanted to say: your ex-husband is an ordinary man doing an ordinary stupid thing and it'll hurt you until one day it won't and I can't tell you when that day is because grief doesn't keep a schedule, not even the grief for a man who's still alive and just chose someone else.

But she was Lola Fernandez, room 112A. Warm, confused, mildly disoriented. And Tammy was the person who brushed her hair on the mornings the tremor made the brush jump and skitter in her grip. Tammy was the one who knew about the two sugars and the milk. If Lola spoke too clearly, too specifically, Tammy would notice. Tammy was tired, not stupid. She noticed things. And noticing would lead to questions, and questions would lead to the ring, and the ring would lead to Harry and Agnes and Earl and Carmen and Marcus and Burt, and Lola would be the one who'd unraveled everything because she couldn't stand being lied to about coffee by a person she was lying to about everything else.

So she said: "Men." She said it with a wave of her hand, a puff of air, the way her performed self would — dismissing the whole gender as a weather pattern. "Tomás — my husband — he forgot our anniversary once. Thirty years, mija. Thirty years married and he forgot. Brought flowers the next day like that fixed it." She let the story ramble, let it loop the way her cover required, and the story was true — Tomás had forgotten their thirtieth, and the flowers had been carnations from the bodega, and she'd forgiven him before he walked through the door because forgiveness wasn't something she'd ever had to decide about with Tomás, it was just the climate they lived in — but the truth of the story was separate from its job here, which was to be harmless, to fill space, to be the kind of thing a confused old woman said to a younger woman's pain.

"Greg never forgot an anniversary," Tammy said. "He just forgot to give a shit about the other three hundred and sixty-four days."

"Mm."

"Sorry. Language."

"Mija, I've heard worse." She had. She'd heard Tomás curse God and a chaplain and the pharmaceutical company that made the morphine that wasn't strong enough and the insurance adjuster who'd denied the home health aide request two weeks before he died.

"Yeah." Tammy stretched her legs out. Her shoes were the white nursing clogs that all the CNAs wore, scuffed gray on the sides and collapsed at the heels from the miles she logged every shift — the steps between rooms, the laps around the wing, the back-and-forth to the supply closet for gloves and wipes and the incontinence briefs they went through forty of per day. Lola looked at those shoes and saw fifteen years of work that nobody wanted to discuss over dinner. "She posted pictures on Facebook. Her and Greg at some restaurant with candles. On the table. Candles." Tammy's voice thinned on the word. "We ate off paper plates because I was too tired to wash dishes after a twelve-hour."

"I'm sorry, mija."

"Don't be. I'm just — it's just stupid. The whole thing's stupid."
