Lola felt the guilt arrive the way it always did when Tammy told her something real — not as a pang or a wave but as a weight, a physical addition to what she was already carrying, settling in next to the grief and the tremor and the fear about the words she kept losing. She was deceiving this woman. Every afternoon, every cup of coffee, every time Tammy sat on this bench and said things that mattered to her, Lola was receiving them through a screen of performed confusion, and Tammy didn't know. Tammy brought her coffee and brushed her hair and told her about Greg and the apartment and the bad lighting and the cat, and Lola took all of it and gave back a character. A warm character. A character who cared, because Lola did care, that was the terrible part — the caring was real and the person doing the caring was a fraud, and those two facts existed in the same body and she couldn't separate them any more than she could separate the performance tremor from the Parkinson's tremor.

The coffee shook in her left hand. She put the cup down on the bench between them.

"You know what the worst part is?" Tammy said.

"Tell me."

"I miss him." She said it the way someone confessed to a crime they were ashamed of. Not the volume. The posture — her shoulders came forward, her chin dropped. "Not the — I don't miss the lying. Or the gym woman. Or the part where he let me pick up doubles every week while he watched videos on his phone. I miss having someone there. In the apartment. I miss noise. It's so quiet, Lola. My place is so quiet. It's like —" She stopped. Picked at the rim of her Styrofoam cup. "You know when you turn the TV off and the silence afterward is louder than the silence before you turned it on? That's what it's like. Every night."

Lola knew that silence. She'd lived inside it for ten years, first in the apartment in Union City where it had been Tomás-shaped — a quiet that filled the exact spaces his sounds had occupied: the scratch of his pen on the crossword, the wheeze in his breathing that she'd told him to get checked and he hadn't, the thud of his boots by the door — and then in this room, where the quiet was different, institutional, a quiet made up of the sounds just outside it: Margaret's breathing, the ventilation hum, the distant call bells.

"I know," Lola said.

And then, because the words pressed against her and she had to let some of them out or they'd crack something inside her, she said: "You're a good person, mija."

She said it in her performed voice. She said it with the warm, slightly drifting delivery she used for everything. But she meant it from underneath the performance, from the place where Lola was still entirely herself, where the confusion hadn't reached, where she was a woman who recognized goodness because she'd spent enough years around its absence to know the difference.

Tammy looked at her. For a second — less than a second, a flicker — something crossed her face. Not suspicion of the ring. Nothing that specific. Just a flicker of attention, the way a person looked when a sentence arrived with more weight than its packaging suggested. As if Tammy had heard something underneath the warm old woman's voice that didn't match the warm old woman's diagnosis.

Then it passed. Tammy looked back at the squirrel, which had climbed halfway up the garbage can near the courtyard entrance and was evaluating its options.

"Good people get paid shit," Tammy said.

"That's true."

"Yeah."

They sat. A car pulled into the parking lot, idled for a minute near the entrance, then backed out and drove away. Someone who'd come to the wrong place, or come to the right place and changed their mind. Lola had seen it happen before — family members who pulled in and sat in their cars for ten, fifteen minutes, working up to the doors. She understood. This building was hard to enter and harder to leave, because leaving meant driving away from someone who couldn't drive with you, and that act, repeated every visit, accumulated into something that felt like a judgment even when it wasn't one.

"I gotta get back," Tammy said. She stood and her back cracked, two sharp reports like knuckles popping, the sound of a forty-three-year-old body that did the physical work of a much younger person for compensation that assumed she was replaceable. She picked up both coffee cups — hers and Lola's — because that was Tammy. She picked up the cups. She remembered the sugars. She came back.

"Tammy."

"Yeah?"

Lola looked at her. She wanted to say: I'm not confused. I haven't been confused. I remember every word you've said to me about Greg and Brandy and the apartment and the cat. I'm lying to you every day, every time you brush my hair and bring me coffee and sit on this bench and tell me things that cost you something. I'm lying and you deserve better than a friend who isn't real.

She almost said it. The words were right there, lined up, ready to walk out of her mouth and ruin everything.

"Tell Brandy I said hi," she said instead.

Tammy snorted. Not a real laugh. The short, hard sound a person made when something was funny enough to crack the surface but not enough to break through. "Yeah, I'll get right on that."

She walked back inside. The courtyard door closed behind her with the heavy pneumatic exhale that all institutional doors made, a sound engineered for a building designed to contain rather than welcome. Lola sat with the bench to herself and the two wet rings where the coffee cups had been and the leaves collecting in the corners of the pavement and the last of the afternoon light angling across the parking lot.

Her left hand trembled against her thigh. She put her right hand over it, covering it, the way you'd hold a small animal that was shaking — not to stop the shaking but to let it know the shaking had been noticed.

The word came back to her. Aluminum. It had been there all along, she supposed. It was the not-knowing that was the thing. Not the forgetting — everybody forgot, that was just the tax on being alive long enough — but the not-knowing which kind of forgetting it was. Whether the word had gone because she'd spent fourteen months teaching her brain to lose things on purpose, or because her brain was losing things on its own now, because seventy-nine years and Parkinson's and grief and a daily performance of incapacity had, together, begun to do what each one alone might not have managed.

She said the word once, out loud, to the empty courtyard. Aluminum. Just to prove she could.

The trees didn't answer. The parking lot didn't care. She sat there until the light changed and the air got cold enough that her tremor worsened, and then she went inside and became the person everyone expected her to be.


# Chapter 13

Marcus was sitting in the vinyl chair closest to the door when Carmen came down the hall at quarter past midnight. That was wrong. Marcus didn't sit in meetings — Marcus paced, shifted his weight, leaned against walls and pushed off again like a man who couldn't afford to stop moving. But tonight he was sitting with his hands on his knees, still, contained, and that was how Carmen knew he'd come to ask for something.

The TV room was cold. October had arrived while facilities management debated switching the heating system from its summer settings, and the result was a building that held the day's chill instead of releasing it. The overhead fluorescents were off. They used the television's standby light and a brass lamp Agnes had brought from her room months ago, its shade throwing amber across the card table and leaving the rest of the room in blue-gray shadow. The single-pane windows were black, and when Carmen passed close to one she could feel the cold radiating off it like the opposite of warmth, a presence rather than an absence.

Harry was at the table in his wheelchair, though he'd pushed back from it by a few inches — not close enough to work, not far enough to be out of the circle. A calculation in furniture. Agnes sat beside him with only a coffee cup, no notebooks, no pill organizer. Her hands wrapped around the cup with a grip that suggested she already knew what this meeting was about. Earl was against the far wall, arms crossed, legs extended in his institutional slippers. Next to him, Burt's empty wheelchair was folded and leaned against the baseboard. Nobody mentioned it. Nobody had mentioned it for two weeks, not since Burt had come back from the hospital with his eyes fogged over and his hands forgetting what they were holding. The empty wheelchair was just a shape in the room now, like the TV nobody watched or the fake plant by the window that had been there since before any of them arrived.

Lola was at her position by the hallway door, but she kept turning back toward the group with quick glances that made her tremor visible even in the low light. Her left hand opened and closed around the gold crucifix at her throat.

Marcus had come in during visiting hours — signed the log as Lola's nephew at four-thirty, stayed through dinner, and melted into the building the way he always did. Devon, the overnight aide, had been asleep at the nurses' station since eleven, earbuds in, a true-crime podcast murmuring through tiny speakers. Carmen had heard it when she passed on her med check.

"So," Harry said.

Marcus looked at the floor, then at Carmen, then at Harry. He was checking the room's temperature before he opened his mouth. Carmen had seen him do this with buyers in the parking lot descriptions Harry gave her — read the space, calibrate, then talk. Tonight the calibration took longer.

"I got approached," Marcus said. "By a buyer. Not like our regular people."

Agnes's fingers tightened around her cup.

"A reseller," Marcus said. "He works the club scene, maybe college towns — I didn't go deep on details. He's got his own customers who want oxy. Steady demand. He doesn't care about metformin, doesn't want the blood pressure meds or the generics. Oxycodone, five and ten milligram, and he wants volume."

"How much volume," Harry said. Not a question — a prompt.

"Two hundred tabs a month to start. He'll pay six dollars per milligram."

Agnes set her coffee cup on the card table. The sound it made was small and precise, a single click, and in the quiet room it functioned like a period at the end of a sentence nobody had finished.

"That's—" Carmen started.

"Thirty-six hundred a month just on oxy," Harry said. "On top of existing distribution."

"More like eight grand combined if we keep everything else running." Marcus stood up. There was the pacing — he crossed to the window, turned, crossed back. His sneakers were quiet on the linoleum but Carmen could feel the movement as spatial pressure, the room getting smaller with each pass. "Look, that's double. Harry, you could pay off Jack's debt in what, two, three months? Earl, Denise wouldn't need those weekend shifts anymore. Everybody benefits."

"Not everybody," Agnes said.

"Come on, Agnes—"

"I am not finished." She didn't raise her voice. The room adjusted anyway. "You said he works clubs. College towns. His customers are not people with prescriptions they cannot afford. They are recreational users."

"Some of them might have—"

"They are recreational users buying oxycodone to get high, and some percentage of them will combine it with alcohol or benzodiazepines because they do not know any better, and at six dollars per milligram they will buy enough to kill themselves because they have no concept of opioid tolerance." Agnes spoke faster as she went, facts compressing, the rhythm of a pharmacist who'd spent forty-two years making sure the wrong pill didn't reach the wrong person. "Do you know what the lethal dose of oxycodone is for a naive user? Forty milligrams if they are unlucky. Eighty if they have been drinking. That is eight tablets at ten milligrams. That is one Saturday night."

The room held the number. Carmen felt it settle.

Marcus stopped pacing. "We don't know that's what happens—"

"We know we do not know," Agnes said. "That is the point. When I sort pills for Rosa, I know her condition, her weight, her other medications. When I sort pills for your reseller, I know nothing about the person who swallows them. I have spent my career making sure that does not happen."

"Agnes." Marcus put his hands up, palms out — a peace gesture that didn't land. "I hear you. I get it. But we're already selling controlled substances out of a nursing home. We crossed the line a long time ago."

"We crossed a line," Carmen said. "Not this line."

Marcus turned to her. Something in his expression shifted — not anger yet, but the pre-anger look of someone gearing up. "You sure about that? Because from where I'm standing, the only difference between selling oxy to Janine and selling it to this guy is that Janine makes you feel better about it."

"The difference is Janine has a documented pain condition and a doctor who cut her off because he's scared of the DEA. The difference is she takes it as prescribed."

"She takes it as we give it to her. We're not her doctors."

"We know what it's for."

"So now we're pharmacists?"

"One of us actually is," Carmen said, and heard herself aim the line like a weapon and regretted it immediately — she wasn't trying to score points, she was trying to hold the floor, and the room was moving faster than she could think.

***

Harry tapped the table twice. Two quiet taps, the kind that came from a man who'd run meetings for fifty years — PTA boards, condo associations, this.

"Two questions," he said. "First, what does this do to our exposure. Second, what does it do to us."

"The exposure's manageable," Marcus said. He sat back down. His leg bounced. "I'd handle the reseller same as everybody else. Separate phone, different location, cash only. He doesn't know about Lakeview, doesn't know where the supply—"

"He knows about you," Agnes said. "That is sufficient."

"He knows a first name and a number."

"He is a drug dealer, Marcus." Agnes said it the way she'd state a dosage — flat, terminal, without decoration. "He has his own legal exposure. His own customers who could be arrested and pressured to cooperate. Every distribution charge creates an incentive for someone in the chain to trade information for a lesser sentence. You add a reseller to our network, you are not adding a customer. You are adding every person he sells to as a potential point of failure."

"Every buyer we have now is a point of—"

"Our buyers are patients purchasing medication for documented conditions. If Rosa is arrested with thirty hydroxychloroquine tablets, the charge is simple possession and a sympathetic judge. If your reseller is caught with two hundred oxycodone, that is trafficking with intent to distribute, and trafficking investigations go upstream." Agnes paused long enough for the word upstream to do its work. "They will find you. And you will lead them here."

Marcus rubbed the back of his neck. His leg was still bouncing. "You're assuming the worst case."

"I am assuming the likely case. Do you know this person's real name?"

"I know what he goes by."

"Do you know whether he has prior convictions? Whether he is currently under investigation? Whether he was sent to you by someone cooperating with law enforcement?"

Marcus didn't answer. The bounce in his leg slowed and stopped.

"A person you have never dealt with before approached you and offered premium prices for Schedule II opioids in quantity," Agnes said. "You did not find him. He found you. That alone should concern you more than it apparently does."

Carmen watched Marcus absorb this — watched the arguments he'd prepared hit the wall of Agnes's specificity and lose shape. Agnes didn't argue the way other people argued. She didn't raise her voice, didn't appeal to emotion, didn't repeat herself. She stated facts in series and let the accumulation do the work. It was like being corrected by a textbook.

But Marcus wasn't done. He leaned forward, elbows on his knees, and when he spoke his voice had changed register — lower, harder, the humor stripped out.

"Let me tell you something. I drive a car with a hundred forty thousand miles on it and a brake light that's been out for two months because I can't afford the bulb and the ticket at the same time. I meet people in strip mall parking lots at nine o'clock at night with Schedule II controlled substances in my glove box. Every time I do that, I'm the one who goes to prison if anything goes wrong. Not Harry. Not Agnes. Not any of you. You'd get the news cameras and the sympathetic interviews — poor confused elderly victims, manipulated by some outside operator. I'd get Rahway."

The room was quiet except for Earl's breathing — the rasp underneath each exhale, the faint wet whistle that Carmen registered every time and told herself she should mention to someone and never did.

"You carry more risk," Harry said. His voice was neutral, the voice he used when he was still calculating. "That's true."

Marcus turned toward him. Something opened in his face — not gratitude exactly, but the relief of being heard after a long stretch of not being heard.

"But that's a separate question from this buyer," Harry said. "The risk isn't just yours anymore. It's the count discrepancy that shows up on Carmen's log. It's the medication room inventory that doesn't balance. Two hundred additional oxycodone a month — that's a shortage we can't attribute to documentation error. That's a pattern that triggers a DEA referral, not just a state survey question."

"So we adjust the—"

"There's no adjustment that covers two hundred pills a month, Marcus. The arithmetic doesn't work. I've been running the numbers on what we can move without triggering automated flags for fourteen months. We're close to the ceiling now."

Marcus looked at Carmen. Under the brass lamp his face was all planes and shadow, and she could see the man he'd been before — the CNA who'd gotten fired for caring too much, the caregiver the system had ground up and spit into a basement apartment. That person was real and present. But so was the person who'd walked into this room with a number in his head that would fix his life, and those two people were pulling in different directions.

"You know what, Carmen?" His voice went tight. "You got here late. Harry set this up, Agnes figured out the supply chain, I built the distribution routes. You showed up and we were already running. So it's real easy for you to stand here and talk about what kind of crime is the right amount of crime."

"That's not—"

"You're pulling thirteen hundred extra a month from this and telling me where the moral line is."

"I don't take money from this." She heard her own voice shift — the contractions thinning, the sentences getting shorter, the formality that rose in her when she was angry enough to stop being casual. It was the voice she used with administrators and surveyors, the voice of someone holding herself together by gripping the professional register like a handrail. "You know that."

"Right. You just take the protection. The part where nobody asks why your controlled substance log doesn't add up. That's free, I guess."

It wasn't free. It was the most expensive thing Carmen had ever accepted, and Marcus knew it, and throwing it at her was his way of saying he'd run out of better arguments. She let the accusation sit. She didn't dignify it.

Lola came away from the doorway. She'd been standing there the whole time, one hand on the frame, the other working the crucifix, and now she moved into the circle with the unsteady gait that Carmen still couldn't parse — Parkinson's or performance or some blend of both that Lola herself probably couldn't untangle anymore.

"I don't like this," Lola said. Her voice was faster than usual, the words running together the way they did when she was scared. "I don't — we had something that worked, we were helping people, and now it's — Dios mio, Marcus, I can't do this. I can't sit here and sort pills that end up God knows where. I'm already—" She stopped. Restarted. "I'm already having trouble, you know? With all of it. With who I am in the daytime and who I am at night and where one stops. I can't add this. I can't carry this."

Her tremor was bad. Her left hand shook against her hip in small rapid oscillations, and she pressed it flat against her thigh to stop it, which didn't work, it never worked, it just moved the shaking somewhere else. Marcus watched her and something in his face softened. Lola was the closest thing he had to family — Carmen knew this, had seen the way he called her Tia when he didn't think anyone was listening, had seen the way Lola touched his face when he visited, the way she asked about his mother who'd been dead for two years and he kept the lie going because it was what she needed. That bond was in the room now, pulling against everything Marcus had just argued.

"Lola," he said. "I'm not trying to—"

"I know, mijo. I know." She wiped her eyes with the heel of her hand. "But that doesn't make it okay."

Harry looked at Earl.

Earl had not spoken. He'd been in the same position against the wall since the meeting started — arms crossed, legs out, his face giving Carmen nothing. His breathing was the loudest thing about him, that persistent rattle underneath each exhale. He'd been coughing more these past weeks, deep and wet, and he waved it off every time Carmen looked at him with her nurse's eye. He was coughing now — or not coughing, holding it in, the effort visible in the way his chest rose and caught before releasing.

"Earl," Harry said.

Earl cleared his throat. The clearing turned into a cough he couldn't suppress — short, hard, rattling, the sound of something loose and heavy moving in his chest. He turned his head away from the group and covered his mouth with his fist. When he was done he wiped his hand on his pajama pants and looked at Marcus.

"We started this because nobody was looking out for us," Earl said. "That's not what this is."

One sentence. The room held it the way a room holds a dropped glass before anyone moves to pick up the pieces. Carmen had been in enough of these meetings to know that Earl's silence was his primary instrument and his speech was something else entirely — rare, weighed, final. When Earl talked, it was because he'd measured the situation and found the single sentence that contained its whole truth.

Marcus stared at him. Not with anger. With something worse — the look of being excluded from a group he thought he belonged to. Marcus had been coming to this building for eighteen months, smuggling pills in cookie tins, meeting buyers who needed medication they couldn't get, sending money to people who'd treated him like family after the institution that employed him threw him out. He'd earned his place in this room. And Earl had just told him the thing he was proposing put him outside of it.

"That's it?" Marcus said. His voice was rough.

Earl didn't answer. He'd said what he had to say.

"You're sitting here telling me what this is about? I'm the one who—" Marcus caught himself, shook his head. He'd already made the risk argument. He could hear it looping. "Fine. Okay."

Harry straightened in his wheelchair. His hands went flat on the table again, the posture of decision.

"Here's what's going to happen," Harry said. "You're going to decline the buyer. You're going to tell him the supply isn't available. You break the contact — no second meeting, no counter-offer, no keeping his number for later."

"Harry—"

"That's the decision."

"It's the wrong one."

"Maybe. We've survived wrong decisions before. We won't survive a trafficking investigation."

Carmen spoke. She'd been running the calculation the whole time they argued — not Marcus's financial calculation but the legal one, her name on every dispensation record, her license number on every controlled substance log, the Board of Nursing file that would end her career and her freedom if any of this surfaced through a reseller's arrest and the chain of testimony that followed.

"Marcus." She waited until he was looking at her. "If you take this deal, I report everything. I go to the Board. I give them the diversion records, the distribution pattern, dates, quantities. All of it."

The room went still. Even Earl's breathing seemed to quiet, though Carmen knew that was her own attention narrowing, the way it did in a crisis when the peripheral sounds dropped out and the thing that mattered filled the whole channel.

"You'd go down too," Marcus said.

"I know."

"You'd lose your license." He paused. "You'd lose Reggie."

Her son's name in this room, in this argument, at one-thirty in the morning in a nursing home where she was supposed to be administering care. She felt the badge under her scrub top pressing against her sternum and she pushed back against the feeling the way she pushed back against everything that threatened to crack her open at work — with procedure, with the next task, with the professional competence that was both her identity and her shield.

"I know what I'd lose," she said.

"And you'd still do it."

"I'm telling you what I'll do."

Harry was watching her. He'd gone completely still — the dangerous still, the Harry who stopped moving when the ledger showed a number he didn't like. Agnes had both hands flat on the table, her coffee forgotten, her posture a mirror of Harry's that she didn't seem conscious of. Lola was crying, silently, one hand on the crucifix and the other pressed against her stomach. Earl was looking at the wall.

Marcus looked around the room. He took his time with it — Harry, immovable behind his calculation; Agnes, who met his gaze without softening or looking away; Earl, who wouldn't look back; Lola, who loved him and was crying because of what he'd proposed; Carmen, who was not going to blink.

"Okay," Marcus said. "Fine. I'll tell him no."

He said it too fast.

Carmen heard it — the speed of the surrender after fifteen minutes of hard argument. He'd fought like a man who believed what he was saying, and he'd given up like a man who didn't need permission. Agnes heard it too; her eyes changed, a micro-adjustment Carmen recognized from watching Agnes check medication labels — the slight narrowing that meant something didn't match. Harry's hands came off the table and settled in his lap, and Carmen couldn't read whether that was satisfaction or a quieter, more worried thing.

"I'll call him tomorrow," Marcus said. He was already standing, already straightening his jacket — gray, clean, pressed. He always kept himself neat. The CNA's institutional habit, the reflex of a person trained to present as competent and trustworthy. "I'll shut it down. It's done."

"Good," Harry said.

"Yeah." Marcus adjusted his collar. He wasn't looking at anyone now. "So. We good?"

Nobody said we good. The question sat there, unanswered, which was its own answer.

"Lola will walk you out," Harry said.

Lola wiped her face with the back of her hand and went to the hallway door. She leaned out, checked both directions — the lookout muscle, automatic even now — then turned back to Marcus. "Vamos, mijo," she said, and the softness of it, the unchanged tenderness, landed on Marcus in a way Carmen could see. His mouth moved. He didn't say anything. He followed her out.

Their footsteps faded down the hall — Lola's shuffle, Marcus's heavier tread — and then there was only the building's night sounds: the ventilation cycling, a call bell pinging in the other wing, the hum of Devon's podcast leaking from the nurses' station.

Agnes picked up the coffee cups and stacked them with the efficiency of someone who needed her hands busy. She carried them to the side table and stood there for a moment with her back to the room.

"He is going to do it regardless," Agnes said.

Harry said nothing. The silence was his agreement — or rather, his acknowledgment that the calculation had already accounted for this outcome and found no variable that changed it.

"He's going to do it," Carmen said. Not a question. Confirmation. She was leaning against the wall and the plaster was cold through her scrub top, the building's October chill pressing through the material like a hand.

"Probably," Harry said. It was the first time Carmen had heard him use the word. Harry dealt in numbers, not probabilities. Probably meant the spreadsheet had a cell he couldn't fill.

Agnes turned from the side table. She looked at Carmen — a direct look, no warmth in it but no hostility either, just the flat professional attention of a woman who'd spent four decades assessing whether the right thing was in the right bottle.

"I will not sort pills for a reseller," Agnes said. "That is my position regardless of what Marcus does or does not do."

"Okay," Carmen said.

"I want that to be clear."

"It's clear, Agnes."

Agnes left. Her footsteps were nearly silent — she moved through the building like someone who'd practiced invisibility and gotten too good at it. Earl followed a moment later, getting up slowly, one hand on the wall, his cough working through him in three hard pulses he turned away from Harry and Carmen to manage. He didn't say goodnight. His slippers made no sound on the linoleum.

Harry and Carmen sat in the TV room. The brass lamp was still on, its amber circle shrunk to the card table where nobody sat. The television's standby light gave the vinyl chairs a faint blue sheen. Outside the windows, the parking lot was dark — it got dark by five now, the days pulling in, and by midnight the lot was a field of black with the orange glow of the two working sodium lights at the far end.

"He was right about one thing," Harry said. "The risk distribution. He carries more than the rest of us."

"I know."

"That doesn't make the deal right. But it makes his argument not nothing."

Carmen looked at the empty room. Burt's folded wheelchair against the wall. The card table where Agnes's notebooks used to be, where pill counts and buyer lists and expiration dates were tracked with a pharmacist's precision. The vinyl chairs holding the impressions of the people who'd just been in them.

"What do we do if he takes it anyway?" she said.

"Count the inventory. See what's missing."

An accountant's answer. Carmen almost smiled but didn't. It wasn't funny. It was the sound of a man whose only tool was a ledger trying to solve a problem that lived in another person's need.

She turned off the lamp. The room went dark except for the hallway light leaking under the door, a thin bright line across the linoleum. She unplugged the television and the standby light died.

"You should get some sleep," she said.

"I don't sleep much." Harry's wheelchair creaked as he shifted. In the dark, his voice sounded different — older, thinner, the voice of a man who was eighty-four regardless of what his mind could still do. "Carmen."

"Yeah."

"If you report, make sure it's because you need to. Not because you're angry."

She didn't know how to answer that. She let the silence stand.

Harry wheeled himself to the door. She watched his silhouette against the hallway light — hunched in the chair, his thin shoulders, the faded blue cardigan. He turned right toward his room and was gone.

Carmen stood in the dark TV room for another minute. She could hear the building breathing — the ventilation, the call bell in the far wing that nobody was answering, a toilet flushing somewhere, a cough from a room she couldn't identify. The building was full of people whose bodies made sounds they couldn't control, whose lungs and bladders and joints announced their decline whether they consented to it or not. She was the youngest person in this wing by forty years, and she'd just threatened to burn it all down because a man she didn't fully trust had agreed with her too quickly.

She went back to the sub-station and charted. She checked on Room 110 — the woman inside breathing with the mechanical rhythm of her oxygen concentrator. She checked on Burt in 116A, asleep with his mouth open, his hands loose on the blanket. Earl's shape was visible in the other bed, the rise and fall of his chest, the rattle audible from the doorway.

She went to the medication room and counted. It was the thing she did when she couldn't do anything else — opened the cabinet, logged the controlled substances, matched the numbers to the sheet. Everything balanced. The oxycodone was where it should be, the alprazolam, the tramadol. The count was clean.

The count was clean and Marcus had agreed too fast and outside the medication room window the October dark sat on the parking lot and she was thirty-one years old and in too deep and the only thing between her and what came next was the hope that a man who'd spent the last hour explaining why he deserved more from this operation had actually agreed to take less.

She put the clipboard back on its hook and locked the cabinet.

She charted the count. She charted it accurately, the way she'd charted everything since the beginning — every number true, every timestamp real, every entry a faithful record of a system that concealed its losses inside the institution's tolerance for error. The charting was honest. Everything around it was not. It was getting harder to tell where one ended and the other began, and Carmen thought of Lola saying the same thing about her performance, about who she was in the daytime and who she was at night, and she closed the chart and put the pen down and sat very still in the medication room's alcohol-and-pill-dust quiet until her shift was over.


# Chapter 14

The resident in Room 110 had soiled herself again by seven-thirty, which made three times that shift. Tammy rolled her onto her left side, stripped the pad, cleaned her with the efficiency of someone who had done this particular sequence of motions several thousand times in fifteen years, and replaced the linens while the woman stared at the wall and made the low, steady sound she always made — not quite a moan, not quite a hum, somewhere in the register of a radiator that couldn't be fixed. Tammy told her she was almost done. She told her she was doing fine. She balled up the soiled pad and dropped it in the hamper, and the smell — warm, biological, cut with the chemical bite of the barrier cream — followed her into the hallway and sat in her scrubs for the rest of the night.

That was the job. Not the job description, not the intake brochure with the courtyard photo and the woman doing a puzzle in natural light that didn't exist anywhere inside the actual building. The job was bodies. Lifting them, turning them, cleaning them, checking the skin that broke down when nobody turned them fast enough. The job was answering the call bell from Room 104 and knowing before she got there that Mr. Delaney wanted the TV turned up because his hearing aid battery was dead and nobody had ordered replacements since September. The job was Mrs. Okafor's three-times-a-shift incontinence and the fact that there was no CNA coverage on the other wing tonight because Shayla had called out and Cliff hadn't approved the overtime to replace her, which meant the overnight aide was going to inherit forty-two residents with one pair of hands, and he'd handle it the way Devon always handled it, which was to fall asleep at the nurse's station by midnight and let the call bells ring.

Tammy had been at Lakeview Gardens for fifteen years. She'd outlasted four administrators, six Directors of Nursing, and more CNAs than she could count. She'd watched the facility change owners twice — first from the family who built it to a regional chain, then from the chain to a private equity group in Connecticut whose name she'd seen on a memo exactly once. Each time, the carpets got older and the staffing got thinner and the lobby got a new motivational poster, and the work stayed the same because the work was always the same. People got old. Their bodies failed. Someone had to be in the room when it happened.

She clocked out at eleven-oh-three. The night air hit her in the parking lot like a hand on her chest — late October, that particular New Jersey cold that wasn't dramatic enough to feel like weather, just a persistent damp chill that crawled under her jacket and stayed. The trees in the courtyard were nearly bare. She could see the last few leaves on the middle one from where she stood, dark shapes against the parking lot lights. By next week they'd be gone.

Her car started on the second try. She sat in it with the heater on, waiting for the vents to stop blowing cold, and looked at the building. Lakeview at night was a low, flat shape with lit windows scattered randomly — some residents kept their lights on all night, some rooms were empty, the nurse's station glowed steady at the center like a pilot light. She'd been looking at this building almost every night since she was twenty-eight. She could identify which wing had the bad fluorescent tube by the flicker pattern. She could tell from the parking lot whether the overnight nurse was at the central station or the sub-station by which window was lit. These were not useful skills. They were just what happened when you stayed somewhere long enough.

The drive home took twelve minutes. The studio apartment was on the second floor of a converted house in Dunellen, above a woman named Grace who played Christian radio at volumes that suggested either devotion or hearing loss. The apartment was four hundred and seventy-five dollars a month, first and last, no security deposit because the landlord couldn't get one — the previous tenant had kept cats. Not a cat. Cats. Plural. The carpet remembered them. Tammy had tried baking soda, enzyme spray, a rented steam cleaner. The smell lived in the pad underneath, and the landlord wasn't replacing the pad for four seventy-five a month, so the smell stayed, and Tammy had learned to stop noticing it by the third week and then to notice it again every time she came home after being away for eight hours. She noticed it now, stepping inside, the particular ammonia-musk that wasn't exactly cat piss anymore but wasn't exactly not cat piss either.

She dropped her bag by the door. She kicked off her shoes. The apartment was one room plus a bathroom so small the door hit the toilet when she opened it. Her things were here — the couch from the old apartment that Kevin had let her have because it had a stain on the middle cushion, the TV she'd bought at Walmart, the bed she slept in alone. Kevin had the house. Kevin had the house and the good furniture and a woman named Trish or Tricia who worked at the Verizon store in Bridgewater and was thirty-one, which Tammy knew because she'd looked her up once on a night when she'd finished a bottle of Yellow Tail and hated herself the entire time she was doing it but did it anyway. Trish-or-Tricia had blond highlights and ran 5Ks. Her Facebook was public. Tammy had closed the laptop and poured the rest of the wine down the sink, which was the correct thing to do, and then she'd fished the bottle out of the recycling and checked if there was actually any left, which was the honest thing to do.

Kevin had filed. That was the fact of it. Twelve years of marriage, no kids because they'd kept saying next year until next year became a thing neither of them mentioned, and he'd filed on a Tuesday and she'd come home from work — from changing Mrs. Okafor and answering Mr. Delaney's call bell and doing the work that paid sixteen dollars and fifty cents an hour — and the papers were on the kitchen table with a Post-it note that said "Sorry, T." Sorry, T. Twelve years distilled into a Post-it note and an initial, like she was a task he'd finally gotten around to completing.

She'd signed. There wasn't anything to fight about. He made more money, but not enough to owe her anything significant. They'd split the checking account, which had fourteen hundred dollars in it. She'd gotten seven hundred. Seven hundred dollars and a stained couch and a studio apartment that smelled like cats.

Tammy sat on that couch now and ate a bowl of cereal because it was eleven-thirty at night and she wasn't going to cook. The milk was close to turning. She ate it anyway. She watched half an episode of something on her phone — a show about people renovating houses, buying houses, people with budgets of four hundred thousand dollars looking at crown molding and saying things like "it's not really us." She turned it off.

Sixteen fifty an hour. Thirty-three thousand a year before taxes if she worked full-time, which she did, every week. After taxes, insurance that covered almost nothing, and the union dues that Kevin used to call a waste but that Tammy paid anyway because the union was the only reason she'd survived the last two ownership changes without being fired, she cleared about twenty-two hundred a month. Rent took four seventy-five. Car payment, two forty. Insurance. Gas. Phone. Food. She was not one crisis away from not making it. She was already not making it, and had been not making it for so long that not making it had become the baseline, the thing she organized her life around, the way other people organized their lives around mortgages or retirement plans.

She washed the bowl. She brushed her teeth. She got into bed and stared at the ceiling, which had a water stain in the corner shaped like nothing — it wasn't shaped like anything, it was just a stain, and the fact that she noticed it every night and tried to make it into something was the kind of thing that happened when you lived alone in a room and there was nothing else to look at.

Fifteen years at Lakeview. She had started at twenty-eight, fresh off the CNA certification course at the county college, and she'd thought it would be temporary. A year, maybe two, while she figured out what she actually wanted to do. She'd been good at it. She was still good at it. The job rewarded a specific kind of competence — you had to notice things. Skin changes. Breathing changes. The difference between a resident who was quiet because they were sleeping and a resident who was quiet because something was wrong. You had to know who ate and who didn't, who was losing weight, who had a UTI brewing before the lab work came back because you recognized the confusion and the smell. Tammy noticed all of these things. She noticed them the way a mechanic notices engine sounds — automatically, without deciding to, because the alternative was not noticing, and not noticing in this job meant someone got hurt.

She'd seen two residents die on her shifts. One had been expected — a woman on hospice who'd been declining for weeks and went at four in the morning with her daughter holding her hand, which was the version people imagined when they thought about dying in a nursing home. The other had been a man who choked on a piece of chicken at dinner, turned blue, and died on the dining room floor while Tammy did the Heimlich and another aide called 911 and three other residents watched and one of them kept eating. She'd been twenty-nine for that one. She'd gone home and sat in Kevin's car — their car, then — and cried until her nose ran and her eyes swelled shut, and then she'd gone back the next day because she had a shift and somebody had to work it.

She'd seen staff fired for stealing cash from residents' drawers. She'd seen a night nurse passed out drunk in the medication room. She'd seen families visit once after admission, sign the paperwork, and never come back — not once, not for Christmas, not when the facility called to say their mother had fallen or their father had stopped eating. She'd seen the good aides leave because the pay was an insult, and the bad aides stay because nowhere else would have them. She'd seen Cliff Moorehead walk through the building at survey time like he gave a damn, and she'd seen him the rest of the year, which was to say she hadn't, because Cliff spent his time in his office with the door closed, watching census numbers on a spreadsheet.

What fifteen years taught you was that nothing in this building was what it looked like. The clean hallways smelled like disinfectant because underneath the disinfectant was something worse. The cheerful activity calendar on the wall — Chair Yoga, Movie Afternoon, Arts & Crafts — existed for the state surveyors, not the residents. The residents who smiled when families visited sometimes cried when families left, and the residents who seemed angry or vacant sometimes had the clearest sense of what was happening to them.

Tammy knew all of this. She knew it in her hands and her back and her knees, which ached now in a way they hadn't when she was twenty-eight. She knew it and she kept working because the work was real, even when everything around it wasn't.

She lay in bed and thought about Carmen Reyes, who had been acting strange for weeks.

---

It had started — or Tammy had started noticing, which might not be the same thing — around the time of the state survey back in September. Before that, Carmen had been the way she always was: competent, professional, a little tired, good with the residents. She did her job well. Tammy respected that. In a building where most people were either burned out or coasting, Carmen showed up and did the work, and she did it without the martyr energy that some nurses brought, the look-how-much-I-care performance that made Tammy's teeth itch. Carmen just worked. She charted. She did her med passes on time. She called families when she was supposed to call families. She was, in Tammy's fifteen-year assessment, one of the better nurses Lakeview had managed to keep.

But something had shifted. Carmen stayed late now — not double-shift late, which she'd always done for the money, but a different kind of late. She'd finish her charting and then linger, walking the halls, checking on rooms that didn't need checking. She spent time with certain residents. Harry Zielinski in 114A. Agnes Chen in 108A. Lola Fernandez in 112A. Not quick med-pass visits but actual time, with the door closed, which wasn't standard. You closed the door for private care — toileting, bathing, wound changes. You didn't close the door for a chat.

And Carmen had gotten evasive. Not obviously — she was too smart for that. But when Tammy asked her how things were going, Carmen's answers had developed a lag. A half-beat of recalibration before she said "Fine" or "Same old" or "You know how it is." Tammy knew how it was. That was the problem. She knew exactly how it was, and the way Carmen was acting wasn't how it was.

Then there were the medication counts.

The controlled substance count had been off three times since Tammy started paying attention. Three oxy here, five alprazolam there, always documented as charting errors, always explained by the kind of systemic excuse that worked because the system really was that broken — the overnight staff didn't count right, the hybrid system dropped entries, the pharmacy shorted the delivery. These explanations were plausible. Tammy had heard them for fifteen years. They were plausible the way everything at Lakeview was plausible: individually reasonable, collectively suspicious, and nobody with the authority to investigate had the energy to bother.

During the state survey, Tammy had been in the hallway outside the conference room when Carmen was interviewed. She hadn't been trying to listen. The walls were thin and she was restocking the supply cart ten feet away. She'd heard Carmen explain the count discrepancies to the surveyor — calmly, professionally, with the specific competence of a nurse who knew which excuses the state would accept. Overnight staff. Documentation lags. System migration errors. Carmen's voice had been steady and practiced, and the surveyor had moved on.

The explanations were good. They were, in fact, too good. Tammy had given a lot of explanations over the years — to surveyors, to families, to administrators. She knew what a real explanation sounded like. It sounded uncertain. It sounded annoyed. It sounded like someone who didn't know the answer and didn't like not knowing. Carmen's answers had sounded like someone who knew exactly what the answer was and had prepared a different one.

Tammy didn't do anything with this observation. She filed it away the way she filed everything — next to the dying man on the dining room floor and the nurse passed out in the med room and the families who never came back. It was information, and it wasn't her problem. She was a CNA making sixteen fifty an hour with no authority to investigate anything and no reason to believe anyone would listen if she did.

But then she noticed Agnes Chen.

Agnes had come to Lakeview three years ago, after a stroke. Tammy remembered because she'd been on shift the day Agnes arrived, and Agnes had been furious. Not confused, not docile, not any of the things the intake paperwork suggested. Furious. She'd argued with the admissions coordinator. She'd demanded cognitive testing. She'd written letters — Tammy had seen her at the desk in the common room, filling legal pads with small, precise handwriting, addressed to the ombudsman's office, to her daughter, to the state. She'd called her daughter Helen and said things Tammy could hear from the hallway — clear, articulate, blistering things about autonomy and rights and the fact that a temporary speech deficit from a stroke did not constitute cognitive impairment.

Then, about six months after admission, Agnes stopped. She stopped writing. She stopped arguing. She stopped talking, mostly. She sat in her chair and stared at the wall and responded to direct questions with silence or single syllables. The staff noted the change as expected decline. Post-stroke depression, probably. Cognitive deterioration. These things happened. Tammy had noted it too, at the time.

But she'd worked this wing long enough to know what decline looked like, and this wasn't it. Decline was gradual. Decline was Mrs. Okafor, who'd lost language in a slow erosion over eighteen months — first the complex sentences, then the simple ones, then the words, then the sounds that used to be words. Agnes hadn't eroded. Agnes had stopped. Like a switch.

And now, weeks into whatever was bothering Tammy about Carmen's behavior, she'd started watching Agnes more carefully. Not watching — she didn't have time to watch anyone, she had forty-two residents and half the aide coverage she was supposed to have. But noticing. The way she noticed skin changes and breathing patterns. And what she noticed was Agnes's eyes.

Agnes sat in her chair in 108A and stared at the wall. But when people talked near her — when Carmen stopped in the doorway, when Tammy herself came in to check her water pitcher, when the physical therapist documented her "refusal to engage" — Agnes's eyes moved. They tracked. They followed the conversation the way a person followed a conversation they understood. Not the random scanning of someone confused. Not the fixed gaze of someone withdrawn. Agnes was listening. Agnes was comprehending. And she was doing neither of those things when the doctors came through or when Cliff made his rare appearances.

That was the thing Tammy couldn't let go of. Not the medication counts, which might genuinely be charting errors. Not Carmen's evasiveness, which might be personal — the woman had a kid and money problems and a hundred reasons to be distracted. But Agnes Chen's eyes didn't lie. You couldn't fake the direction of attention. You couldn't decide to track a conversation with your pupils while simultaneously being too impaired to understand it. The eyes either followed or they didn't, and Agnes's eyes followed.

And once Tammy let herself see that, she saw Harry.

Harry Zielinski in 114A, diagnosed with moderate-to-severe dementia, wheelchair-bound, asked about his dead wife Eleanor at least four times per shift. He answered Dr. Prasad's orientation questions wrong every two weeks when Prasad came through — wrong day, wrong month, wrong president, the answers wandering through a timeline that didn't exist. He needed help eating. He needed help transferring. He was, according to every assessment in his chart, exactly the kind of resident who required total care and would continue declining until he didn't require anything at all.

But Tammy had worked evenings with Harry for years, and she'd noticed something she hadn't thought much about until now. His confusion wasn't constant. It was variable. He was most confused during Dr. Prasad's visits, during the state survey, during Cliff's quarterly walk-throughs. He was least confused in the evenings, during the quiet hours when the building thinned out and the day staff went home. She'd seen him in the hallway at nine-thirty one night, supposedly on his way to the bathroom, and he'd looked at her — right at her, steady, no confusion in it — and said "Good night, Tammy" in a voice that had nothing wrong with it. Then he'd gone into his room and closed the door, which was a level of motor coordination that didn't match his chart.

She'd let that go too. She let everything go. That was the skill she'd spent years developing — the ability to notice things and then not do anything about them, because doing something meant reporting to Cliff, who would either panic and generate paperwork that went nowhere or ignore her entirely, and either way the thing she'd noticed would continue happening and she'd have wasted her time and whatever small amount of institutional goodwill a CNA accumulated by keeping her mouth shut.

But the observations were adding up. Carmen's changed behavior. The count discrepancies. Agnes's eyes. Harry's variable confusion. Lola Fernandez, who chattered warmly and seemed confused but who Tammy had once seen, during a fire drill six months ago, move with calm efficiency to the exit — not the shuffling uncertainty of someone disoriented but the purposeful movement of someone who knew exactly where she was going and needed to get there. Tammy had been helping another resident at the time and only caught it from the corner of her eye. She'd dismissed it. Adrenaline. Even confused people could surprise you in emergencies.

She didn't dismiss it anymore.

Tammy didn't go to Cliff. She didn't go to anyone. She'd learned a long time ago that the only person in this building who would take her observations seriously was herself. She decided to watch.

Not actively — she didn't have the time or the energy for a stakeout or whatever cops called it. She just kept doing her job and kept her eyes open, which was something she'd always done anyway. The difference was that now she was directing her attention instead of diffusing it. She was looking at specific people. She was noting times. She was paying attention to which rooms Carmen visited and for how long and whether the door was open or closed.

On a Thursday in late October, Tammy finished her shift at eleven. She clocked out, said good night to the overnight nurse — a travel nurse named Priya who was competent but unfamiliar with the residents and would be gone in six weeks — and walked to her car. The parking lot was dark. It had been dark since five. The air was cold enough that her breath showed, and the bare trees in the courtyard looked like the kind of thing someone would photograph in black and white and call art.

She sat in her car. She turned the heater on. She should have driven home. There was nothing keeping her here except the studio apartment that smelled like phantom cats and the silence she'd go back to and the twelve minutes of driving that separated this parking lot from that silence. She sat.

She wasn't doing anything. She was sitting in her car in a parking lot, and that wasn't suspicious or strange; people sat in cars in parking lots all the time. She checked her phone. Nothing. Kevin hadn't texted in three weeks, which was fine because they had nothing to text about, but the absence of his name on her screen was its own kind of presence, the way a missing tooth was a presence. She scrolled through nothing. She looked at the building.

Lakeview at night. She knew every window. The nurse's station glow. Room 110's light, because Mrs. Okafor's overhead stayed on around the clock — nobody had asked Mrs. Okafor if she wanted it off, and Mrs. Okafor couldn't tell them. A few other windows. The building settled into its overnight shape, the shape she'd memorized from all those years of leaving it at this hour — most lights off, the central station holding steady, the hallways dimmed to their half-brightness.

Eleven-fifteen. Eleven-thirty. She should go home. She was tired. Her lower back ached from the lifting, and her knees had that deep, specific soreness that ibuprofen touched but didn't fix. She opened her car door to get out and drive home.

She didn't get out. She pulled the door shut again. She didn't know why. Or she did know why, and she wasn't ready to call it what it was.

Eleven-forty-five. She watched Priya walk past the nurse's station window, chart in hand. Normal. Midnight. The building was still. She could see the faint glow of Devon's phone at the aide's desk near the entrance — he'd be asleep within the hour if he wasn't already.

Twelve-fifteen. Her heater ticked. Her back hurt. She was a forty-three-year-old woman sitting alone in a parking lot in the dark because she didn't want to go back to an apartment that smelled like cats and she couldn't stop thinking about the way Agnes Chen's eyes tracked conversations she wasn't supposed to understand.

Twelve-thirty.

A light came on at the far end of Wing A. The TV room. It was the farthest common area from the nurse's station, around the L-shaped corner, not visible from the aide's desk or the central station. Tammy knew this because she knew the geometry of its blind spots the way she knew the geometry of her own apartment — not because she'd studied it but because she'd lived inside it long enough that the knowledge was physical.

The TV room light was on. At twelve-thirty in the morning. In a wing where every resident was supposedly asleep or supposed to be, according to the care plans and the overnight check schedule that Devon was supposed to follow and didn't.

Tammy watched the window from her car. The light stayed on. She could see movement — not clearly, not faces, just the suggestion of shapes behind the glass, which meant someone was in there. More than one person, maybe, though she couldn't be sure from this distance and angle.

She stayed for another ten minutes. The light stayed on. The shapes moved.

She started her car. She drove home. The twelve minutes passed the way twelve minutes passed when your mind was somewhere else — she registered the turns and the stop signs and the left onto her street without actually seeing any of it. She parked. She went upstairs. The cat smell greeted her like it always did, and she stood inside the door with her jacket still on and her keys in her hand.

She didn't know what she'd seen. She didn't know what it meant. She knew the TV room light was on at twelve-thirty in the morning, and she knew that Agnes Chen's eyes followed conversations, and she knew that Harry Zielinski said good night in a voice that belonged to a man who didn't have dementia, and she knew that Carmen Reyes was staying late and closing doors and explaining things with the smooth readiness of a person who had practiced the explanation before she needed it.

Tammy took off her jacket. She hung it on the hook Kevin had installed four apartments and a marriage ago, the one piece of hardware that had followed her out of that life. She went to bed. She didn't sleep for a long time.

She'd go back. Not tomorrow — she had a day off, and she needed the day off, her body needed it. But after. She'd go back and she'd watch again, and she'd keep watching until the pieces she'd collected added up to something she could name. She wasn't going to tell Cliff. Cliff would do nothing useful with the information, and even if he did, she'd be the one who'd reported it, and fifteen years in this building had taught her what happened to people who reported things: they became the problem. Marcus Webb had reported the understaffing, and Marcus Webb didn't work here anymore, and the understaffing was worse than ever.

She'd watch. She was good at watching. It was maybe the only thing she was still good at, the one skill that fifteen years and a divorce and sixteen fifty an hour hadn't worn down to nothing. She could see what was in front of her, and she could remember what she'd seen, and she could wait.

The water stain on the ceiling looked the same as it always did. Tammy stared at it until her eyes adjusted to the dark, and then she stared at it some more, because the dark didn't change what was there. It just made you work harder to see it.


# Chapter 15

Earl woke at 5:14 the way he'd woken at 5:14 for fifty years. The plant had set that clock in him, and nothing since — not retirement, not Maryann dying, not this place — had reset it. His body was a shift worker's body. It didn't know how to sleep past the whistle.

The room was dark except for the bar of hallway light under the door. Burt breathed in the other bed, a shallow wet sound like a straw pulled through the last of something. Since the fall, Burt slept fourteen, fifteen hours a day. When he was awake he spoke in Hungarian — to his dead wife, to people Earl couldn't see. The doctors said the concussion had accelerated what was already happening. The word they used was "trajectory." Earl understood the word. He'd seen enough trajectories. Steel cooling too fast, a man's back giving out at fifty-two, a local folding after the last round of layoffs. Things headed one direction.

He lay still for ten minutes, listening. The overnight nurse — not Carmen, the one named Janet who walked heavy in her sneakers — passed the door at 5:21. A call bell rang down the hall. The heating system ticked in the walls, radiators warming up for the morning, the building's pipes expanding with a sound like knuckles cracking.

Then the cough came.

It started low, below the ribs, a pressure that built before it broke. Earl sat up and pressed the handkerchief to his mouth and let it work through him. Deep, wet, a sound like something loose and heavy turning over in his chest. His eyes watered. His shoulders seized. He coughed until the muscles between his ribs burned, and then he coughed past that, because the cough didn't care about pain — it had its own schedule, its own clock, and it ran until it was done.

Burt didn't stir. Six months ago, Burt would have said something — "Earl, you all right?" or muttered in English about the noise. Now nothing. The room absorbed the sound the way it absorbed everything: the smell of disinfectant and old skin, the hum of Burt's oxygen concentrator, the thinning light of late October coming through a window that faced the parking lot.

Earl got up and went to the bathroom. He closed the door and turned on the light and held the handkerchief under the fluorescent bar.

White. Clean.

He checked the folds, tilting the cloth the way he used to check a weld seam, looking for the stain that meant the joint was bleeding. Nothing. He folded the handkerchief into quarters and put it in the pocket of the pants he'd laid on the towel bar the night before. Khakis, a flannel shirt, the brown jacket that still fit because he hadn't gained a pound in thirty years — just lost them, slowly, the way you lost everything in here.

It was outing day. First Thursday of the month.

He'd set everything out before lights-out: the clothes, the jacket, and in the jacket's inside pocket, the greeting card. He checked it now, fingers finding the envelope by feel. A generic card — "Thinking of You" — bought from the rack in the lobby near the front desk, where Lakeview sold greeting cards and puzzle books and small stuffed animals for visiting grandchildren. Inside the card, Earl had written "Love, Dad" and nothing else, because Denise wouldn't see it and the person who would didn't need a message. Taped flat against the inside of the card, covered by a second piece of cardstock Earl had cut from a cereal box, were forty-six alprazolam tablets and twenty oxycodone. Each pill fixed to a strip of medical tape, arranged in rows like a sheet of postage stamps. Agnes had counted them. Earl had taped them. The work had taken twenty minutes the night before, seated on the edge of his bed while Burt muttered about Budapest.

He dressed. He shaved with the electric razor the facility required — no blades, fall risk, as if he hadn't handled a straight razor for fifty years without cutting himself. The razor buzzed against his jaw and he looked at himself in the mirror: an old man, tall but stooped now, the stoop getting worse each month. His face was thinner than it had been in September. He could see that. The cheekbones more prominent, the skin looser along the jaw. Eighty-one. He'd been bigger once. Two hundred and ten pounds on the shop floor, arms that could hold a beam steady while the crane operator repositioned. Now one-sixty, maybe less. He didn't weigh himself. He didn't want the number.

He brushed his teeth, rinsed, spat. Went back into the room and sat in the chair by the window and waited.

---

The van left at ten. Phil, the maintenance man who doubled as driver, had it running in the circle drive with the heat on. Phil was fifty-eight and had a bad knee from a fall off a scaffold in '09 — not a football story, not a war story, just a man who climbed a scaffold for fifteen dollars an hour and came down wrong. He walked with a careful, testing limp that worsened in the cold, and he kept AM sports talk on the radio at a volume just above silence, a murmur of scores and opinions that filled the van the way pipe noise filled the halls.

Earl was the only passenger. He climbed into the second row, holding the grab bar with his left hand. Phil waited, watching in the rearview, but didn't offer help. Phil had driven Earl to the cemetery seven times and had learned early that Earl didn't want a hand up.

"Same place?" Phil said.

"Yeah."

They pulled out of the circle drive and onto Route 27. The trees along the road had turned, but the color was already going — the maples brown and bare in patches, the oaks holding yellow leaves that rattled in a wind Earl couldn't feel through the van's windows. The sky was white-gray, the kind of November sky that sat on central New Jersey like a lid. He watched the strip malls go by. A nail salon, a check-cashing place, a laundromat with a hand-lettered sign in the window: WASH & FOLD $1.25/LB. He'd used a place like that once, after Maryann went to the hospital the last time and he was doing his own laundry for the first time in forty years. He'd stood at the counter with a garbage bag of clothes and the woman had weighed it and told him the price and he'd paid without speaking because the act of paying someone to wash his clothes — an act that meant his wife was dying and his life was changing in ways he couldn't reverse — had closed his throat. Nine dollars and seventy-five cents. He remembered the amount. He remembered everything in amounts.

The pension was $2,340 a month. Medicaid took $2,290 as his patient responsibility — his share of the nursing home bill, computed by a formula he'd read three times and understood perfectly and considered a legalized theft. That left fifty dollars. His personal needs allowance. Fifty dollars a month for a man who'd worked forty-one years, paid into the local, walked the picket line in '82 when the company tried to break the contract, and spent thirty years building a pension that was supposed to mean something. Fifty dollars bought toothpaste, the electric razor heads, stamps, the greeting cards from the lobby rack. It did not buy flowers. It did not buy anything for his grandchildren. It did not buy a goddamn thing for Denise.

Denise was forty-four. Home health aide in Trenton, $14.80 an hour, no benefits she could afford to use. Three kids: Keisha, thirteen and on the honor roll; Jaylen, ten, who'd been suspended twice for fighting and once for talking back to a teacher who told him to sit down when he was already sitting; and Amara, six, who started first grade in September and came home every day with a painting that Denise taped to the kitchen wall because the refrigerator magnets were broken. Denise worked six days a week. Her car was a 2014 Altima with 180,000 miles. Her rent was $1,100 for a two-bedroom in a building where the hallway smelled like cooking grease and someone else's weed.

The $400 a month Earl sent her — that was the difference. That was after-school for Jaylen and Amara, because Keisha was old enough to get herself home but the younger two needed supervision until Denise finished her shift at five. Without the $400, Jaylen walked home alone and sat in the apartment watching TV until his mother got there, and Amara went to a neighbor's whose name Denise wasn't sure of.

Earl had told Denise the money was a VA supplemental benefit. Aid and Attendance, he'd called it — a real program, which made the lie better. He'd looked it up at the facility library, on the one computer in the activity room that still connected to the internet. He'd read the eligibility requirements, the payment amounts, the application process, all of it, so that if Denise ever asked a specific question he could answer it with the precision of a man who'd actually filed the paperwork. She hadn't asked. She'd called and said, "Daddy, are you sure? Four hundred a month?" and he'd said, "The government owes me. Korea." And she'd said, "Okay," and he'd heard in her voice the relief of a woman who'd been counting coins for years and just found out she could stop counting for a while.

She couldn't stop counting. He knew that. Four hundred a month was a dent, not a fix. But it was a dent in the right direction, and Earl had spent his whole career understanding that you didn't fix things all at once — you organized, you built pressure, you moved the line an inch. An inch was sometimes enough. An inch meant Jaylen had somewhere to go after school that wasn't an empty apartment. An inch meant Amara got picked up by someone Denise had met.

Phil turned onto Raritan Road. The cemetery was another fifteen minutes.

"Mind if we stop?" Earl said. He'd said it the same way every month. Phil knew the stop.

"ShopRite?"

"Yeah."

Phil pulled into the lot. Earl got out and went in. The flower bucket was by the entrance, between the shopping carts and a display of pumpkins marked down to $2.99. He picked up a bunch of carnations in cellophane — white and pink, the stems cut short, a sprig of baby's breath that was already browning at the edges. $4.49. He paid with a five at the self-checkout and put the change in his jacket pocket with the fifty-one cents already there. He walked back to the van, holding the flowers low against his hip, and climbed in without help.

Phil pulled out. The sports radio murmured about the Giants' offensive line. Earl held the flowers on his lap and watched the road.

---

The cemetery was off a county road south of New Brunswick, set back from the street behind a stone wall that had been pointed and repointed so many times the mortar was thicker than the stones. The grounds were maintained by a crew that came Tuesdays — Earl knew because Maryann's plot was always trimmer on Wednesdays, the grass clipped close, the edges clean. By Thursday the first day's growth had softened things. He preferred it.

Phil parked the van in the small lot near the maintenance shed. Three other cars — visitors, or maybe the office staff. And Marcus's Corolla, dark blue, parked at the end of the row facing out.

Earl didn't look at it directly. He sat in the second row with the flowers on his lap and waited.

Marcus knew the routine. Within a minute his Corolla pulled forward and stopped alongside the van, driver's window to Earl's window, close enough that the side mirrors nearly touched. Marcus was wearing a gray hoodie, no jacket, which was too little for the weather but Marcus always ran warm.

"Hey," Marcus said through his open window.

Earl cranked the van's window down — an old van, no power anything — and the cold came in, raw, the kind of late-October cold that meant November was already here in everything but the calendar. He reached into his jacket pocket and pulled out the greeting card envelope and passed it through. Marcus took it and slid it under his thigh without looking at it. They'd done this seven times. The motions were set.

Marcus reached across to his passenger seat and handed a white envelope through the gap between the vehicles. Business-size, sealed. Four hundred dollars in twenties. Earl put it in his inside jacket pocket, the one the greeting card had been in. The pockets traded contents. An empty one filled, a full one emptied.

"How you doing?" Marcus asked.

"Fine."

"Keisha still getting those grades?"

Earl looked at him. Marcus knew about the grandkids because Marcus asked about them every month, and every month Earl gave him the same amount of information: enough to answer, not enough to invite more. "She's doing all right."

"Good." Marcus drummed his fingers on the wheel. He had something else to say — Earl could see it in the way he held his jaw, slightly open, the next sentence waiting. But Marcus looked at Earl's face and decided against it. Smart. Marcus was smarter than the others gave him credit for.

"I'll see you," Earl said. He cranked the window back up. Marcus put his window up and pulled out of the lot, and Earl watched the Corolla turn onto the county road and disappear behind the stone wall. Phil glanced in the rearview but said nothing. A greeting card passed between vehicles at a cemetery — it could be anything. Phil had no reason to wonder.

"Take your time," Phil said. He was already settling back with the thermos. He'd wait. He always waited. Earl figured Phil used the cemetery runs as a break — an hour of sitting in a warm van listening to the radio while the building didn't need anything from him. Earl didn't blame him. The building needed too much from everyone.

Earl took the flowers and got out. The cold was worse standing in it. The leaves on the cemetery's oaks had gone amber and were falling in the wind, scraping across the pavement in clusters, collecting along the curbs and against the headstones like small drifts. He walked carefully — the asphalt had cracks and his knees weren't what they were — but his gait was steady. He'd been walking with bad knees since sixty-two, and he knew how to distribute his weight.

The cough came as he crossed the lot — a single bark followed by a tightness that he swallowed down. He cleared his throat and walked into the cemetery.

Maryann was in Section C, fifth row, nine stones in from the path. He knew the count the way he knew the count of everything: steps from the van to her plot (sixty-four), headstones between the path and hers (eight others, then hers), years since she'd died (four), years they'd been married (forty-three). The headstone was gray granite, modest — he'd picked it himself, the cheapest one that wasn't flimsy, $1,800 installed, paid in three installments that he'd finished six months after the funeral. MARYANN ELOISE WASHINGTON. 1944–2022. BELOVED WIFE AND MOTHER.

He'd wanted to add something else but couldn't think of what. The woman at the monument company had suggested "Forever In Our Hearts" and Earl had looked at her until she stopped talking. Maryann was in the ground, not in his heart. His heart was a muscle that pumped blood. She was here, in this dirt, in this box, and the fact of that — the plain, immovable, inarguable fact of it — was what he came to visit. Not a feeling. A location.

He took the old flowers from the bronze vase mounted on the stone's base — last month's carnations, dried to brown paper, the cellophane faded — and put the new ones in. The vase had an inch of rain-blackened water in it. He didn't change it. The flowers would sit in the old water for a month and nobody would know and it wouldn't matter. He put the dead flowers in the cellophane wrapper from the new ones and set the bundle on the ground to throw away on the way out.

Then he stood there.

"Keisha made honor roll again," he said. His voice was quiet but not a whisper. He talked to Maryann at normal volume, the way he'd talk to someone standing next to him. "Second quarter straight. Denise said she wants to do some science program at the community college in the spring, the Saturday one. It costs something. I'll figure it out."

The wind moved the carnations in the vase. A leaf scraped across the base of the headstone and caught in the dead grass at the plot's edge.

"Jaylen got in a fight. Not a bad one — he pushed a kid who said something about his shoes. Denise had to go in and talk to the vice principal. He's not a bad kid. He's ten. You remember what I was like at ten." He paused. Maryann would have said something here — she always had something to say, always had a story or a correction or a piece of context that made the thing he'd said larger than he'd meant it. She'd have said, "Earl, you were worse than Jaylen and you know it," and she'd have been right, and he'd have looked at her and she'd have known he agreed without him saying so.

"Amara drew me a picture. Denise mailed it. It's a fish." He almost smiled. "A purple fish with legs. I don't know what it's supposed to be. She wrote my name on it. She wrote GRANDPA and the R is backward."

He coughed. Not a bad one — just the chest reminding him it was there, the way a foreman tapped you on the shoulder before he said something you didn't want to hear.

"Denise is tired," he said. "She's always tired. She doesn't say it but I can hear it. She sounds like you did, the last year. Not sick-tired. Life-tired." He stopped. He didn't want to say more about that. He didn't want to compare Denise to Maryann's last year, because Maryann's last year had an ending he could see from here — the headstone, the dirt, the dried flowers on the ground.

He stood with his hands at his sides. The cemetery was quiet. A maintenance truck idled somewhere on the far side, near the newer section, and a woman in a red coat was walking between headstones three rows over, but she was far enough away to be a shape, not a person. The oaks filtered the gray light, and the shadows of bare branches moved on the ground like something being drawn and erased, drawn and erased.

"I'm all right," he told her. He said it the way he said "Fine" to Marcus: the word that ended the conversation. Maryann would have known it was a lie. She always knew when he was lying, because he lied so rarely that the sound of it was distinctive — like a machine running rough, a noise you noticed because the machine usually ran clean.

He didn't tell her about the ring. He'd never told her about the ring, not in four years of monthly visits, and at first it was because the ring hadn't started yet and then it was because he didn't know how to explain it to a woman who'd believed in rules the way she'd believed in church — not because she was naive but because the rules were supposed to protect people like them, and breaking them was a concession she'd have struggled with. He thought she'd understand eventually. He thought she'd look at Keisha's science program and Jaylen's after-school and Amara's purple fish and understand. But he wasn't sure, and so he didn't say it.

He didn't tell her about the cough.
