He picked up the dead flowers, nodded once at the headstone — a small motion, barely visible, the way you acknowledged someone you'd see again next month — and walked back toward the parking lot. Sixty-four steps. The envelope in his pocket. The flowers in the trash can by the maintenance shed. His knees holding.

Phil had the engine running. The radio was on a commercial. Earl climbed in and pulled the door shut and Phil backed out without asking how it went, because Phil had learned that too.

They drove back. Earl watched New Jersey go by through the window and thought about the money order he'd fill out tomorrow — the post office in the lobby had the forms, and the facility's outgoing mail went out Monday mornings. Four hundred dollars. Payable to Denise Washington. In the memo line he'd write "VA A&A Nov" the way he wrote it every month: an abbreviation of a benefit he'd never applied for, a code that meant his daughter could pay for her children's afternoons.

---

That night, after dinner — pork chop with applesauce, mashed potatoes from a box, green beans that had been green once — Earl lay in bed and listened to Burt breathe.

The room was dim. The hallway light came under the door in its permanent stripe. Burt's oxygen concentrator hummed at a pitch Earl's ears had absorbed into background, the way they'd absorbed the plant's compressors forty years ago — you stopped hearing it, and then you retired, and the silence was the loudest thing you'd ever experienced.

He thought about the envelope in his jacket, hanging on the hook behind the bathroom door. He thought about the greeting card somewhere in Marcus's car, its contents inventoried and sorted and ready for distribution to people whose insurance had failed them or never existed. He thought about Agnes, who kept the records, and Harry, who kept the system running, and Lola, who watched the door, and Burt, who didn't know anymore what his pills were being used for. He thought about Carmen, who'd crossed a line to protect them and would cross another line eventually — he could see it in her face, the way he used to see a man on the shop floor deciding whether to file a grievance. Some people moved toward the right thing slowly, testing each step. Carmen was testing.

The cough came.

He'd been waiting for it. It always came at night, worse than the morning, as if his chest saved it up during the day and released it in the dark. He rolled onto his side and pressed the handkerchief to his mouth and coughed until his ribs ached and his throat felt scraped raw and his eyes streamed. The sound was bad. Wet. Something deep in the left lung shifted and rattled, a noise he could feel in his back, between the shoulder blades, as if someone were shaking a box of broken parts inside him.

He coughed for two minutes. Maybe three. When it stopped, it didn't stop cleanly — it tapered off, each cough weaker than the last, his body losing the force to produce them. He lay on his side with his mouth open, breathing in small gulps, his hand closed around the handkerchief.

Burt slept through it. Burt slept through everything now.

Earl sat up. He reached for the lamp on the nightstand — a small lamp with a forty-watt bulb that made everything in the room the color of weak tea — and turned it on.

He opened the handkerchief.

White. Still white.

He checked every fold. He held it six inches from his face and examined each surface under the lamp's yellow light. He was looking for pink. For rust. For the stain that would mean the cough had crossed from nuisance to fact, from something he could ignore to something his body wouldn't let him. Hemoptysis — he'd looked up the word on the activity room computer, the same computer where he'd researched the VA benefit. He'd read the causes. He knew what the cough could be. He knew the names for it. He didn't want the name. He wanted the handkerchief to stay white, because as long as it stayed white he could keep not knowing, and as long as he didn't know he could keep working, and as long as he kept working the money went to Denise and the pills went to Marcus and the whole system — fragile, illegal, necessary — kept turning.

If he went to Dr. Prasad and said the word "cough," a chain of events would begin that he couldn't control. A chest X-ray. Blood work. A CT scan, probably, at the hospital, which meant a van ride and a waiting room and a technician asking him to hold still while a machine looked inside him for the thing he'd decided not to look for. And if the machine found something — a mass, a shadow, a spot, whatever the words were for the thing growing in his chest — then his medications would change. New drugs for the new problem. His current prescriptions altered or discontinued. The oxycodone he contributed to the ring's supply, the alprazolam, the tramadol — reassigned, recalculated, possibly cut. Agnes would know immediately. Harry would have to restructure the inventory. The operation would lose a supplier. And then what? Denise would lose the four hundred dollars, because Earl's usefulness was the only reason the money existed.

He sat on the edge of the bed with the handkerchief in his lap and his bare feet on the cold linoleum. His ankles were thin. His feet were ashy and the nails were thick and yellow — fungal, probably, the kind of thing that happened when you were old and your blood didn't reach your feet the way it used to. Carmen trimmed them for him every two weeks, kneeling on the floor with clippers, not flinching, not making a face. He'd never asked her to. She just did it.

He thought about telling someone. He ran through the list the way he'd run through a grievance chain: tell Carmen, who'd want him to see the doctor. Tell Harry, who'd calculate the operational impact. Tell Agnes, who'd look at him with her pharmacist's eyes and know more from the sound of the cough than the X-ray would show. Tell Denise, who'd drive up from Trenton and sit in the chair by the window and cry, and then what — what would she do with the information? What could she do? She'd worry. She'd add his dying to the list of things she managed on fourteen dollars and eighty cents an hour. He'd give her that weight and take nothing off the pile.

He put the handkerchief back in his pocket. The jacket hung on the hook. The envelope was in the pocket. Tomorrow: the post office form, the money order, the memo line, the outgoing mail. Next month: the same. The month after: the same. As long as the handkerchief stayed white.

He thought about Maryann. He'd told her he was all right, and she would have known he was lying, and she would have sat with him the way she sat with him through forty-three years of his silences — not pushing, not waiting for him to speak, just present, her hip against his on the couch, her hand sometimes on his arm, the warmth of a person who understood that words were not his instrument and didn't penalize him for it.

She'd been the talker. She'd talked to the neighbors and the mailman and the women at church and the teachers at Denise's school and the clerk at the hardware store where Earl bought his supplies. She'd talked to Denise about everything — boys, grades, college, the pregnancy at nineteen that wasn't the plan but became the life. She'd talked to Earl about his work, his union fights, his anger at the company, his pride in the local, his fear, at the end, that none of it had mattered, that the pension was shrinking and the plant was closing and thirty years of organizing had built a house that someone else was tearing down.

She'd listened too. But mostly she'd talked, and mostly he'd listened, and the arrangement had worked because it was honest — she needed to speak the way he needed not to, and in the space between them something got said.

Now he talked to her grave once a month, and said more to that granite slab than he said to any living person in the weeks between visits, and even there he held back — no ring, no cough, no fear. Just the grandkids. Just the facts of their small lives, offered to a woman who would have done more with them than he could.

Earl turned off the lamp. He lay back down and pulled the blanket to his chest and closed his eyes. The cough was still there — he could feel it waiting, a heaviness behind his sternum, not ready to surface but not gone either. It would come again. It came every night now. And every morning he would check, and the handkerchief would be white or it wouldn't, and the day he saw color on the cloth would be the day the clock he'd been ignoring started ticking loud enough for everyone to hear.

Not today. Not today.

Burt shifted in the other bed, making a small sound that wasn't a word in any language. The concentrator hummed. The radiator ticked. Outside the window the parking lot lights cast orange on the ceiling, and somewhere in the building a call bell rang and was answered, and the night kept going the way nights kept going in places where people were old and couldn't leave — slowly, and with sounds that nobody was awake to notice.

Earl lay in the dark with his hand on his chest and breathed carefully, the way you carried something that might break.


# Chapter 16

Agnes didn't touch people. That was the first thing Carmen had learned about her, back in September when she was still figuring out which residents bit and which ones just looked like they might — Agnes kept a pharmacist's distance from everyone, hands either sorting pill bottles or folded in her lap or gripping the armrests of her chair like she was about to stand but had decided against it. Agnes's hands were instruments, not social tools.

So when Agnes's fingers closed around Carmen's wrist at the sub-station just after eleven, Carmen stopped charting mid-word.

Agnes was in performance mode. Slack face, unfocused eyes, the institutional shuffle of someone who'd had a stroke and never fully recovered. She looked like she was wandering — an 11 PM bathroom trip, maybe, or one of those aimless hall-walks the genuinely confused residents did when their sleep cycles scrambled. But her grip was wrong. Too exact, too located. Two fingers on the inside of Carmen's wrist, right over the pulse point, the way a pharmacist takes hold of a patient who's about to do something stupid with a prescription.

"Mrs. Chen?" Carmen said, loud enough for Devon to hear. Devon was asleep at the main station with his phone propped against the keyboard, a hunting podcast droning through his earbuds. "You need something? Bathroom?"

Agnes's eyes flicked down the hall toward Wing A. Her grip tightened once, then released. She turned and walked toward Harry's room, her slippers scuffing the linoleum in that halting, post-stroke rhythm she'd perfected — a shuffle so convincing that Dr. Prasad had renewed her mobility restrictions twice without questioning them.

Carmen followed.

Room 114A was at the far end of Wing A, single occupancy because Harry had outlasted three roommates and the facility hadn't bothered to fill the second bed. The door was open four inches. Agnes pushed through without knocking. Carmen stepped in behind her and pulled it shut.

Harry was standing between his bed and the window. Not holding anything, not leaning — standing with his weight evenly distributed, his shoulders squared under the faded blue cardigan that he wore to every meeting, buttoned wrong tonight, one button off so the whole thing hung crooked on his thin frame. He was eighty-four years old and he looked it in a way that the performance usually hid — the pouches under his eyes, the scabbed knuckles where he'd knocked them on the bed rail, the white stubble he'd missed shaving because his right hand wasn't as steady as it used to be and that wasn't part of any act.

He was holding the shared prepaid phone.

"Read it," he said. Real voice. Short, flat, final.

Carmen took the phone. The screen was cracked across one corner — it had been cracked since August, since Earl dropped it getting out of the van at the cemetery. A text message, two hours old, from a number she didn't recognize. Not one of the ring's buyer contacts, not any number she'd seen in Agnes's notebooks.

*can do 200 oxy 5s and 100 bars wkly. same price. lmk about thurs*

Carmen read it once. Then again, because the numbers were wrong. They had to be wrong. The ring moved eighty, maybe ninety oxycodone a month. A hundred in a flush month when Dr. Prasad added a new script. Two hundred a week was eight hundred a month. And bars — a hundred Xanax a week, four hundred a month.

"He sent it to our phone," Agnes said. She was sitting on the radiator cover beneath the window, her spine very straight, her hands pressed flat against her thighs. Not folded, not sorting. Pressed, like she was holding them down. "He meant to send it to the buyer. He sent it here instead."

"Two hundred a week," Harry said. He wasn't looking at Carmen. He was looking at the photograph taped above his closet — the landscaping truck with ZIELINSKI & SON painted on the door in green letters. "We have never moved two hundred in a month."

"He is sourcing elsewhere," Agnes said. "Or he is promising volume he cannot deliver. Either scenario is a problem I did not agree to."

The radiator under Agnes clicked and hissed. Late October in central New Jersey, and Lakeview's heating system ran like it did everything else — unevenly, with long gaps between effort. The room smelled like Ben-Gay and the metallic dryness of forced air that wasn't quite warm enough to be comfortable.

"When did this come in?" Carmen asked.

"Nine fourteen," Harry said. "Agnes was checking the phone for a delivery confirmation from last week. She found this instead."

Carmen set the phone on the nightstand, next to Harry's water cup and the crossword puzzle book he never opened. Her fingers were tingling, which was stupid — her body reacting like this was a medical emergency, the adrenaline dump that happened when a fall alarm went off or a resident coded. But nobody was coding. Nobody was hurt. A text message on a cracked prepaid phone, and the numbers on the screen meant the thing she'd spent months trying to hold together had already come apart.

"Call him," Harry said.

Carmen picked the phone back up and dialed Marcus's number. It rang three times.

"Yo." She could hear a car engine, road noise, a bass line from his stereo cut short as he turned it down. "What's up?"

"It's Carmen. Harry needs you to come in tonight."

A pause. The engine noise smoothed out — he was coasting, slowing down. "Tonight? I got — it's almost midnight, I can't just —"

"One o'clock." Harry held out his hand. Carmen gave him the phone. "One o'clock, Marcus. Use the loading dock. Carmen will leave it unlocked."

"Harry, come on, man, what's —"

"One o'clock," Harry said, and ended the call.

He sat on the edge of his bed. The mattress groaned under him and his knees cracked — both of them, a sound like someone stepping on dry sticks. He set the phone down. He looked at Agnes. Agnes looked back at him, and something passed between them that Carmen couldn't read, something built over thirty months of midnight meetings and pill counts and arguments about expiration dates while the building slept around them.

"I will not sort for a reseller," Agnes said. "I told him that in October and I meant it."

"I know," Harry said.

"I will not touch product that ends up at a party in Edison where some twenty-year-old takes three pills because nobody explained opioid naivety to him. That is not what I do."

Harry nodded once. He didn't say anything else. Carmen realized this was his anger — not the shouting kind, not even the cold-lecture kind she'd seen him use on her back in September when she'd first confronted him. This was the accountant's version. A ledger that had been balanced and someone had taken a pen to the numbers without permission. Harry's fury was in the silence between his sentences, in the precise way he set the phone down, in the fact that he was looking at the photograph of his grandson's truck instead of at the two women in his room.

"Get the others," Harry said. "Lola and Earl. Not Burt."

---

Lola arrived in the TV room wearing her housecoat and the gold crucifix she never took off, the one that caught the fluorescent light and threw a small bright point against the wall when she moved. Her tremor was bad tonight — her left hand shaking visibly against her thigh, the Parkinson's worsened by whatever Agnes had told her when she woke her up. She sat in the vinyl chair closest to the door, the lookout's chair, but she wasn't watching the hallway. She was watching Harry.

Earl came in behind her, tall and stooped in his bathrobe, clearing his throat the way he always did before speaking — buying time, settling whatever was in his lungs. His cough had been worse this month. Carmen had charted it twice and he'd waved her off both times. He sat down and folded his hands over his knees and didn't ask why they were here.

Carmen had unlocked the loading dock door at 12:40 and waited in the cold, her breath smoking in the parking lot light. October had turned hard this week — the courtyard's three trees had dropped most of their leaves, and the wind came off the highway with a flat, metallic edge. When Marcus's sedan pulled into the far end of the lot, headlights off, she felt something in her chest that wasn't anger and wasn't fear but sat between them like a coin balanced on its edge.

Marcus came through the loading dock in a hoodie and jeans, moving the way he always moved in the building — quiet, deliberate, the CNA's muscle memory of navigating a sleeping facility. He smelled like the cold outside and something chemical, hand sanitizer or the cleaning spray his car always reeked of. He saw Carmen's face and stopped.

"What?" he said.

"TV room."

She walked him down the hall. He didn't ask questions. When they reached the TV room, Harry was sitting at the card table in his wheelchair — the performance wheelchair, the one he used as a prop, though Carmen noticed he hadn't stood up once since she'd gathered everyone. Maybe his knees were bad tonight. Maybe something else.

Marcus saw the group — Harry at the card table, Agnes standing by the window, Lola in her lookout chair, Earl in the far corner — and his face changed. Not guilt, not yet. Recognition. He knew what this was.

Harry set the prepaid phone on the card table and slid it across to Marcus. The phone scraped on the laminate. Marcus didn't pick it up.

"You sent that to the wrong number," Harry said.

Marcus looked at the phone. Then at Harry. Then at Agnes, who was watching him the way she watched medication interactions — with clinical attention and no sentiment.

"Shit," Marcus said quietly.

"Two hundred oxycodone a week," Harry said. "A hundred alprazolam. Weekly. We have never moved that volume in a month, Marcus. We have never moved half that in a month."

"Harry —"

"You told us you'd walk away from the volume buyer. Three weeks ago, in this room, you sat where you're sitting and you said you'd decline."

Marcus pulled out the chair across from Harry and sat down. He rubbed his face with both hands, the way he did when he was buying time, when the joke he wanted to make wasn't going to land and he knew it. When he dropped his hands, his expression had shifted — not apologetic, not defensive. Something harder.

"I did decline," Marcus said. "For about a week. Then he came back with better numbers and I thought about it and yeah, I took it."

"Without telling us," Harry said.

"Without telling you. Yeah."

The room was quiet except for the radiator in the corner and the distant infomercial still playing in the hallway, a woman's voice describing a vegetable chopper with the intensity of someone narrating a moon landing.

"Where are you getting two hundred a week?" Agnes asked. Her voice was compressed, fast — more facts per sentence, less air between them. Her stress mode. "We do not produce that. We have never produced that. What are you promising that you cannot source from us?"

"I've got other sources," Marcus said. "A guy at a pain clinic in Paterson. Another one through — look, it doesn't matter where. The point is I'm not taking more from you than I was before. Your end doesn't change."

"Our end," Harry repeated.

"Your supply. I'm still moving the same amount from Lakeview. The rest is separate."

"The rest is separate," Agnes said. She said it the way she'd say a medication name — precisely, clinically, with the full weight of someone who understood what the words actually meant. "You are running a distribution operation on top of ours. Using the same phone. The same contacts. The same car you drive here every Tuesday."

"It's a different phone," Marcus said. "Usually. I fucked up. I sent one text to the wrong —"

"One text," Harry said. "One text to the phone that sits in my nightstand. One text that Agnes read. One text that connects your volume operation to our operation to this building to these people." He pointed, a precise gesture, his index finger moving from Agnes to Lola to Earl. "One text."

Marcus leaned back in his chair. The vinyl squeaked. Carmen was standing by the door, her back against the frame, her hand on her badge — she'd been adjusting it without realizing, the nervous fidget she'd never been able to break.

"Okay," Marcus said. "Okay, I hear you. I messed up with the phone. That's on me. But the rest of it — Harry, come on. You knew this was coming."

"I didn't know anything."

"You knew I wasn't gonna just sit there making twelve hundred a month while some dude's offering me five times that. You knew the math didn't work for me the way it works for you."

"The math," Harry said.

"Yeah, the math. Your math. You send money to Jack, a couple hundred a month, and that's — that's fine for you, that's your grandson's truck payment or whatever. Earl sends Denise her four hundred. Agnes has an envelope under her mattress. You're all — you're taken care of, right? Your rooms are paid for, your food shows up, your heat works most of the time." Marcus gestured at the radiator, which chose that moment to click and go silent. "I'm in a basement apartment in Plainfield. I drive a car with a hundred and sixty thousand miles on it. I don't have a pension, I don't have insurance, I don't have a bed I don't pay rent on."

"You agreed to the terms," Harry said.

"I agreed to terms that made sense when there wasn't a better option. There's a better option now."

Agnes turned from the window. "The better option is distributing opioids to a reseller who will sell them at street price to anyone with cash. No condition screening. No dosage information. No interaction checks."

"That's his problem, not mine."

"It is everyone's problem when someone dies." Agnes's voice was sharp — not loud, Agnes was never loud, but the consonants had edges. "When a nineteen-year-old takes six oxycodone because he does not know what he bought and his mother finds him in the morning. That is my problem. That is pharmacology, Marcus. That is what I was trained for forty-two years to prevent."

Marcus looked at the ceiling. "Agnes, I respect you, I do, but you're not my pharmacist. You're — we're not a pharmacy. We're selling stolen pills out of a nursing home. That's what this is."

"They are not stolen," Agnes said. "They are prescribed to us and diverted by our consent."

"Okay, fine, diverted. Whatever word makes you feel better about it."

Lola's crucifix caught the light as she shifted in her chair. She'd been silent the whole time, her hands pressed together in her lap, the tremor visible even from across the room. "Mijo," she said quietly.

Marcus looked at her. The hardness in his face cracked — not all the way, not enough to change what he was going to do, but enough that Carmen saw it. The fissure.

"Lola, I'm sorry I didn't tell you," he said. "I should've. But you would've told Harry and Harry would've said no and we'd be right here anyway."

"You don't know that," Lola said. "You didn't give us the chance."

"Because I already knew the answer."

Harry put both hands flat on the card table. His fingers were spread, the tendons standing out, an old man's hands — knobbed, liver-spotted, steady. "You're out," he said. "Effective now. You don't take product from this building, you don't visit as Lola's nephew, you don't come through that loading dock again."

Marcus stared at him. Then he laughed — short, hard, no humor in it. "And then what? Who's your distribution? Who's gonna carry the pills out the front door, Harry? Agnes? She's gonna shuffle down to the parking lot in her performance slippers and hand a bag of oxy to Rosa? Earl's gonna wheel the van to Paterson and make drops?"

"We'll figure it out."

"No you won't. Because you can't. Because the whole thing depends on somebody who can walk out of here, and that's me. That's been me for eighteen months. I'm the one in the car. I'm the one meeting people in parking lots with Schedule II narcotics in a backpack. If the cops pull me over, I go to prison. You know what happens to you? You get the newspaper story. You get the — " He waved his hand. "The cute story. Elderly residents outsmart the system. Poor old people, they didn't know what they were doing. You'd get sympathy. A human interest segment. I'd get a cell."

"That's not —" Carmen started.

"It's exactly what would happen and you know it." Marcus turned to her. "You know it, Carmen. You've worked this system. You know what a forty-four-year-old Black guy with a prior distribution charge looks like next to five white-haired grandparents in wheelchairs. Tell me I'm wrong."

Carmen didn't say anything. She couldn't, because he wasn't entirely wrong. The racial calculus of the criminal justice system wasn't something she could argue around, and Marcus knew it.

"I got fired from this place," Marcus said, and now his voice had changed — the self-repair had kicked in, the sentences starting and restarting as he tried to find the honest version. "I got fired for — I filed a complaint. About staffing. About the fact that there were twelve residents and one aide on the overnight and somebody was gonna fall and get hurt, and I was right, by the way, because Burt fell and got hurt, which is exactly what I said would happen. And they fired me. They called it insubordination and they fired me. I cared about these people and I got punished for it. So I found another way to — I came back and I found a way to still be useful, to still be part of something, and now you're sitting here telling me I can't look out for myself? That I'm supposed to make twelve hundred a month and be grateful because at least I get to be the one who takes all the risk?"

The room held the silence after that. The vegetable chopper infomercial had ended. The hallway was quiet. Carmen could hear the building's bones settling — the slow click and groan of a structure that had been standing for forty years and showed it in every wall crack and stained ceiling tile.

Lola was holding her crucifix in her right hand, the gold chain tight around her fingers. Earl hadn't moved from his corner. His hands were still folded over his knees, his expression unreadable.

"Marcus," Lola said. "Mijo, please."

"Lola, I love you. I do. But I can't do this for love. Love doesn't pay my rent."

Harry said nothing. He was in ledger mode now — the numbers calculated, the column totaled, the result written in ink. Carmen had seen this before, the night she'd confronted him in September. Harry processed in silence, and when the silence ended, the decision was already made.

"We built this," Harry said. "Agnes and Earl and Lola and me. We built it because nobody else was going to take care of us. It was never about the money."

"It was always about the money," Marcus said. "For me it was. You had the luxury of it being about something else."

Earl coughed. The deep, wet rattle that he covered with his fist, the one that had gotten worse every week — a sound like something was loose in his chest and shifting position. He cleared his throat. The room waited, because when Earl cleared his throat, he was about to say something, and when Earl said something, it landed.

"You're not one of us anymore," Earl said.

Five words. He didn't raise his voice, didn't change his posture, didn't look away from the spot on the far wall he'd been studying since the meeting started. But the words had weight — the specific weight of a man who'd spent thirty years organizing shop floors and knew exactly what it meant to say someone was out. Not a punishment. A fact. A door closing.

Marcus flinched. It was small — a tightening around his eyes, a shift in his shoulders — but Carmen saw it. Earl saw it too, and Earl's expression didn't change, because Earl's expression never changed when he'd said the thing that ended the discussion.

Marcus sat there for a moment. His hands were flat on his thighs, mirroring Harry's hands on the table. He looked at Lola, who was crying silently, the tears running down her face into the creases around her mouth, her tremor so bad now that the crucifix was shaking in her grip. He looked at Agnes, who was watching him the way she watched everything — with assessment, not judgment, though the distinction felt thin tonight. He looked at Harry.

"Yeah," Marcus said. "Okay." He pushed back from the table and stood up. The chair screeched on the linoleum. "I'll stay away from the building. I won't use the phone again. You want me to — I don't know. You want me to return Lola's cookie tin?"

Nobody answered.

Marcus stood there for another few seconds, and Carmen could see it on him — not the anger, not the defiance, but the thing underneath both of those, which was just a man realizing that the people he loved most had told him to leave. His jaw worked. He shoved his hands in the hoodie pocket.

"Tell Burt I said hey," he said. Then he walked out.

Carmen listened to his footsteps down the hall — the CNA's quiet walk, instinctive, even now. The loading dock door opened and closed. A minute later, an engine started in the parking lot, and headlights swept across the window and were gone.

Lola made a small sound, something between a word and a breath. Agnes walked to the TV and turned it off — the screen had been dark anyway, the infomercial block over, but she pressed the power button with the precision of someone who needed to turn off one thing she could control.

Earl stood up, coughed into his fist, and left without speaking. His bathrobe belt trailed behind him on the floor.

Lola wiped her face with the back of her hand. "Ay, Dios mio," she whispered, and followed him out, her slippers catching on the threshold.

---

Harry didn't move from the card table. Carmen sat down in the chair Marcus had left, the vinyl still warm. The room was quiet now — that specific late-night Lakeview quiet where you could hear the ventilation system cycling and the soft tick of the wall clock above the TV and, if you listened for it, the faint moaning from Room 110 that never fully stopped.

The card table still had the phone on it. Carmen picked it up and set it on the windowsill, face down.

Harry was looking at his hands. His fingers were laced together now, resting on the table, and for the first time since she'd known him — the real him, not the performance — he looked his age. Not diminished, not confused. Just old. An eighty-four-year-old man in a cardigan buttoned wrong, sitting in a nursing home TV room at two in the morning because the one thing he'd built that still mattered was falling apart.

"You knew," Carmen said.

"That he'd take the deal? Yeah. When he agreed too fast in October, I knew."

"So why didn't you —"

"Do what? Follow him? We can't leave the building, Carmen. That's the whole problem. That's always been the whole problem."

He unfolded his hands and laid them flat on the table again. His right index finger tapped once — just once, the accountant's tic, like he was entering a figure on an adding machine.

"I started this because they were cutting our medications," he said.

Carmen waited. Harry didn't tell stories. Harry stated facts and let you draw your own conclusions. But something in the room had shifted — Marcus's chair was still pulled out, the space he'd occupied still registering as absence — and Harry was talking.

"Three years ago. Cliff decided to switch pharmacy providers. The new contract saved the facility eleven thousand a year. He put it in his quarterly report — cost savings, operational efficiency. He got a bonus for it." Harry's voice was even, metronomic, the voice he used for numbers. "The new pharmacy was in South Brunswick. They had a centralized fulfillment system — big operation, served thirty-some facilities. They were cheaper because they were bigger, and they were bigger because they cut corners."

He stopped. Looked at the photograph of his grandson's truck, which wasn't here — it was in his room, taped above his closet — but he looked at the spot where it would have been if it were.

"Agnes's hydrocodone got switched to a generic she'd never been prescribed. Different manufacturer, different fillers, different absorption rate. She told the nurse it wasn't right. The nurse told the charge nurse, the charge nurse told Cliff, Cliff told the pharmacy, and the pharmacy said the generic was therapeutically equivalent and that was the end of it." He paused. "Agnes was in pain for two weeks. She's a pharmacist. She knew exactly what was happening to her body and why, and she couldn't get anyone to listen."

"Harry," Carmen said.

"My oxycodone got delayed. Just — delayed. The new pharmacy had fulfillment issues, backlogs, they said it would arrive in forty-eight hours. It took nine days. I was eating Tylenol for a hip that needed oxycodone, and I'm not a complainer, Carmen, but nine days with a bad hip at eighty-one years old is — you don't sleep. You don't move right. You fall." The metronomic voice wavered, just slightly, like a number that didn't round clean. "Earl's blood pressure medication got substituted twice in one month. Lola's Parkinson's prescription arrived three weeks late and when it came it was the wrong dosage. Burt's pain management — Burt was on a stable regimen that had taken two years to calibrate, and they changed his oxycodone manufacturer without telling anyone and he went through a week of breakthrough pain that he couldn't describe because he couldn't always find the words."

Harry's hands were still on the table. Perfectly still. The accountant under maximum pressure, reciting facts without interpretation, which was worse than any emotion he could have shown.

"So we started keeping our pills. Not selling them — keeping them. Agnes set up a system. She inventoried what we had, checked interactions, tracked expiration dates. We were doing what the facility was supposed to do and wasn't. We were managing our own medications because the people paid to manage them had decided it was cheaper not to."

"And the selling?" Carmen asked.

"That came six months later. Marcus was already fired by then. He came to visit Lola, and Lola mentioned that Agnes had noticed they were being over-prescribed — the new pharmacy was auto-filling scripts that should have been reviewed. We had surplus. Marcus knew people who needed it. The math was simple."

He stopped again. The wall clock ticked. The ventilation system cycled off, and the silence got thicker, the kind of silence that pressed against the windows.

Carmen thought about the parking lot outside — dark at five o'clock now, the overhead lights buzzing the sickly orange they always buzzed, the leaves from the courtyard trees scattered across the asphalt in wet brown clumps. She thought about Marcus driving away from Lakeview for the last time, or maybe not the last time, his headlights disappearing down Route 27 toward Plainfield and his basement apartment and whatever deal he'd made with whoever was on the other end of that text.

"He's going to get caught," she said. "Moving that kind of volume."

Harry didn't respond to that. He was looking at his hands again.

"What do we do now?" Carmen asked.

Harry lifted his head. His eyes were clear — not sharp the way they usually were, not calculating. Just clear, the way a window is clear when there's nothing behind it to obstruct the view.

"We wait," he said. "Marcus will either come back or he won't. And if he doesn't, we figure out what we are without him."

"And if he gets caught?"

"Then we've got a different problem."

Carmen sat with that. The vinyl chair was cold through her scrubs — the room's heat had cycled off with the ventilation, and the late October air was seeping through the single-pane window behind her, the kind of cold that was patient, that didn't push but just waited until you stopped being warm.

Harry put his hands on the arms of his wheelchair. For a moment Carmen thought he was going to stand — to push himself up the way he did when the performance was over and the real Harry took his body back. But he didn't. He sat in the chair, and she couldn't tell if it was exhaustion or choice or the kind of decline that didn't announce itself but just arrived, settling into the joints and the muscles and the places where the body had been pretending it was younger than it was.

"Go do your rounds," Harry said. "Devon's gonna wake up eventually."

Carmen stood. She looked at the card table where Marcus had been sitting, the chair still pulled out at an angle. She pushed it back under the table. The scrape of the legs on the linoleum was loud in the empty room.

"Harry."

"Yeah."

"I'm sorry."

He looked at her. Then he looked at the dark TV screen, which reflected the room back at them — two people in a nursing home common area at two-thirty in the morning, one in scrubs and one in a wheelchair, the overhead fluorescents washing both of them the same flat institutional white.

"Go do your rounds, Carmen," he said.

She went. The hallway was quiet, the half-light casting long shadows from the handrails. At the nurse's station, Devon was still asleep, the hunting podcast finished, his phone screen dark. Carmen sat down at the charting desk and picked up her pen and looked at the patient log and didn't write anything for a long time.

Outside, the parking lot was empty where Marcus's car had been. The wind moved the last leaves across the asphalt and they made a dry scraping sound that she could hear through the window, or thought she could — the sound of things that had held on as long as they were able, letting go.


# Chapter 17

Carmen was in the medication room doing the evening count when she heard it. Not the words at first — just the cadence. Every nurse learns the difference between the TV room's daytime murmur and the local news anchor's emergency tone, the slight tightening in the broadcaster's delivery that means something happened nearby, something with a suspect, something with a mugshot.

She set down the clipboard and walked to the TV room.

It was mostly empty at 8:40 PM — dinner was over, the last visitors had signed out at eight, and most of the Wing A residents were back in their rooms. Burt was dozing in the recliner nearest the screen, his mouth open, one hand curled around the armrest like he was holding onto something in a dream. The volume was up too loud because nobody had adjusted it after Mrs. Petroski had gone back to her room, and Mrs. Petroski was functionally deaf.

The anchor was describing an arrest in Edison. Prescription drug distribution. A man named DeShaun Briley, thirty-seven, picked up during a traffic stop with approximately four hundred tablets of oxycodone, eighty alprazolam, and an undetermined quantity of hydrocodone in the trunk of his vehicle. Possession with intent to distribute, state and federal charges. Bond set at seventy-five thousand.

Carmen didn't recognize the name. She didn't recognize the face in the mugshot — a heavy young man, scared-looking under the flat police lighting, his left eye slightly swollen like he'd been rubbing it or hadn't slept. But she recognized the volume. Four hundred tablets of oxycodone wasn't a person's stash. It wasn't even a mid-level dealer's weekly turnover. That was the kind of quantity that required a supply chain, and the kind of quantity Marcus's new buyer had been purchasing.

Her hand went to her badge and stayed there. The news anchor moved on to a car accident on Route 18.

She stood in the doorway for another thirty seconds, watching the screen without seeing it, while her mind ran the chain. Briley to Marcus. Marcus to Lakeview. Lakeview to the count discrepancies documented under her name, her license number, her signature on every shift-end log for the past seven months. If Briley had Marcus's phone number in his contacts — if he had a text, a single text, with Marcus's name or a location or a time — investigators would pull Marcus's records. And Marcus had visited this building every week for over a year, signing in at the front desk as Lola's nephew, his name in a visitor log that no one cross-referenced because no one at Lakeview cross-referenced anything.

Carmen turned off the TV room light on her way out. Burt didn't stir.

She went to Harry's room. He was sitting up in bed reading the Trenton Times, his faded blue cardigan buttoned wrong — top button through the second hole — and his reading glasses taped at the left temple where the arm had broken six weeks ago. He looked up when Carmen knocked on the open door, and his face told her he already knew.

"You saw it," she said.

"I saw it." He folded the paper and placed it in his lap. "Edison. That's twenty minutes from here."

"It's the volume buyer. The quantities match."

Harry took off his glasses. Without them, his eyes were sharper — the lenses were for reading, but they also softened his face, made it rounder, made him look more like the befuddled old man he played during daylight hours. "I've been trying Marcus's phone for an hour. His personal one. Goes straight to voicemail."

"Since when do you have Marcus's personal number?"

Harry looked at her the way he looked at her whenever she said something he considered obvious. He didn't answer.

"When's the last time he came in?" Carmen asked.

"Ten days ago. He hasn't been here since the meeting." That was what they called it — the night Marcus had been confronted, had argued back, had been cut off by Earl in a single sentence that ended more than the conversation. "Sit down, Carmen."

She didn't sit down. She adjusted her badge, felt the clip digging into the fabric of her scrub top. "If they trace Briley to Marcus — any connection, a phone record, a text, anything — they follow Marcus here. They pull the visitor log and they see his name every week for fourteen months. They look at the medication records and they see my count discrepancies, documented and signed. Every one of them mine."

"Sit down."

"I can't sit down right now."

Harry put his glasses on the nightstand and lined them up parallel to the edge, the way he aligned everything — pens, papers, envelopes of cash. The accountant's reflex for order, even now. "We clean house," he said.

"Everything?"

"Everything. Pills, records, the phone. Tonight."

Carmen leaned against the doorframe. The hallway behind her smelled the way it always smelled at night — disinfectant and the warm-skin scent of people who didn't go outside, the fading trace of dinner's meatloaf, hand sanitizer from the dispensers every fifteen feet. Familiar smells. She'd worked in this hallway for thirteen months and the smell had become invisible to her, the way her own apartment's smell was invisible, and now it registered again, sharp and specific, the way things register when you know you might be leaving them under circumstances you didn't choose.

"I'll tell Agnes," she said.

"Tell her about the notebooks."

"I know."

She found Agnes at 9:15. Agnes was still awake — she rarely slept before midnight, a habit from four decades of pharmacy shifts that ended at 10 PM. Her roommate, Dolores Gutierrez, was asleep and snoring wetly in the bed nearest the door, the sound of a woman whose body worked hard just to breathe. Agnes was sitting upright in the far bed with a crossword puzzle book open on her lap. She hadn't filled in any squares.

"I heard it," Agnes said before Carmen could speak. "The volume on the TV was up. Mrs. Petroski's hearing."

"Harry wants everything gone tonight."

Agnes closed the crossword book. Her hands were steady — the left-side weakness from her stroke showed in her gait when she walked, a slight drag in the left foot, but her grip had recovered fully within the first year — and she placed the book on the nightstand with the same deliberate precision she placed everything. Pharmacist's hands. Everything in its location. "My notebooks."

"Yeah."

"There are four. Composition books. Black-and-white marbled covers." Agnes's voice was clinical, but the words came faster than usual — closer together, compressed, the way her speech tightened under pressure. "Under my mattress. Under the fitted sheet, between the mattress and the mattress pad. Not under the bed frame."

Carmen looked at her. Agnes's face was what it always was — precise, impatient, stripped clean. But her hands had moved from the crossword book to the mattress edge and were pressing down, her fingertips whitening against the hospital-grade fitted sheet.

"I'll come get them after Devon falls asleep," Carmen said. "Eleven-thirty."

"I'll have them out."

---

Devon was asleep by 11:45. Carmen checked twice — at 11:30, when he was still scrolling his phone with the audio off, and again fifteen minutes later when the phone was face-down on his chest and his breathing had gone slow and even. He'd picked up the overnight because the regular night aide had called out, and Devon worked overnights the way he worked every shift — minimally, resentfully, one earbud in, his attention dimmed to whatever frequency let him avoid being fired for a third time. He was twenty-three. He'd keep sleeping.

Carmen went to Agnes's room first.

Agnes was standing by her bed in a housecoat that had belonged to someone else — the facility distributed donated clothing to residents whose families didn't bring enough, and Agnes's daughter Helen sent the same three outfits in rotation, all a size too large because Helen still pictured the woman she'd been during the stroke recovery, fifteen pounds lighter and unable to say the word "prescription" without it coming out wrong. The housecoat was a faded floral print with a zipper up the front. Agnes held the notebooks out in both hands, the way you hand off something fragile.

Four composition books. Black-and-white marbled covers. The kind sold at every CVS and Walgreens in New Jersey for two dollars and forty-nine cents, the kind Carmen had bought for Reggie's school supplies in September.

She took them. They were heavier than they looked — filled cover to cover, no blank pages. The top one fell open in her arms and she saw Agnes's handwriting: small, square, pharmacist's print that could have been typed. Columns of drug names with dosages. Buyer codes — not names, just two-letter abbreviations. Expiration dates noted in red ink. Margin notes: *Do not combine w/ warfarin. Check renal function. Ro — skip if on new antibiotic, interaction risk. Jn — monitor for signs of tolerance escalation (none observed).* One page had a hand-drawn table of pill counts going back months, each row dated, each column a different controlled substance, every number precise to the individual tablet.

Eighteen months of pharmaceutical expertise applied to an illegal operation with the same rigor Agnes had applied to forty-two years behind a pharmacy counter in Edison. These notebooks were evidence of a federal crime. They were also the most careful, most specific, most conscientious pharmaceutical records in the building — better than what Carmen charted, better than what Dr. Prasad's prescriptions produced, better than anything the state survey had reviewed.

"I'll burn them behind the loading dock," Carmen said.

Agnes looked at the notebooks in Carmen's arms. She was a small woman — five-two, gray hair cut short because she could manage it without help, wire-rimmed glasses reflecting the hallway's fluorescent light. She didn't say anything.

"Agnes."

"Go," Agnes said. Her voice was flat and fast.

Carmen went.

She started with the pills. Harry had the remaining stockpile in two shoeboxes in the back of his closet, behind a row of Reader's Digest condensed books that had come with the room. She retrieved them while Harry sat in his wheelchair by the window, watching the dark parking lot.

"Thirty-six oxycodone," he said without turning around. "Fourteen alprazolam. Twenty-one tramadol. Some non-controlled — the metformin, the lisinopril, a few rosuvastatin. Agnes has the exact count in the second notebook."

"It doesn't matter anymore, Harry."

"No." He turned the wheelchair around. His hands were on the wheels, and the motion was smooth but slow — not the efficient push-and-turn she'd seen in the midnight meetings, when he moved the chair like it was an extension of his body. Tonight the wheelchair looked like what it was supposed to look like: a device for someone who needed it. Carmen watched him and couldn't tell if the slowness was exhaustion or aging or the performance reasserting itself like muscle memory, and for the first time since she'd known the real Harry, she wasn't sure which one she was seeing.

"I suppose it doesn't," he said.

Carmen carried the shoeboxes to the handicapped bathroom at the end of Wing A — single-occupancy, lockable from the inside, a wider toilet with grab bars and a pull cord she'd respond to if someone fell, which no one would tonight because Carmen was the only person in Wing A who was awake and doing what she was supposed to be doing, and she was not doing what she was supposed to be doing. She locked the door.

The first box: oxycodone. She took the ziplock bag — labeled in Agnes's handwriting, *Oxycodone 5mg, 18 ct, exp. 04/27* — and poured six tablets into the toilet. They were small, round, white. They looked like anything. They looked like the mints her mother kept in a dish by the front door.

She flushed. Waited for the tank to refill. The plumbing in Lakeview was from the original 1978 construction — slow, loud, the pipes knocking in the walls like someone rapping their knuckles. She poured six more tablets. Flushed. Waited. The last six. Flushed.

The alprazolam went next. Then the tramadol. Then the non-controlled medications — the metformin and lisinopril and rosuvastatin that Agnes had identified as valuable to uninsured buyers, medications that cost two hundred or three hundred dollars a month at a pharmacy and that the ring had been selling for forty or fifty, medications that Rosa needed for her lupus and Derek needed for his diabetes and that were now swirling through the pipes beneath the building on their way to the New Jersey sewer system.

The process took forty-five minutes. She flushed in groups of five or six, waiting between each flush for the tank to refill, listening for sounds in the hallway — Devon's footsteps, a call bell, any indication that the building had noticed what she was doing. The bathroom was small and poorly ventilated, and the November night was cold outside but the building's heating system ran at a single temperature year-round, which was always too warm, and Carmen was sweating through her scrub top by the time the last tablet disappeared.

When she unlocked the door and stepped into the hallway, Earl was standing outside the medication room.

He was leaning against the wall, breathing through his mouth. His nighttime clothes — sweatpants, a flannel shirt with a fraying collar — hung on a frame that had been losing weight for months, though Carmen only registered it now, standing in the half-lit hallway with an empty shoebox in each hand. His big hands were flat against the wall behind him, the posture of a man who needed the wall more than he wanted to admit.

"Earl, you should be in bed."

"Harry said help."

"I don't need—" She stopped. She looked at him. He was 81 years old and his cough had been getting worse since at least September — she could hear it through the walls during her shifts sometimes, a deep wet rattle that sounded like it was pulling something loose — and he was standing in the hallway at half past midnight offering to carry things. "Okay. Take these back to Harry's room."

She handed him the empty shoeboxes. He took them and turned down the hall, and three steps in the cough hit — a real cough, not for show, the kind that bent him forward at the waist and made his shoulders shake. He stopped and leaned against the wall and coughed into a handkerchief he pulled from his pocket, and the sound of it filled the hallway, wet and thick and rattling. When it passed, he folded the handkerchief and put it back without looking at it. Carmen had been a nurse long enough to know what that meant — the not-looking. You don't check the handkerchief when you already know what you might find and you've decided not to find out.

"Earl."

"I'm fine." Said once. Flat. Finished. He kept walking.

Lola was at the junction of Wing A and the main hallway, sitting in one of the vinyl chairs near the window. She was supposed to be lookout — watching for Devon to stir, for movement from the nurse's station — and she was doing it with the same quiet alertness she'd brought to fourteen months of ring meetings, her eyes tracking the hallway in both directions. But her hands were shaking. She'd taken a coffee mug from the dining room — white ceramic, Lakeview Gardens printed in blue letters that had mostly worn off from industrial washing — and she was holding it with both hands, not drinking from it, just holding it. The coffee inside trembled, small concentric rings moving from the center to the edges and back. Her Parkinson's tremor was always there, but tonight it was worse, and the mug didn't help. The mug was supposed to give her hands something to press against, something to steady, but the tremor came through anyway, and the coffee shook and Lola's jaw was set tight with the effort of pretending she didn't notice.

"Mija," she said when Carmen passed. "How much longer?"

"Almost done, Lola. Stay here."

Lola nodded. She brought the mug closer to her chest, holding it against the front of her nightgown, and her gold crucifix clinked against the ceramic.

Carmen got the notebooks from where she'd left them on the supply closet shelf and went out the back. The loading dock was a concrete platform at the rear of the building, four feet off the ground, with a rusting metal ramp for deliveries and a dumpster that the trash service came for on Tuesdays and Fridays. Not visible from the front entrance or the parking lot. Carmen had checked. She'd also checked the security cameras — Lakeview had three, all pointed at the entrances, one of which had been broken since before she started and had never been repaired because Cliff Moorehead's maintenance budget was a joke that nobody at corporate was laughing at.

The November air hit her when she opened the back door. Forty degrees, maybe less — the kind of cold New Jersey does in early November, not winter yet but the memory of summer completely gone. The loading dock was lit by a single bulb above the door, yellow and weak, throwing a circle of light maybe ten feet across. Beyond that, the back of the property was dark — a strip of dead grass and then a chain-link fence and then the access road that ran behind the strip mall next door. The dumpster smelled like old food and the industrial disinfectant wipes that Lakeview went through in cases of forty-eight. No stars — just the brownish suburban sky, lit from below by the highway.

She found a metal trash can near the ramp — the maintenance staff used it for cigarette disposal and small burns — and dragged it to the center of the dock. She opened the first notebook.

Agnes's handwriting. Page after page. Every drug name spelled out in full, never abbreviated — oxycodone, not oxy; alprazolam, not Xanax; hydroxychloroquine, not HCQ — because Agnes had been a pharmacist for forty-two years and pharmacists didn't abbreviate. Carmen tore out the pages in bunches of ten or twelve, crumpled them, and dropped them into the trash can. She'd brought a box of kitchen matches from the break room — the kind left next to the coffeemaker for birthday candles, a small red box with the striker strip half worn through. She struck a match. The sulfur flared in the cold air and she dropped it onto the paper.

The first notebook burned slowly. Composition-book paper is heavy — that semi-glossy stock that resists flame, curls rather than catches, and needs feeding. Carmen tore more pages and pushed them in. The smoke was white and thin and smelled like a classroom. The ink made the fire flicker blue at the edges.

She was midway through the second notebook when Agnes appeared at the back door.

Carmen hadn't heard it open. Agnes was standing at the edge of the loading dock in her donated housecoat and slippers, her arms at her sides, the cold November air moving through the thin fabric. The firelight caught her glasses and made her eyes invisible behind two small rectangles of reflected orange.

Carmen stopped tearing pages. "You should be inside. It's cold."

Agnes didn't move. She watched the fire. The pages curled, blackened, fell apart — handwriting she'd put down over fourteen months, catalogues of medications and dosage calculations and interaction flags, the whole pharmaceutical architecture of the operation documented with the same rigor she'd applied to every prescription she'd filled for four decades. She watched it the way Carmen had once watched a nurse she'd trained fail a competency — professional grief, specific and unsentimental, the recognition that something well-made was being destroyed because the situation required it.

"I started those notebooks the first week Harry asked me to join," Agnes said. Her voice was faster than usual — the words closer together, compressed, more data per sentence. Her hands were at her sides and they were not sorting, not organizing, not performing the physical categorization that was her version of breathing through it. They were just hanging there. "Fourteen months of inventory. Every tablet accounted for, every interaction flagged, every expiration date tracked to the day. I maintained buyer profiles. Rosa's lupus medication schedule. Derek's insulin timing and when the metformin needed to coordinate. Janine's tolerance levels — I was monitoring for signs of escalating use, which I did not observe." She paused. "Nobody asked me to keep those records. I kept them because that is what a pharmacist does."

Carmen tore another fistful of pages and fed them into the trash can. The fire was hotter now, the draft pulling the flame up, the metal warming under her hand when she steadied the can's rim.

"This is the only professional work I have done in three years," Agnes said.

Carmen looked at her. Agnes was standing very still in the cold, her housecoat moving slightly in the wind off the access road, her slippers on the concrete of the loading dock. Her face was what it always was. She was not going to cry. Agnes Chen did not cry. She stated facts, and the facts were: she had been a pharmacist, and then she had been institutionalized by a daughter who mistook a stroke for senility, and then she had spent three years in a room with a woman who couldn't remember her own name, and the only thing in those three years that had used the expertise she'd spent a career building was an illegal drug operation run out of a nursing home TV room, and now the records of that operation were burning in a trash can on a loading dock in central New Jersey in November.

Carmen went back to burning. She finished all four notebooks by twenty past one. The ash was gray and powdery and collapsed when she stirred it with a stick from near the ramp. Agnes watched the whole time. She stood in the cold for the full forty minutes and did not move and did not speak again. When the last page was ash, she turned and went back inside. She didn't say goodnight.

Earl appeared in the hallway as Carmen came through the back door. He was carrying Lola's cookie tin — the Danish butter cookie tin that had been the handoff container, pills in ziplock bags sealed inside it every week for Marcus to pick up. It was empty now, just a blue tin with a picture of a windmill and some Danish words Carmen had never bothered to read.

"Lola said to get rid of it," Earl said. He was leaning against the wall again, his hand braced on the doorframe of the linen closet. His breathing was wrong — shallow, with a sound underneath it, a low wheeze that Carmen could hear from five feet away. The flannel shirt hung off his shoulders. He'd made two trips tonight, from his room to Harry's and from Lola's room to here, and the two trips had cost him something visible.

"I'll take it." She held out her hand and he gave her the tin. It was light, the cheap kind that dented if you pressed your thumb into it. She carried it out to the loading dock and dropped it in the dumpster, where it clanged against the metal floor and settled next to a garbage bag that had been sitting there long enough to leak something brown.

The prepaid phone was last. She got it from Harry's nightstand — a cheap Samsung flip phone, the kind you could buy at a 7-Eleven for thirty dollars with a prepaid SIM card. Seventeen text messages on it, all from Marcus or to Marcus, the most recent from six weeks ago, the oldest from ten months back. She deleted the messages one at a time, pressing the button with her thumb, watching each one vanish from the screen. Then she turned the phone off and carried it to the loading dock.

She put it on the concrete and stepped on it. The screen cracked but the casing held. She shifted her weight and ground her heel down. The hinge broke with a snap that sounded too loud for the size of the thing. She pried the SIM card out with her thumbnail — it was small and slippery and her fingers were cold — and bent it until it snapped. She dropped the phone casing in one corner of the dumpster and the SIM fragments in another, under the leaking bag.

When she came back inside, her hands smelled like smoke and something chemical — the SIM card's plastic, probably, or the ink from Agnes's notebooks. She washed them at the sink in the medication room with the orange antibacterial soap, the same soap she used twenty times a shift, and washed them a second time, and her hands still smelled like burning.

---

By four in the morning, it was done.

Carmen sat in the TV room. The overhead light was off. The television was off. The room was lit only by the hallway fluorescents coming through the open door, a blue-white half-light that made the vinyl chairs look colorless and the walls look like they were receding. She could hear the building's heating system — a low drone that had been the background noise of her working life for thirteen months — and Burt's snoring carrying down the hall from 116A, and, if she listened, Earl's cough. One cough, then silence, then two more, muffled by the wall and the closed door but audible in the 4 AM stillness of a building where nobody was supposed to be awake.

Harry wheeled himself in at 4:15. He'd changed into his blue cardigan — the good one, the one he wore to every ring meeting, buttoned correctly this time — and Carmen realized only now that it was a superstition, that he'd been wearing this same cardigan to every meeting for fourteen months and she'd noticed without noticing, and that the meetings were over and the cardigan was just a sweater now. He parked his wheelchair by the card table where Agnes used to spread out her notebooks and the ziplock bags and the envelopes of cash. The table was bare. A coffee ring stained the surface — old, from weeks or months ago — and Harry looked at it.

"Done?" he said.

"It's done. Pills, notebooks, phone. Lola's tin."

Harry nodded. His hands were in his lap. Carmen noticed them because Harry's hands were always doing something — calculating, adjusting, squaring the edges of papers, performing the constant small calibrations of a man who ran numbers the way other people breathed. Now they were still. They rested on his thighs, palms down, and they looked like an old man's hands — spotted, thin-skinned, the knuckles swollen with arthritis that he never mentioned because mentioning it would acknowledge a vulnerability that wasn't part of the act or the plan.

"This is over," Carmen said. "No more pills, no more buyers, no more anything."

Harry didn't argue. He'd argued with Marcus — coldly, precisely, with the weight of every number he'd ever counted — but he didn't argue with Carmen. Not tonight.

"It was good while it lasted," he said.

Carmen shook her head. "It wasn't good. It was necessary."

Harry looked at her. The hallway light caught the side of his face — the thin skin over the cheekbone, the pale eyes, the jaw that used to tighten when he was calculating leverage and now just rested in the architecture of his skull. "Is there a difference?"

"Yeah," she said. "There's a difference."

They sat. The building settled around them — the heating system cycling, the squeak of a mattress from someone turning over in a room down the hall, the small mechanical sounds of a facility built in the late seventies and maintained the minimum amount required to pass state inspection. Through the window, the parking lot was dark and empty. Beyond it, the courtyard's three trees stood bare, their branches sharp against the brown suburban sky, all the leaves gone since late October.

"What are you going to do?" Harry asked.

"Go home. Sleep. Come back tomorrow and do my job."

"That's not what I mean."

She knew what he meant. She didn't answer. She pulled her ponytail tighter — it had come loose hours ago, during the loading dock, and strands hung around her face — and adjusted her badge and looked at the empty card table. The coffee ring. The surface where Agnes's notebooks used to open, the drug names in precise columns, the buyer codes in red ink, all of it ash now in a trash can on the loading dock. In a few days the maintenance staff would empty that can and the ash would go to the dumpster and the dumpster would go to a landfill somewhere in Middlesex County, and nobody would ever know that a seventy-six-year-old retired pharmacist had maintained the most careful pharmaceutical records in the state of New Jersey from a shared room in a mid-tier nursing home while pretending she couldn't speak.

"I'm going to check on everyone," she said, and stood up.

"Carmen."

She stopped at the doorway.

Harry was looking at his hands. "Thank you," he said.

She nodded. She left.

Agnes was in bed, awake, staring at the ceiling with her glasses still on. The ceiling was acoustic tile, water-stained in one corner from a leak that maintenance had patched but not painted. Her roommate's snoring was the only sound. Carmen stood in the doorway.

"You okay?"

"Yes."

It was a lie, and Carmen accepted it the way you accept a lie from someone who has earned the right to tell one. She moved on.

Lola's room. Lola was asleep, curled on her right side, the rosary beads wound through her left hand. Her right hand rested on the pillow near her face, and even in sleep the tremor was there — a fine vibration in her fingers that moved the beads against the cotton pillowcase, a faint clicking sound like something small and persistent ticking down.

Earl and Burt in 116A. Burt was asleep, had been asleep through everything — his decline so steady now that the ring ending meant nothing to a man who could no longer remember what the ring was. He was on his back with his mouth open, his wispy white hair pressed flat against the pillow, his long musician's hands slack on the blanket. The Hungarian-English dictionary that used to hold the pill stash was gone — Carmen had moved it to the supply closet months ago, after his fall, and it was still there, hollowed out and empty, a dead artifact of a dead operation. Earl was in the other bed with his eyes closed, but his breathing was wrong — too controlled, too careful, the breathing of a man who was pretending to sleep so nobody would ask him how he was feeling. Carmen stood in the doorway and listened to his breathing and heard, beneath it, the faint wet rattle that had been there for months and was not getting better.

She went back to the nurse's station and did her charting. Overnight rounds: checked on Mr. Kovacs at 11 PM, 1 AM, 3 AM. Checked on Mr. Washington, Mrs. Fernandez, Mrs. Chen. Medication count documented as correct, which it now was, because the medications that would have been short were dissolving in the sewer system beneath the building. She signed the log with the same signature she signed everything — C. Reyes, LPN — and put the clipboard back on its hook.

The front door beeped at 5:48 AM.

Early. The morning shift didn't start until seven, but Tammy always came in early. She said it was because the bus schedule was better before six, and that was partly true, but Carmen had worked here long enough to know the rest of it: Tammy came early because Tammy was the kind of person who showed up, who did the thing, who put on her white sneakers that she replaced every six months when the soles wore through from eight hours a day on her feet at sixteen-fifty an hour and came in before anyone asked her to. She'd been doing it for fifteen years. It was not dedication. It was not passion. It was the specific, unglamorous competence of a woman who did the job right because she didn't know how to do it wrong and nobody was going to pay her enough to make the distinction matter.

Tammy came around the corner in her coat and her scrub pants and stopped when she saw Carmen.

"You're still here."

"Picked up the overnight." Carmen could hear herself, the automatic ease of the excuse. "Devon needed coverage and I was already—"

She stopped. The explanation was too smooth, too practiced, the kind of plausible professional thing she'd been saying for months now, and standing in the nurse's station at ten to six in the morning with her eyes raw and her scrubs smelling like smoke, the smoothness felt like what it was.

"I stayed," she said.

Tammy looked at her. Not casually — the diagnostic look, the scan of a CNA who'd spent fifteen years reading bodies because bodies told you what charts didn't. Carmen knew what Tammy was seeing: the red-rimmed eyes, the loose hair, the exhaustion that went past tired into something structural. The faint smell of smoke on her clothes that the soap in the medication room hadn't fully covered.

Tammy didn't say anything.

She put her bag in her locker. She clipped on her badge and started her rounds, her footsteps going down Wing A — the familiar rubber-soled rhythm, steady and measured, the footsteps of a woman who could change a bed, dress a wound, calm a frightened resident, and chart it all before the next call bell rang, and who did this every day for money that insulted the work.

Carmen gathered her things. She signed out on the overnight log. She put on her coat — it was the same coat she'd worn last November, a black puffy jacket from Old Navy that she'd bought on sale for twenty-nine dollars and that had a broken zipper she kept meaning to fix — and slung her bag over her shoulder and walked toward the exit.

She passed Harry's room.

The door was mostly closed. Lakeview policy required doors to remain openable from the hall, not shut fully, but Harry had pulled it to within an inch. Through the gap, in the strip of visible room, Carmen saw him sitting in his wheelchair by the nightstand. He had something in his hand. A phone — not the prepaid, which was in pieces in the dumpster behind the building, but a different one. A cell phone she had never seen. Small, black, a flip phone like the prepaid but not the prepaid. He was holding it to his ear.

He was talking. His voice was low and Carmen couldn't hear the words from the hallway — just the rhythm, the cadence of short sentences delivered with the controlled precision of his real register. Not the performance. Not the wandering mumble about Eleanor and what day was it and who were these people. The real Harry, at ten minutes to six in the morning, calling someone on a phone that Carmen didn't know existed, saying things she couldn't hear to a person she couldn't identify.

She stood in the hallway for three seconds. She could have pushed the door open. She could have walked in and asked who he was talking to and why there was another phone and why the man who had just sat in the TV room and agreed that it was over was already making calls before the sun came up.

She didn't.

She adjusted her badge. She walked to the front door and pushed through it into the morning. The parking lot was dark and cold and the wind came across it flat, unbroken — the courtyard's bare trees did nothing, and the building didn't block it from this angle. Carmen walked to her car with her coat unzipped because the zipper was broken and her hands in her pockets because they were cold and still smelled like smoke despite the soap, despite the washing, despite the fact that the notebooks and the pills and the phone were all gone now and there was nothing left to smell like except the memory of having done it.

She got in the car and sat with her hands on the steering wheel and the engine off. The dashboard clock said 5:53. In an hour and seven minutes she needed to be at her mother's house to drop off Reggie's lunch, and in two hours and seven minutes Reggie needed to be at school, and in eleven hours she needed to be back here for her regular shift. The math of her life, the same math, the same numbers that didn't change because she'd spent the night flushing pills down a toilet and burning a pharmacist's notebooks on a loading dock. Forty-seven thousand a year. Six-eighty a month in loan payments. Twelve hundred rent. Her license number on every shift log, every count sheet, every controlled substance record at Lakeview Gardens Rehabilitation and Nursing Center.

She started the car. She drove home.


# Chapter 18

The day shift was louder than she remembered.

Carmen clocked in at seven and the hallway was already full — therapists wheeling equipment carts, dietary pushing breakfast trays, a family member arguing with the front desk about a missing sweater. Fluorescent lights at full power, no half-light mercy. The disinfectant smell was the same but the building felt bigger in daylight, more crowded, the way a bar looks wrong when you see it in the morning.

She hadn't worked a seven-to-three in over four months. The switch was supposed to ease the transition — the New Brunswick interview was next Thursday, and if the hospital wanted her, she'd be working days permanently. Better pay. Benefits that actually covered Reggie's dental. A forty-minute commute instead of twenty-five, but the math still worked.

She'd submitted the application two weeks ago, sitting in her car after dropping Reggie at school, typing on her phone with her thumbs. She'd written the cover letter in twelve minutes. It was the easiest professional document she'd ever produced because she didn't have to lie in it.

The med pass took longer in the morning. More residents awake, more questions, more family members hovering to make sure Mom or Dad got the right pills. Carmen worked her way down Wing A with the cart, popping blister packs, pouring water cups, charting each administration on her tablet. The routine was the same. Her hands knew it. Her body knew how to lean into the doorframe to steer the cart around the corner by Room 108.

Agnes was sitting up in bed. Her roommate, Mrs. Delacroix, was snoring in the next bed with her mouth open, a thread of saliva connecting her lower lip to the pillow. Agnes had her wire-rimmed glasses on and was looking at the wall opposite her bed, where someone had hung a print of a covered bridge in autumn. Agnes had never looked at that print once, as far as Carmen knew, because Agnes didn't look at things that weren't worth looking at. But she was looking at it now.

"Morning, Mrs. Chen."

Agnes turned her head. The movement was slower than it should have been. Not the performance — Carmen had learned the difference. The performance was a specific kind of vacancy, a deliberate evacuation of the face. This was something else. Agnes looked tired in a way that started behind her eyes.

"Your morning medications." Carmen held out the cup. Lisinopril, rosuvastatin, the calcium supplement, a daily aspirin. Medications Agnes could have identified, dosed, and cross-referenced for interactions in her sleep.

Agnes took the cup. She put the pills in her mouth, drank the water, opened her mouth for Carmen to check. The motion was mechanical but not theatrical — no performance left in it.

"Did you eat breakfast?"

Agnes's tray was on her bedside table. The scrambled eggs had been pushed around the plate. The toast was torn into pieces but none of the pieces were missing. The juice cup was empty. Carmen had seen this pattern for two weeks — Agnes drinking liquids but not eating solids, as though sustaining herself had become a task she was completing partially, then abandoning.

"I had the juice," Agnes said.

"Mrs. Chen, you need to eat. Dr. Prasad's doing rounds today, and she's going to ask me about your intake."

Agnes looked at Carmen. For a moment the old sharpness was there — the pharmacist's impatience, the look that said she knew more about what was in those pills than anyone in the building. Then it went out, like a pilot light.

"The eggs are cold," Agnes said.

Carmen marked the tray as less than twenty-five percent consumed and moved on.

Room 112. Lola's roommate was already at physical therapy, her bed neatly made by an aide. Lola was in her wheelchair by the window, which surprised Carmen — Lola was usually in the hallway by now, parked near the nurses' station where she could watch people and talk to anyone who passed. The hallway was Lola's territory, her stage.

But Lola was at the window. The courtyard outside had lost its leaves. The three trees were bare, their branches sharp against a November sky that was the color of old dishwater. The benches were empty. Nobody sat out there once the temperature dropped below fifty.

"Good morning, Lola."

Lola turned from the window. She smiled, and it was a real smile, but it arrived late — like she'd had to find it first. "Mija. You're here early."

"I'm working days now."

"Days." Lola repeated the word as if tasting it. Her left hand was in her lap, the tremor visible even at rest. The small gold crucifix at her throat caught the flat light from the window. "That's good. You look tired, though."

Carmen handed her the cup. Lola took her medications without the old theatrical production — no practiced swallow, no opening her mouth with a wink. She just took them. That bothered Carmen more than it should have. The performance had been annoying when Carmen thought it was real confusion, then impressive when she understood it was strategic, and now its absence felt like watching someone stop bothering to lock a door they used to guard.

"You want me to take you out to the hall? Tammy's on today."

Lola shook her head. "Not today. I'm fine here."
